Condition for Universal Conditional Propositions
Commentator explains that universal conditional judgments must hold on all possible states of the antecedent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a conditional proposition is universal by checking if the relation (implication or opposition) holds across all possible coexistent states of the antecedent.

Core Idea
Universal conditionals require the relation to hold on all possible states that can coexist with the antecedent.

This passage from Yazdi's Hashiya refines the definition of a universal conditional. Without the restriction to logically possible states, no conditional would be truly universal, because impossible states would break the relation. Let's see how the commentator draws this distinction and why it matters for logical reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the antecedent and consequent
In the example 'Whenever the sun rises, day exists', the antecedent is 'the sun rises' and the consequent is 'day exists'. In the disjunctive example 'This number is either even or odd', the two parts are 'even' and 'odd'.
2
List possible states of the antecedent
Consider all states of the antecedent that are logically possible to coexist with it. For sunrise, these include eclipsed, not eclipsed, cloudy, clear, any season, and any concurrent events (e.g., someone speaking). For numbers, states include being written in different bases, being expressed in words, etc.
Only states that do not entail a contradiction are considered.
3
Check the relation in each possible state
For implication (luzum), verify that the consequent necessarily follows in every selected state. For opposition (inad), verify that the two parts cannot both be true (or both false) in every selected state.
If the relation holds in all such states, the conditional may be universal.
4
Exclude impossible states
States like 'the sun rises but day does not exist' (for implication) or 'this number is both even and odd' (for opposition) are impossible because they would lead to contradictions. The commentator argues that these must be excluded; otherwise, no universal conditional would be true.
This exclusion is necessary to avoid self-contradiction.
5
Formulate the universal condition
A conditional is universal if the relation holds on all states that can logically coexist with the antecedent. This is the condition taught by the commentator.
Memorize: 'All possible states = those that do not contradict the relation itself.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الحكم في الشرطية إن كان على جميع تقادير المقدم نحو هذا الشيء أما أن يكون حجراً وأما أن يكون شجراً. قال في شرحه: أي إن كان الحكم باللزوم والعناد في الشرطية على جميع التقادير والأوضاع الممكنة الاجتماع مع المقدم وإن كانت محالة في أنفسها، فالقضية كلية، كقولنا: كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، وكقولنا: هذا العدد إما أن يكون زوجاً أو فرداً. فإن الحكم باللزوم في الأول وبالعناد في الثاني على جميع التقادير والأوضاع الممكنة، بمعنى أن وجود النهار لازم لطلوع الشمس في جميع حالاتها وأزمانها وأوضاعها من كونها منكسفة أو غير منكسفة، محجوبة بغيم أو غير محجوبة، ومن كونها في الحمل أو السرطان أو غيرهما، ومن كونها مع نطق زيد أو ضرب عمرو أو غير ذلك مما لا تعد ولا تحصى، وهكذا عناد الفردية للزوجية. ثم إنما اعتبرنا إمكان الاجتماع لأنه لو أطلق لم يصدق شرطية كلية لا متصلة ولا منفصلة. أما في الأولى فلأن من التقادير ما لا يلزم معه التالي ككون المقدم مع عدم التالي مثلاً، فحينئذ لا يصح الحكم بلزوم التالي على جميع تقادير المقدم واللزم اجتماع النقيضين. وأما في الثانية فلأن من التقادير ما لا يعاند معه التالي المقدم ككون المقدم مع التالي، فحينئذ لا يصح الحكم بالعناد على جميع التقادير واللزم معاندة الشيء لنفسه. وإن شئت فقل: إن المقدم إذا كان مع التالي، يجب أن يكون معانداً لنقيض التالي ضرورة امتناع
Then the judgment in the conditional, if it is upon all the states of the antecedent, like 'This thing is either a stone or a tree.' He said in its commentary: That is, if the judgment of implication and opposition in the conditional is upon all the possible states and situations that can coexist with the antecedent, even if they are impossible in themselves, then the proposition is universal, as in our saying: 'Whenever the sun rises, day exists,' and our saying: 'This number is either even or odd.' For the judgment of implication in the first and opposition in the second is upon all possible states and situations, meaning that the existence of day is necessary for sunrise in all its states, times, and situations, from its being eclipsed or not, veiled by clouds or not, from its being in Aries or Cancer or other, from its being with Zayd's speech or 'Amr's hitting, and so on without count or measure, and likewise the opposition of oddness to evenness. Then we only considered the possibility of coexistence because if it were absolute, no conditional universal proposition would be true, neither conjunctive nor disjunctive. As for the first, because among the states there are those in which the consequent does not follow, like the antecedent being with the absence of the consequent for example, then it is not valid to judge the necessity of the consequent upon all states of the antecedent, and that would necessitate the conjunction of two contradictories. And as for the second, because among the states there are those in which the consequent does not oppose the antecedent, like the antecedent being with the consequent, then it is not valid to judge the opposition upon all states, and that would necessitate a thing opposing itself. And if you will, say: If the antecedent is with the consequent, it must oppose the opposite of the consequent by necessity of the impossibility
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage discusses the condition for a conditional proposition (both conjunctive and disjunctive) to be universal (kulliyya). The commentator explains that the judgment of implication (luzum) in a conjunctive conditional or opposition (ʻinād) in a disjunctive conditional must hold for all possible states (taqādīr) of the antecedent that can coexist with it. The states considered are those possible in themselves (mumkina al-ijtimāʻ maʻa al-muqaddam), not just actual states. The example given is 'Whenever the sun rises, day exists' — this holds regardless of eclipses, clouds, season, or any concurrent events. Similarly, the opposition between even and odd in a number holds in all possible contexts. The commentator then explains why we restrict to possible states: if we considered all states without restriction, including impossible ones like the antecedent being together with the consequent (for a conjunctive) or without its opposite (for a disjunctive), the universal would be false. For a conjunctive, if the antecedent is with the absence of the consequent, the implication fails. For a disjunctive, if the antecedent is with the consequent itself, the opposition fails (as it would imply self-opposition). Therefore, only states where the antecedent can coexist (without contradiction) are considered. This ensures the coherence of universal conditional judgments.

Key Terms
تقادير المقدم
إمكان الاجتماع
شرطية كلية
لزوم
عناد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator restrict universal conditionals to states where the antecedent can coexist with the consequent?
A) To avoid contradictory states where the relation would fail.
B) Because such states are more common.
C) To simplify the definition.
D) Because the author said so without reason.

Key Takeaway
A universal conditional is not universal in an absolute sense; it is restricted to states that are logically possible to coexist with the antecedent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Consider the conditional: 'If a person steals (sariqa), then his hand is cut (qat' al-yad).' Determine if it is universal by listing possible states of the antecedent (e.g., stealing from a public place, stealing from a private home, stealing small amounts) and check if the consequent holds in all possible coexistent states. Note: exclude states that are impossible, such as 'stealing while not being a thief' or 'stealing a nonexistent object'.
?
Task 2: Compare with a conditional that is not universal: 'If it is Friday (yawm al-jumu'a), then prayers are held in the mosque (salat al-jumu'a).' Identify a possible state of the antecedent that does not satisfy the consequent (e.g., traveling, being sick, or a public holiday when the prayer is not held). Explain why the conditional fails to be universal.

Types of Hujjah and the Precedence Problem
Classification of hujjah and critique of giving qiyas precedence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify hujjah into three types based on the direction of reasoning and critically evaluate why giving qiyas overall precedence for certainty is problematic.

Core Idea
Only demonstration (burhan) among qiyas' five arts yields certainty, so prioritising all qiyas is unsound.

The author presents a common classification of proof (hujjah) into qiyas, istiqra', and tamthil, based on whether reasoning goes from universal to particular, particular to universal, or particular to particular under a universal. Some scholars gave qiyas precedence because it is the main route to certainty. But the author objects: qiyas includes five arts, and only one (burhan) yields certainty. So the justification is flawed unless qualified. This lesson unpacks the classification and the critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of hujjah
Find the three terms: qiyas, istiqra', tamthil. Note the criterion for division: direction of reasoning from universal/particular.
Qiyas goes from universal to particular, istiqra' from particular to universal, tamthil from one particular to another under a common universal.
2
Understand the basis for precedence
The reported view: qiyas is primary for attaining certainty (yaqin). Ask: why would one think that? Because syllogistic form seems more rigorous.
3
Locate the objection
The author points to a later passage: qiyas is divided into the five arts (demonstrative, dialectical, rhetorical, poetic, sophistical). Only demonstration yields certainty. So the claim that qiyas as such yields certainty is false.
The objection rests on the internal diversity of qiyas.
4
Evaluate the possible resolution
The author suggests a possible reinterpretation: maybe they meant qiyas yields certainty in some materials (i.e., when used demonstratively). Decide if this salvages the claim.
This shows the commentator's care: not outright rejection but a call for reflection (ta'ammul).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم ان الحجة على ثلاثة اقسام القياس والاستقراء و التمثيل، وذلك، لان الاستدلال اما ان يكون من حال الكلى على الجزئى او بالعكس او من حال الجزئى على الجزئى الاخر بشرط ان يكونا داخلين تحت كلى واحد فالقسم الأول يسمى بالقياس والثاني بالاستقراء و الثالث بالتمثيل وقدم القياس، لكونه العمدة في الايصال لافادة اليقين دون اخويه . هكذا قال جمع من المحققين. و فيه انه سيأتى فى آخر الكتاب ان القياس ينقسم الى الصناعات الخمس والمفيد لليقين منها واحد و البواقي لا تفيده كما سيتلى عليك ، فلا يصح ما ذكر وجهاً للتقديم. اللهم الا أن يكون مرادهم انه يفيده في الجملة و في بعض المواد فتأمل (انشاء الله تعالى خ ل ) . (میرزا محمد علی)
Know that the proof (hujja) is of three types: qiyas, istiqra', and tamthil. That is because reasoning (istidlal) is either from the universal to the particular, or vice versa, or from one particular to another particular on condition that both fall under a single universal. The first type is called qiyas, the second istiqra', and the third tamthil. And qiyas is given precedence because it is the main one in reaching certainty, unlike its two companions. This is what a group of investigators said. But there is a problem: it will come at the end of the book that qiyas is divided into the five arts, and the one that yields certainty is only one of them, while the rest do not yield it, as will be recited to you. So what was mentioned as a justification for precedence is not correct. Unless they meant that it yields certainty in some materials in general, so reflect. (Mulla Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary on a classification of hujjah (proof) into three types. The author begins by stating the classification: qiyas (syllogism), istiqra' (induction), and tamthil (analogy). The basis for this tripartite division is the direction of reasoning: from universal to particular (qiyas), from particular to universal (istiqra'), or from particular to particular under a common universal (tamthil). Then he reports that a group of scholars gave qiyas precedence over the other two because it is the primary means to attain certainty (yaqin). However, the author objects to this justification by pointing out that qiyas itself is subdivided into the five arts (the five logical arts: demonstrative, dialectical, rhetorical, poetic, sophistical), and only one of these (demonstration, burhan) yields certainty, while the others do not. Therefore, the claim that qiyas as a whole is a source of certainty is not accurate as a basis for precedence, unless the scholars meant that it yields certainty in some materials (i.e., when used in demonstrative materials) in a general sense. The author leaves the matter for the reader to reflect upon and references the later part of the book.

Key Terms
الحجة (al-hujja): Proof or argument, here divided into three types: qiyas, istiqra', tamthil. [Note: In earlier texts, hujja may be synonymous with burhan; here it is a broader term.]
الاستقراء (al-istiqra'): Induction, reasoning from particular to universal.
التمثيل (al-tamthil): Analogy, reasoning from particular to particular under a common universal. Often used in fiqh as qiyas, but here distinguished. [Note: In usul al-fiqh, tamthil is often synonymous with qiyas; here it is a separate logical type.]
اليقين (al-yaqin): Certainty, which is only achieved by demonstration (burhan) among the five arts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author object to the claim that qiyas is given precedence because it yields certainty?
A) Because qiyas is divided into five arts and only one yields certainty
B) Because istiqra' and tamthil also yield certainty
C) Because qiyas never yields certainty
D) Because the claim was made by a group of scholars that is unreliable

Key Takeaway
Qiyas is divided into five arts, and only demonstration yields certainty, so any claim that qiyas overall is the path to certainty must be qualified to demonstrative contexts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a real fiqh ruling derived by qiyas (e.g., the illegality of hashish by analogy to wine). Apply the author's critique: would this qiyas be considered demonstrative certainty or probable? Justify your answer using the five arts framework.
?
A scholar claims: 'Qiyas is the best form of reasoning because it leads to certainty.' Using the critique in this passage, identify the error in this claim and explain how the scholar could refine it.

The Definition of Qiyas and Its Conditions
Author defines qiyas and explains each part of the definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four components of the definition of qiyas and explain what each condition excludes.

Core Idea
The syllogism is defined by four conditions: statement, composition from propositions, entailment, and self-sufficiency.

The definition of qiyas is carefully crafted to include only valid syllogistic arguments and exclude other forms of inference. In his Hashiya, al-Yazdi unpacks the classical definition term by term, showing how each word serves to narrow the scope. Understanding this definition is essential for recognizing what counts as a proof in the Islamic logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four parts of the definition
The definition states: 'A statement (qawl) composed (mu'allaf) of propositions (min qadaya) that entails (yalzimuhu) by itself (liddhatihi) another statement.' Break it into four components: qawl, mu'allaf min qadaya, yalzimuhu, and liddhatihi.
2
Distinguish 'statement' from 'composed'
Note that 'statement' is broader than 'composed'; 'composed' implies an appropriate relation (munasaba) between parts. The author explains that mentioning 'composed' after 'statement' is a rhetorical move to narrow the definition, as is customary.
This distinction shows the precision of logical definitions.
3
Analyze what 'composed of propositions' excludes
This phrase excludes incomplete sentences (like single terms) and single propositions, even if they entail their converse, because 'propositions' in their convention means multiple explicit propositions.
A single proposition like 'All A is B' entails its converse by conversion, but that is not a syllogism.
4
Analyze what 'entails' excludes
The condition of entailment excludes induction (istiqra') and analogy (tamthil), because they only yield probability (zann), not necessary entailment.
The text states: 'nothing is entailed from them; rather, probability of something else is obtained.'
5
Analyze what 'by itself' excludes
The condition 'by itself' excludes arguments that require an external premise to generate the conclusion. The example given is the syllogism of equality (qiyas al-musawah): A=B, B=C implies A=C, but only with the external premise 'the equal of the equal is equal.' Without it, no entailment.
Such arguments are not essential conveyers (laysa min aqsam al-muwassil bil-dhat).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: (( القياس قول مؤلّف من قضايا يلزمه لذاته قول آخر. القياس واقسامه باعتبار الهيئة قوله «القياس قول»: ای مرکب (۱) و هو اعم من المؤلّف (۲) اذ قد اعتبر فى المؤلف المناسبة بين أجزائه لانه مأخوذ من الالفة صرح بذلك الشريف المحقق في حاشية الكشاف وحينئذ فذكر المؤلف بعد القول من قبيل ذكر الخاص بعد العام و هو متعارف فى التعريفات و فى اعتبار التأليف بعد التركيب اشارة الى اعتبار الجزء الصوري في الحجة (۳) و القول يشمل المركبات التامة وغيرها كلها (۴) و بقوله : (ه) اعلم ان الحجة على ثلاثة اقسام القياس والاستقراء و التمثيل، وذلك ، لان الاستدلال اما ان يكون من حال الكلى على الجزئى او بالعكس او من حال الجزئى على الجزئى الاخر بشرط ان يكونا داخلين تحت كلى واحد فالقسم الأول يسمى بالقياس والثاني بالاستقراء و الثالث بالتمثيل وقدم القياس، لكونه العمدة في الايصال لافادة اليقين دون اخويه . هكذا قال جمع من المحققين. و فيه انه سيأتى فى آخر الكتاب ان القياس ينقسم الى الصناعات الخمس والمفيد لليقين منها واحد و البواقي لا تفيده كما سيتلى عليك ، فلا يصح ما ذكر وجهاً للتقديم. اللهم الا أن يكون مرادهم انه يفيده في الجملة و في بعض المواد فتأمل (انشاء الله تعالى خ ل ) . (میرزا محمد علی) AV مؤلف من قضاياء» (۵) خرج ما ليس كذلك كالمركبات الغير التامة و القضية الواحدة المستلزمة لعكسها أو عكس نقيضها اما البسيطة فظاهر و اما المركبة فلان المتبادر من اطلاق القضاياء الصريحة (ء) و الجزء الثانى من المركبة ليس كذلك (۷) او لان المتبادر من القضاياء ما يعد في عرفهم قضاياء متعددة وبقوله: «يلزمه» يخرج الاستقراء و التمثيل (۸) اذ لا يلزم منها شيء نعم يحصل منها الظن بشيء آخر وبقوله : . لذاته» خرج ما يلزم منه قول آخر بواسطة مقدمة خارجية (٩) كقياس المساواة ) نحو الف مساو لـ «ب» و ب مساو لـ (ج))) فانه يلزم من ذلك ان الف مساو لـ «ج» «ج» .. لكن لا لذاته بل بواسطة مقدمة خارجية (۱۱) هي ان مساوى المساوى مساو و قياس المساواة مع هذه المقدمة الخارجية يرجع الى قياسين (۱۲) و بدونها ليس من اقسام الموصل بالذات (۱۳) فاعرف ذلك والقول الاخر اللازم من القياس يسمى نتيجة و مطلوباً (۱۴).
Section: The syllogism is a statement composed of propositions that necessarily entails another statement by itself. Syllogism and its divisions according to form. His statement 'The syllogism is a statement' means a composite (1) and it is more general than the composed (2) because in the composed there is consideration of appropriateness between its parts since it is taken from harmony; the noble Sharif stated this in his Hashiya on al-Kashshaf. Therefore mentioning 'composed' after 'statement' is of the type of mentioning the specific after the general, which is customary in definitions. And in considering composition after construction there is an indication of considering the formal part in the proof (3). And 'statement' includes all complete and incomplete compounds (4). And by his statement: (H) Know that proof is of three types: syllogism, induction, and analogy. This is because inference is either from the universal to the particular, or conversely, or from one particular to another particular on condition that both are subsumed under a single universal. The first type is called syllogism, the second induction, and the third analogy. And they gave precedence to syllogism because it is the mainstay in conveying certainty, unlike its two siblings. This was said by a group of investigators. And there is in it that it will come at the end of the book that syllogism divides into the five arts and that which yields certainty is one of them, while the rest do not yield it, as will be recited to you. So what was mentioned as a reason for precedence is not valid. Unless they mean that it yields certainty in general and in some materials, so reflect (God willing). (Mirza Muhammad Ali). AV 'composed of propositions' (5) excludes what is not like that, such as incomplete compounds and a single proposition that entails its converse or the converse of its contradictory. As for the simple, it is obvious; as for the compound, because the apparent meaning of the term 'propositions' is explicit (?) and the second part of the compound is not like that (7), or because the apparent meaning of 'propositions' is what counts in their convention as multiple propositions. And by his statement 'entails' (8) he excludes induction and analogy, for nothing is entailed from them; rather, probability of something else is obtained from them. And by his statement 'by itself' he excludes what entails another statement by means of an external premise (9), like the syllogism of equality: for example, A equals B and B equals C, for it entails that A equals C, but not by itself but by means of an external premise (11), which is that the equal of the equal is equal. The syllogism of equality with this external premise reduces to two syllogisms (12), and without it it is not among the types of essential conveyers (13). So know that, and the other statement entailed from the syllogism is called the conclusion and the sought (14).
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage defines the syllogism (qiyas) and explains its definition term by term. The definition is: 'A statement composed of propositions that necessarily entails another statement by itself.' The commentary opens by analyzing 'statement' (qawl): it is a composite, more general than 'composed' (mu'allaf) because the latter implies a specific appropriateness between parts, echoing the Sharif's Hashiya. Adding 'composed' after 'statement' is a common rhetorical move in definitions to narrow the general term. The phrase 'composed of propositions' excludes incomplete sentences and single propositions, even if they entail their converse, because 'propositions' in their convention implies multiple explicit propositions. Next, 'entails' (yalzimuhu) excludes induction and analogy, which only yield probability. The key condition 'by itself' (liddhatihi) excludes arguments that require an external premise, like the syllogism of equality (e.g., A = B and B = C implies A = C) which needs the external premise that equals of equals are equal; without it, no inference. Such arguments are not essential conveyers. The entailed statement is called the result (natija) or the sought (matlub). The passage also includes a digression on the three types of proof: syllogism, induction, analogy. The author critiques the claim that syllogism alone yields certainty, noting that later the text will show that only one of the five arts gives certainty, so precedence is not absolute unless intended in a general sense.

مؤلّف
لذاته
نتيجة

C) قياس المساواة without the external premise
D) القضية الواحدة (single proposition)

Key Takeaway
The definition of qiyas is a precise tool to distinguish valid syllogisms from other inferences like analogy and induction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Primary Division of Qiyas: Istithna'i vs Iqtirani
Author explains the division of qiyas and sub-divides iqtirani.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between istithna'i and iqtirani syllogisms and correctly classify a given qiyas as istithna'i, hamli iqtirani, or sharti iqtirani.

Core Idea
The division of qiyas hinges on whether the conclusion is explicitly stated in the premises.

In the previous lesson, we defined qiyas as a composite statement. Now we explore its primary division: the author splits qiyas based on whether the conclusion (or its contradictory) appears openly in the premises. This distinction leads to two major families: istithna'i (conditional) and iqtirani (categorical). Within iqtirani, a further split depends on whether the premises are purely categorical or include conditionals. Understanding this division is essential for knowing which syllogistic rules apply.

Guided Path
1
Identify the 'other statement'
Locate the conclusion of the syllogism. The author calls it 'the other statement' (al-qawl al-akhar). Note its two components: matter (maddah) – the subject and predicate terms – and form (hai'a) – the copula (affirmation or negation).
The matter is the terms themselves; the form is the relation between them.
2
Check for explicit mention of the conclusion
Examine the premises. If the conclusion itself or its contradictory is explicitly stated (often introduced by 'but', lakin), the qiyas is istithna'i. If not, it is iqtirani.
Example from text: 'If this is a human, it is an animal; but it is not an animal' – the contradictory 'not an animal' appears.
3
Classify iqtirani as hamli or sharti
If the qiyas is iqtirani, look at the types of premises. If both premises are simple categorical propositions (hamli), it is hamli. If at least one premise is conditional (sharti), it is sharti.
Example of hamli: 'The world is changing; everything changing is originated; so the world is originated.'
4
Apply the classification to a new example
Take a syllogism from an Islamic science (e.g., fiqh or kalam) and determine its type based on the above criteria.
Practice with: 'If this is wine, it is intoxicating; but it is intoxicating, therefore it is wine.' Is this istithna'i? Why?

قوله «فان كان» : اى القول الآخر الذى هو النتيجة. والمراد بمادته طرفاه المحكوم عليه و به (۱۵) و المراد بهيئته الترتيب الواقع بين طرفيه سواء تحقق في ضمن الايجاب او السلب فانه قد يكون المذكور فى الاستثنائى نقيض النتيجة كقولنا ان كان هذا انساناً كان حيوانا لكنه ليس بحيوان ينتج ان هذا ليس بانسان، والمذكور في القياس : هذا انسان و قد يكون المذكور فيه عين النتيجة كقولنا في المثال المذكور لكنه انسان ينتج ان هذا حيوان. قوله «فاستثنائى»: لاشتماله على كلمة الاستثناء (۱۶) اعنى لكن. قوله «والا» ای و ان لم يكن القول الاخر مذكوراً في القياس بمادته و هيئته و ذلك بان يكون مذكوراً بمادته لا بهيئته اذ لا يعقل وجود الهيئة بدون المادة (۱۷) و كذا لا يعقل قياس لا يشتمل على شيء من اجزاء النتيجة المادية و الصورية بمادته و صورته خ ل ومن هنا يعلم (۱۸) انه لو حذف قوله: «بمادته» لكان اولى. قوله «فاقترانی»: لاقتران حدود المطلوب فيه (۱۹) وهى الاصغر و الاكبر والاوسط (۲۰) قوله «حملى» اى القياس الاقترانى ينقسم الى قسمين حملی و شرطى لانه ان كان مركباً من الحمليات الصرفة فحملى نحو العالم متغيّر وكل متغير حادث فالعالم
His statement 'then if it is' means the other statement which is the result. And by its matter (maddah) is meant its two terms: the subject and the predicate (15). And by its form (hai'a) is meant the relation occurring between its two terms, whether realized in affirmation or negation. For it may be that what is mentioned in the istithna'i is the contradictory of the result, as in our saying: 'If this is a human, it is an animal; but it is not an animal' yields that 'this is not a human'. And what is mentioned in the syllogism: 'This is a human' and it may be that what is mentioned in it is exactly the result, as in our saying in the mentioned example: 'but it is a human' yields that 'this is an animal'. His statement 'so istithna'i': because it includes the word of exception (16), meaning 'but' (lakin). His statement 'and if not' means that the other statement is not mentioned in the syllogism by its matter and form; and that is by being mentioned by its matter but not by its form, since it is inconceivable for the form to exist without the matter (17). Similarly, a syllogism that does not include something of the material and formal parts of the result by its matter and form is inconceivable. From here it is known (18) that had he omitted his statement 'by its matter', it would have been better. His statement 'so iqtirani': because of the conjunction of the terms of the sought in it (19), which are the minor, the major, and the middle (20). His statement 'hamli' means that the iqtirani syllogism divides into two types: hamli (categorical) and sharti (conditional). For if it is composed of pure categoricals, it is hamli, like 'The world is changing, and everything changing is originated, so the world is originated'.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage explains the division of qiyas into istithna'i (conditional) and iqtirani (categorical) based on whether the conclusion (result) is explicitly mentioned in the premises. The author first clarifies the terms: 'matter' (maddah) refers to the subject and predicate of the conclusion, and 'form' (hai'a) refers to the relation (affirmation or negation) between them. In an istithna'i syllogism, either the conclusion itself or its contradictory is explicitly stated in the premises, typically introduced by the word 'but' (lakin). An example is given: 'If this is a human, it is an animal; but it is not an animal, therefore it is not a human.' Here the contradictory of the conclusion ('not an animal') is mentioned. Alternatively, the conclusion itself may appear, as in 'but it is a human, therefore it is an animal.' The term 'istithna'i' is derived from the inclusion of the word of exception ('but'). If the conclusion is not mentioned in the premises in both matter and form, the syllogism is iqtirani (categorical). The commentator notes that it is possible for the matter of the conclusion to appear without its form (e.g., the terms but not the same copula), and states that it would be clearer if the author had omitted 'by its matter' from the condition. The iqtirani is called such because its three terms (minor, middle, major) are brought together. Finally, iqtirani divides into hamli (categorical) when both premises are categorical propositions, and sharti (conditional) when at least one premise is conditional. An example of hamli is given: 'The world is changing, and everything changing is originated, so the world is originated.'

اقتراني

Checkpoint
According to the text, what condition makes a qiyas istithna'i?
A) The conclusion is not mentioned in the premises at all.
B) The conclusion is mentioned in the premises either as it is or as its contradictory.
C) The syllogism contains only categorical premises.
D) The syllogism has a middle term.

Key Takeaway
The primary division of qiyas rests on whether the conclusion appears in the premises: if it does (or its contradictory), the syllogism is istithna'i; if not, it is iqtirani, which further splits into hamli (categorical) and sharti (conditional).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following qiyas as istithna'i, hamli iqtirani, or sharti iqtirani, and explain your reasoning: 'If the Quran is created, it is temporally originated; but the Quran is not temporally originated; therefore the Quran is not created.' (Hint: look for the word 'but' and check if the conclusion or its contradictory is mentioned.)
?
Given the qiyas: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; wine is an intoxicant; therefore wine is forbidden.' Is this istithna'i or iqtirani? If iqtirani, is it hamli or sharti? Use the criteria from the passage.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Definition and Division of Qiyas into Exceptive and Conjunctive
Definition and primary division of the syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between exceptive and conjunctive syllogisms based on the presence of the conclusion in matter and form, and identify the definition of qiyas.

Core Idea
A syllogism is a self-sufficient argument that entails a conclusion by itself, split into exceptive (conclusion stated) and conjunctive (conclusion implied).

The author begins by defining the syllogism (qiyas) as a composite of propositions that entails another by itself. He then divides it based on whether the conclusion is explicitly stated in matter and form (exceptive) or not (conjunctive). This fundamental classification structures all subsequent discussion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is 'al-qiyas' (syllogism).
The definition starts with 'mu'allaf min qadaya'.
2
Extract the genus
The genus is 'a composite of propositions' (mu'allaf min qadaya). This places syllogism in the broader category of composite statements.
3
Extract the differentiae
Two differentiae: (1) the composite entails another statement (yalzamu qawlun akhar), and (2) it does so by itself (li-dhatihi), not via an external premise.
The second differentia excludes arguments like the equality syllogism that require an extra premise.
4
Apply the division
Check if the conclusion is mentioned in matter and form within the syllogism. If yes, it is exceptive (istithna'i); if no, it is conjunctive (iqtirani).
Matter means the terms; form means the arrangement.

On the syllogism and its divisions with respect to form. If the other statement (the conclusion) is mentioned in it in its matter and form, then it is exceptive (istithna'i), otherwise it is conjunctive (iqtirani). [The qiyas is] composed of propositions. [This] excludes what is not like that, such as incomplete compounds and a single proposition that entails its converse or the converse of its contradictory. As for the simple [proposition], it is apparent; as for the compound, because what is understood from the unrestricted use of 'propositions' [is explicit propositions], and the second part of the compound is not like that. Or because what is understood from 'propositions' is what is counted in their usage as multiple propositions. By his saying 'it entails' he excludes induction and analogy, for nothing is entailed from them; rather, probability of something else is obtained from them. By his saying 'by itself' he excludes what entails another statement by means of an external premise, like the equality syllogism: A is equal to B and B is equal to C, for it entails that A is equal to C, but not by itself, rather by means of an external premise, which is that the equal of an equal is equal. The equality syllogism with this external premise reverts to two syllogisms, and without it is not among the types that convey knowledge by themselves. So know that. The other statement entailed from the syllogism is called the conclusion (natija) and the sought (matlub). His saying 'for if it is mentioned...' means the other statement which is the conclusion. And by 'its matter' is meant its two terms, the subject and the predicate; and by 'its form' is meant the arrangement occurring between its two terms, whether realized in affirmation or negation, for sometimes what is mentioned in the exceptive syllogism is the contradictory of the conclusion, as in our saying: 'If this is a human, it is an animal; but it is not an animal; therefore it is not a human.' And what is mentioned in the syllogism [may be] the exact conclusion, as in our example: 'but it is a human' yields 'therefore it is an animal.' His saying 'so it is exceptive' because it includes the word of exception, meaning 'but'. His saying 'otherwise' means if the other statement is not mentioned in the syllogism in its matter and form, that is by being mentioned in its matter but not in its form, since it is inconceivable for form to exist without matter. Likewise, it is inconceivable for a syllogism that does not include any of the material or formal parts of the conclusion in its matter and form. Hence it is known that had he omitted his saying 'in its matter' it would have been better. His saying 'so it is conjunctive' because the terms of the sought are conjoined in it, and they are the minor (asghar), the major (akbar), and the middle (awsat). His saying 'categorical' means that the conjunctive syllogism is divided into two types: categorical and conditional. For if it is composed purely of categoricals, it is categorical, like 'The world is changing, and everything changing is temporally originated, so the world is temporally originated.' Otherwise it is conditional, whether composed purely of conditionals, like 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists; and whenever daytime exists, the world is illuminated; so whenever the sun is rising, the world is illuminated,' or composed of categorical and conditional, like 'Whenever this thing is a human, it is an animal; and every animal is a body; so whenever this thing is a human, it is a body.' The author prioritized the discussion of categorical conjunctive because it is simpler than conditional. His saying 'from the categorical' means from the conjunctive categorical. His saying 'minor' because the subject is usually more specific than the predicate and has fewer individuals than it, so the predicate is major and has more individuals. His saying 'and the repeated [term] is middle' because it mediates between the two extremes. His saying 'and what contains the minor' means the premise that contains the minor, and the masculine pronoun is due to the word al-mawsool. His saying 'minor premise' because it contains the minor. His saying 'major premise' because it contains the major. Know that every conjunctive categorical syllogism must contain two premises that share something and differ in another. As for the first, because the relation of the predicate of the sought to the subject is unknown, so a third thing is needed as a means to know that relation. As for the second, due to the necessity of differentiation between subject and predicate. Then they call that shared thing the middle term, for its mediation between the two extremes of the sought. And the other thing by which the first premise is singled out is the minor, and the subject is usually more specific and has fewer individuals than the predicate. And the other thing by which the second premise is singled out is the major, because it is the predicate of the sought, and the predicate is usually more general and has more individuals than the subject. And each of them is called a term (hadd) by analogy with the 'limit' in the mathematicians' ratio, because it is a limit and endpoint of the ratio. And we restricted with 'usually' in both respects because it is possible for them to be equal, as in our saying: 'Human is rational' and 'Some animal is human', for the subject there is the annexed (mudaf) not the object of annexation (mudaf ilayh). Then it is not hidden that 'minor' and 'major' are with respect to the consideration of parts, while 'fewer' and 'more' are with respect to the consideration of individuals. So using the former for the latter is by way of metaphor, due to their sharing in being with respect to quantity. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage defines the syllogism (qiyas) and its division based on form (hay'a). It begins by stating that if the conclusion (al-qawl al-akhar) is explicitly present in both matter and form, the syllogism is called 'exceptive' (istithna'i); otherwise it is 'conjunctive' (iqtirani). The author then defines qiyas as composed of propositions, excluding incomplete compounds or single propositions that entail their converse. Induction (istiqra') and analogy (tamthil) are excluded because they do not entail certainty. The equality syllogism is also excluded unless an external premise is added. The commentary (hashiya) next explains the terms 'matter' (madda) and 'form' (hay'a), noting that in an exceptive syllogism, the mentioned portion may be the conclusion itself or its contradictory. The conjunctive syllogism is then divided into categorical (hamli) and conditional (sharti). Examples are given from natural philosophy and astronomy (e.g., 'the world is changing' and 'when the sun rises, daytime exists') to illustrate the two types. The final part clarifies the technical terms: the subject of the conclusion is the minor (asghar), the predicate is the major (akbar), and the repeated term in the premises is the middle (awsat). The premises are called the minor premise (sughra) and major premise (kubra). The text notes that typically the minor is more specific and has fewer individuals, but equality is possible, exemplified by 'human is rational' and 'some animal is human'. A note by Mirza Muhammad Ali distinguishes between 'minor' in terms of parts and 'fewer' in terms of individuals, stating that the usage is metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
A syllogism is a self-sufficient composite of propositions that entails a conclusion, divided into exceptive (conclusion explicit) and conjunctive (conclusion implicit).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given a syllogism from Islamic theology: 'The world is contingent (mumkin); everything contingent requires a creator; therefore the world has a creator.' Identify whether it is exceptive or conjunctive, and state why.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
The Three Terms: Asghar, Akbar, Awsat
Definition of minor, major, and middle terms and premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the minor, major, and middle terms in any conjunctive categorical syllogism from Islamic texts and label the minor and major premises.

Core Idea
The middle term is the key that connects the subject and predicate of the conclusion.

After defining the syllogism, the author introduces the three terms that appear in every conjunctive categorical syllogism: the minor (asghar), the major (akbar), and the middle (awsat). These correspond to the subject and predicate of the conclusion and the repeated term in the premises. Understanding these terms is essential for constructing and analyzing arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
Find the statement that is the result (natija) of the syllogism.
In a categorical syllogism, the conclusion is a simple subject-predicate statement.
2
Extract subject and predicate
The subject of the conclusion is the minor term (asghar); the predicate is the major term (akbar).
This is usually based on specificity, but not always.
3
Find the repeated term
Look for a term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion; that is the middle term (awsat).
The middle term mediates between the minor and major.
4
Label the premises
The premise containing the minor term is the minor premise (sughra); the one containing the major term is the major premise (kubra).
The names of the premises derive from the terms they contain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في القياس و اقسامه باعتبار الهيئة
فان كان مذكوراً فيه بمادته وهيئته فاستثنائى والا فاقترانی (اماخ ل) حملى

دی
(۰) اعلم ان كل قياس اقترانى عملى لابد ان يشتمل على مقدمتين يشتركان في شيء وينفردان في آخر. اما الاول فلان نسبة محمول المطلوب الى الموضوع مجهولة فلابد من امر ثالث يكون واسطة العلم بتلك النسبة. واما الثانى فلضرورة التغاير بين الموضوع والمحمول.
ثم انهم يسمون ذلك الشيء المشترك الحد الأوسط، لتوسطه بين طرفى المطلوب والشيء الاخر الذي انفردت به المقدمة الاولى اصغر والموضوع يكون فى الاغلب اخص واقل افراداً من المحمول و الاخر الذى انفردت به المقدمة الثانية اكبر لانه محمول المطلوب و المحمول في الاغلب يكون اعم و اكثر افراداً من الموضوع و كل واحد منها حداً تشبيهاً له بالحد الذي هو في نسبة الرياضيين لكونه حداً و طرفاً للنسبة. وانما قيدنا بالاغلبية في المقامين الجواز كونهما متساويين كما في قولنا الانسان ناطق وقولنا: بعض الحيوان انسان فان الموضوع فيه هو المضاف لا المضاف اليه.
ثم لا يخفى ان الاصغر والأكبر انما يكونان فى الشيء بحسب اعتبار الاجزاء والاقل و الاكثر بحسب اعتبار الافراد فاستعمال الأولين فى الاخيرين من باب التجوز لاشتراكهما معهما في كونهما بحسب اعتبار الكمية. (ميرزا محمد علی)
On the syllogism and its divisions with respect to form. If the other statement (the conclusion) is mentioned in it in its matter and form, then it is exceptive (istithna'i), otherwise it is conjunctive (iqtirani). [The qiyas is] composed of propositions. [This] excludes what is not like that, such as incomplete compounds and a single proposition that entails its converse or the converse of its contradictory. As for the simple [proposition], it is apparent; as for the compound, because what is understood from the unrestricted use of 'propositions' [is explicit propositions], and the second part of the compound is not like that. Or because what is understood from 'propositions' is what is counted in their usage as multiple propositions. By his saying 'it entails' he excludes induction and analogy, for nothing is entailed from them; rather, probability of something else is obtained from them. By his saying 'by itself' he excludes what entails another statement by means of an external premise, like the equality syllogism: A is equal to B and B is equal to C, for it entails that A is equal to C, but not by itself, rather by means of an external premise, which is that the equal of an equal is equal. The equality syllogism with this external premise reverts to two syllogisms, and without it is not among the types that convey knowledge by themselves. So know that. The other statement entailed from the syllogism is called the conclusion (natija) and the sought (matlub). His saying 'for if it is mentioned...' means the other statement which is the conclusion. And by 'its matter' is meant its two terms, the subject and the predicate; and by 'its form' is meant the arrangement occurring between its two terms, whether realized in affirmation or negation, for sometimes what is mentioned in the exceptive syllogism is the contradictory of the conclusion, as in our saying: 'If this is a human, it is an animal; but it is not an animal; therefore it is not a human.' And what is mentioned in the syllogism [may be] the exact conclusion, as in our example: 'but it is a human' yields 'therefore it is an animal.' His saying 'so it is exceptive' because it includes the word of exception, meaning 'but'. His saying 'otherwise' means if the other statement is not mentioned in the syllogism in its matter and form, that is by being mentioned in its matter but not in its form, since it is inconceivable for form to exist without matter. Likewise, it is inconceivable for a syllogism that does not include any of the material or formal parts of the conclusion in its matter and form. Hence it is known that had he omitted his saying 'in its matter' it would have been better. His saying 'so it is conjunctive' because the terms of the sought are conjoined in it, and they are the minor (asghar), the major (akbar), and the middle (awsat). His saying 'categorical' means that the conjunctive syllogism is divided into two types: categorical and conditional. For if it is composed purely of categoricals, it is categorical, like 'The world is changing, and everything changing is temporally originated, so the world is temporally originated.' Otherwise it is conditional, whether composed purely of conditionals, like 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists; and whenever daytime exists, the world is illuminated; so whenever the sun is rising, the world is illuminated,' or composed of categorical and conditional, like 'Whenever this thing is a human, it is an animal; and every animal is a body; so whenever this thing is a human, it is a body.' The author prioritized the discussion of categorical conjunctive because it is simpler than conditional. His saying 'from the categorical' means from the conjunctive categorical. His saying 'minor' because the subject is usually more specific than the predicate and has fewer individuals than it, so the predicate is major and has more individuals. His saying 'and the repeated [term] is middle' because it mediates between the two extremes. His saying 'and what contains the minor' means the premise that contains the minor, and the masculine pronoun is due to the word al-mawsool. His saying 'minor premise' because it contains the minor. His saying 'major premise' because it contains the major. Know that every conjunctive categorical syllogism must contain two premises that share something and differ in another. As for the first, because the relation of the predicate of the sought to the subject is unknown, so a third thing is needed as a means to know that relation. As for the second, due to the necessity of differentiation between subject and predicate. Then they call that shared thing the middle term, for its mediation between the two extremes of the sought. And the other thing by which the first premise is singled out is the minor, and the subject is usually more specific and has fewer individuals than the predicate. And the other thing by which the second premise is singled out is the major, because it is the predicate of the sought, and the predicate is usually more general and has more individuals than the subject. And each of them is called a term (hadd) by analogy with the 'limit' in the mathematicians' ratio, because it is a limit and endpoint of the ratio. And we restricted with 'usually' in both respects because it is possible for them to be equal, as in our saying: 'Human is rational' and 'Some animal is human', for the subject there is the annexed (mudaf) not the object of annexation (mudaf ilayh). Then it is not hidden that 'minor' and 'major' are with respect to the consideration of parts, while 'fewer' and 'more' are with respect to the consideration of individuals. So using the former for the latter is by way of metaphor, due to their sharing in being with respect to quantity. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage defines the syllogism (qiyas) and its division based on form (hay'a). It begins by stating that if the conclusion (al-qawl al-akhar) is explicitly present in both matter and form, the syllogism is called 'exceptive' (istithna'i); otherwise it is 'conjunctive' (iqtirani). The author then defines qiyas as composed of propositions, excluding incomplete compounds or single propositions that entail their converse. Induction (istiqra') and analogy (tamthil) are excluded because they do not entail certainty. The equality syllogism is also excluded unless an external premise is added. The commentary (hashiya) next explains the terms 'matter' (madda) and 'form' (hay'a), noting that in an exceptive syllogism, the mentioned portion may be the conclusion itself or its contradictory. The conjunctive syllogism is then divided into categorical (hamli) and conditional (sharti). Examples are given from natural philosophy and astronomy (e.g., 'the world is changing' and 'when the sun rises, daytime exists') to illustrate the two types. The final part clarifies the technical terms: the subject of the conclusion is the minor (asghar), the predicate is the major (akbar), and the repeated term in the premises is the middle (awsat). The premises are called the minor premise (sughra) and major premise (kubra). The text notes that typically the minor is more specific and has fewer individuals, but equality is possible, exemplified by 'human is rational' and 'some animal is human'. A note by Mirza Muhammad Ali distinguishes between 'minor' in terms of parts and 'fewer' in terms of individuals, stating that the usage is metaphorical.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a conjunctive categorical syllogism, the premise that contains the major term is called:
A) الصغرى (sughra)
B) الكبرى (kubra)
C) النتيجة (natija)
D) الاستثنائي (istithna'i)

Key Takeaway
Every conjunctive categorical syllogism has three terms: minor (subject of conclusion), major (predicate of conclusion), and middle (repeated in premises). The premises are named after their contained term.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the syllogism 'The world is changing; everything changing is temporally originated; therefore the world is temporally originated', identify the minor, major, and middle terms, and label the minor and major premises.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Categorical and Conditional Conjunctive Syllogisms
Division of conjunctive syllogism into categorical and conditional

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between categorical and conditional conjunctive syllogisms based on the types of propositions used.

Core Idea
Conjunctive syllogisms are divided into categorical (all premises are subject-predicate) and conditional (premises are conditional or mixed).

After introducing the basic division of syllogisms, the author further divides the conjunctive type into categorical (hamli) and conditional (sharti). A categorical syllogism uses only categorical propositions, while a conditional uses at least one conditional. Examples from natural philosophy and astronomy illustrate the types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the propositions
Examine each premise: are they all simple subject-predicate (categorical) or are any of them if-then (conditional)?
Conditional propositions typically include 'in' or 'idha'.
2
Check for conditionals
If any premise is a conditional, the syllogism is conditional (sharti); if all are categorical, it is categorical (hamli).
Mixed syllogisms (one categorical, one conditional) are classified as conditional.
3
Analyze the composition
If pure categorical, it's hamli; if pure conditional or mixed, it's sharti.
The categorical is simpler and studied first.

(۰) اعلم ان كل قياس اقترانى عملى لابد ان يشتمل على مقدمتين يشتركان في شيء وينفردان في آخر. اما الاول فلان نسبة محمول المطلوب الى الموضوع مجهولة فلابد من امر ثالث يكون واسطة العلم بتلك النسبة. واما الثانى فلضرورة التغاير بين الموضوع والمحمول.
ثم انهم يسمون ذلك الشيء المشترك الحد الأوسط، لتوسطه بين طرفى المطلوب والشيء الاخر الذي انفردت به المقدمة الاولى اصغر والموضوع يكون فى الاغلب اخص واقل افراداً من المحمول و الاخر الذى انفردت به المقدمة الثانية اكبر لانه محمول المطلوب و المحمول في الاغلب يكون اعم و اكثر افراداً من الموضوع و كل واحد منها حداً تشبيهاً له بالحد الذي هو في نسبة الرياضيين لكونه حداً و طرفاً للنسبة. وانما قيدنا بالاغلبية في المقامين الجواز كونهما متساويين كما في قولنا الانسان ناطق وقولنا: بعض الحيوان انسان فان الموضوع فيه هو المضاف لا المضاف اليه.
ثم لا يخفى ان الاصغر والأكبر انما يكونان فى الشيء بحسب اعتبار الاجزاء والاقل و الاكثر بحسب اعتبار الافراد فاستعمال الأولين فى الاخيرين من باب التجوز لاشتراكهما معهما في كونهما بحسب اعتبار الكمية. (ميرزا محمد علی)
On the syllogism and its divisions with respect to form. If the other statement (the conclusion) is mentioned in it in its matter and form, then it is exceptive (istithna'i), otherwise it is conjunctive (iqtirani). [The qiyas is] composed of propositions. [This] excludes what is not like that, such as incomplete compounds and a single proposition that entails its converse or the converse of its contradictory. As for the simple [proposition], it is apparent; as for the compound, because what is understood from the unrestricted use of 'propositions' [is explicit propositions], and the second part of the compound is not like that. Or because what is understood from 'propositions' is what is counted in their usage as multiple propositions. By his saying 'it entails' he excludes induction and analogy, for nothing is entailed from them; rather, probability of something else is obtained from them. By his saying 'by itself' he excludes what entails another statement by means of an external premise, like the equality syllogism: A is equal to B and B is equal to C, for it entails that A is equal to C, but not by itself, rather by means of an external premise, which is that the equal of an equal is equal. The equality syllogism with this external premise reverts to two syllogisms, and without it is not among the types that convey knowledge by themselves. So know that. The other statement entailed from the syllogism is called the conclusion (natija) and the sought (matlub). His saying 'for if it is mentioned...' means the other statement which is the conclusion. And by 'its matter' is meant its two terms, the subject and the predicate; and by 'its form' is meant the arrangement occurring between its two terms, whether realized in affirmation or negation, for sometimes what is mentioned in the exceptive syllogism is the contradictory of the conclusion, as in our saying: 'If this is a human, it is an animal; but it is not an animal; therefore it is not a human.' And what is mentioned in the syllogism [may be] the exact conclusion, as in our example: 'but it is a human' yields 'therefore it is an animal.' His saying 'so it is exceptive' because it includes the word of exception, meaning 'but'. His saying 'otherwise' means if the other statement is not mentioned in the syllogism in its matter and form, that is by being mentioned in its matter but not in its form, since it is inconceivable for form to exist without matter. Likewise, it is inconceivable for a syllogism that does not include any of the material or formal parts of the conclusion in its matter and form. Hence it is known that had he omitted his saying 'in its matter' it would have been better. His saying 'so it is conjunctive' because the terms of the sought are conjoined in it, and they are the minor (asghar), the major (akbar), and the middle (awsat). His saying 'categorical' means that the conjunctive syllogism is divided into two types: categorical and conditional. For if it is composed purely of categoricals, it is categorical, like 'The world is changing, and everything changing is temporally originated, so the world is temporally originated.' Otherwise it is conditional, whether composed purely of conditionals, like 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists; and whenever daytime exists, the world is illuminated; so whenever the sun is rising, the world is illuminated,' or composed of categorical and conditional, like 'Whenever this thing is a human, it is an animal; and every animal is a body; so whenever this thing is a human, it is a body.' The author prioritized the discussion of categorical conjunctive because it is simpler than conditional. His saying 'from the categorical' means from the conjunctive categorical. His saying 'minor' because the subject is usually more specific than the predicate and has fewer individuals than it, so the predicate is major and has more individuals. His saying 'and the repeated [term] is middle' because it mediates between the two extremes. His saying 'and what contains the minor' means the premise that contains the minor, and the masculine pronoun is due to the word al-mawsool. His saying 'minor premise' because it contains the minor. His saying 'major premise' because it contains the major. Know that every conjunctive categorical syllogism must contain two premises that share something and differ in another. As for the first, because the relation of the predicate of the sought to the subject is unknown, so a third thing is needed as a means to know that relation. As for the second, due to the necessity of differentiation between subject and predicate. Then they call that shared thing the middle term, for its mediation between the two extremes of the sought. And the other thing by which the first premise is singled out is the minor, and the subject is usually more specific and has fewer individuals than the predicate. And the other thing by which the second premise is singled out is the major, because it is the predicate of the sought, and the predicate is usually more general and has more individuals than the subject. And each of them is called a term (hadd) by analogy with the 'limit' in the mathematicians' ratio, because it is a limit and endpoint of the ratio. And we restricted with 'usually' in both respects because it is possible for them to be equal, as in our saying: 'Human is rational' and 'Some animal is human', for the subject there is the annexed (mudaf) not the object of annexation (mudaf ilayh). Then it is not hidden that 'minor' and 'major' are with respect to the consideration of parts, while 'fewer' and 'more' are with respect to the consideration of individuals. So using the former for the latter is by way of metaphor, due to their sharing in being with respect to quantity. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage defines the syllogism (qiyas) and its division based on form (hay'a). It begins by stating that if the conclusion (al-qawl al-akhar) is explicitly present in both matter and form, the syllogism is called 'exceptive' (istithna'i); otherwise it is 'conjunctive' (iqtirani). The author then defines qiyas as composed of propositions, excluding incomplete compounds or single propositions that entail their converse. Induction (istiqra') and analogy (tamthil) are excluded because they do not entail certainty. The equality syllogism is also excluded unless an external premise is added. The commentary (hashiya) next explains the terms 'matter' (madda) and 'form' (hay'a), noting that in an exceptive syllogism, the mentioned portion may be the conclusion itself or its contradictory. The conjunctive syllogism is then divided into categorical (hamli) and conditional (sharti). Examples are given from natural philosophy and astronomy (e.g., 'the world is changing' and 'when the sun rises, daytime exists') to illustrate the two types. The final part clarifies the technical terms: the subject of the conclusion is the minor (asghar), the predicate is the major (akbar), and the repeated term in the premises is the middle (awsat). The premises are called the minor premise (sughra) and major premise (kubra). The text notes that typically the minor is more specific and has fewer individuals, but equality is possible, exemplified by 'human is rational' and 'some animal is human'. A note by Mirza Muhammad Ali distinguishes between 'minor' in terms of parts and 'fewer' in terms of individuals, stating that the usage is metaphorical.

Key Terms
الحملي
الشرطي
الحملية
الشرطية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which is an example of a pure conditional conjunctive syllogism?
A) 'The world is temporally originated; everything temporally originated has a cause; therefore the world has a cause.'
B) 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists; if daytime exists, then the world is illuminated; therefore if the sun is rising, the world is illuminated.'
C) 'Zayd is human; every human is rational; therefore Zayd is rational.'
D) 'Either the world is eternal or it is created; it is not eternal; therefore it is created.'

Key Takeaway
Conjunctive syllogisms are categorized as categorical (all premises categorical) or conditional (at least one conditional premise).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Identifying the Three Terms of a Categorical Syllogism
Commentary defines the three terms and their premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the minor, major, and middle terms in any categorical syllogism and name the premises accordingly.

Core Idea
The middle term is the bridge that connects the minor and major terms.

Before you can evaluate a categorical syllogism, you must know its building blocks: the three terms. This lesson will show you how the commentator distinguishes the minor, major, and middle terms and explains why they are so called. You will learn to locate these terms in any syllogism and name the premises that contain them.

Guided Path
1
Locate the conclusion
Find the statement that is being proved (the conclusion). Its subject is the minor term and its predicate is the major term.
2
Identify the minor and major terms
The minor term (asghar) is the subject of the conclusion, usually more specific with fewer individuals. The major term (akbar) is the predicate of the conclusion, usually more general.
In 'every intoxicant is forbidden', 'intoxicant' is minor, 'forbidden' is major.
3
Find the middle term
Look for a term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. This is the middle term (awsat); it mediates between the minor and major.
In the standard example, 'intoxicant' appears in both premises but not in the conclusion.
4
Name the premises
The premise containing the minor term is the minor premise (sughra); the one containing the major term is the major premise (kubra).
Remember: the minor premise often has the more specific term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله من الحملى اى من الاقتراني الحمل. قوله «اصغر»: لكون الموضوع فى الغالب اخص من المحمول و اقل افراداً منه فيكون المحمول اكبر و اكثر افراداً . قوله « والمتكرر (اوسط»: لتوسطه بين الطرفين . قوله « وما فيه الاصغر» اى المقدمة التي فيها الاصغر، وتذكير الضمير نظراً الى لفظ الموصول . قوله «الصغرى»: لاشتمالها على الاصغر. قوله «الكبرى»: لاشتمالها على الاكبر . ثم انهم يسمون ذلك الشيء المشترك الحد الأوسط، لتوسطه بين طرفى المطلوب والشيء الاخر الذي انفردت به المقدمة الاولى اصغر والموضوع يكون فى الاغلب اخص واقل افراداً من المحمول و الاخر الذى انفردت به المقدمة الثانية اكبر لانه محمول المطلوب و المحمول في الاغلب يكون اعم و اكثر افراداً من الموضوع و كل واحد منها حداً تشبيهاً له بالحد الذي هو في نسبة الرياضيين لكونه حداً و طرفاً للنسبة.  في القياس واقسامه باعتبار الهيئة اما محمول فى الصغرى ( ) و موضوع فى الكبرى فهو الشكل الاول او محمونهما فالثانى او موضوعهما فالثالث او عكس الأول فالرابع . ويشترط في الاول ايجاب الصغرى وفعليتها . قوله « الشكل الاول» يسمى اولاً، لان انتاجه بدیهی و انتاج البواقي نظرى يرجع اليه فيكون اسبق واقدم في العلم . قوله «فالثانی»: لاشتراكه مع الاول فى اشرف المقدمتين اعنى: الصغرى . قوله «فالثالث»: لاشتراكه مع الاول فى اخس المقدمتين اعنى الكبرى. قوله «فالرابع» لكونه في غاية البعد عن الاول . قوله « وفعليتها»: ليتعدى الحكم من الاوسط الى الاصغر وذلك لان الحكم في الصغرى انما هو على الافراد لا على المفهوم ولهذا اشترطت فعليتها.
His saying: 'from the categorical' i.e. from the categorical conjunctive. His saying: 'minor' because the subject is usually more specific than the predicate and has fewer individuals than it, so the predicate is greater and has more individuals. His saying: 'and the repeated (middle)' because it mediates between the two extremes. His saying: 'and the one in which is the minor' i.e. the premise which contains the minor, and the masculinization of the pronoun is due to consideration of the word 'al-mawsul'. His saying: 'the minor premise' because it contains the minor. His saying: 'the major premise' because it contains the major. Then they call that common thing the middle term, because it mediates between the two ends of the desired [conclusion]. The other thing which the first premise uniquely has is the minor, and the subject is usually more specific and has fewer individuals than the predicate. The other thing which the second premise uniquely has is the major, because it is the predicate of the desired conclusion, and the predicate is usually more general and has more individuals than the subject. And each one of them is called a 'limit' (hadd) by analogy with the limit which is in the ratio of mathematicians, because it is a limit and extremity of the ratio. [PAGE_084] On the syllogism and its divisions according to form: if [the middle term] is a predicate in the minor [premise] and a subject in the major, that is the first figure; or a predicate in both, that is the second; or a subject in both, that is the third; or the opposite of the first, that is the fourth. And it is required in the first [figure] that the minor premise be affirmative and actual. His saying: 'the first figure' is called first because its production is self-evident, and the production of the others is theoretical, returning to it, so it is prior and earlier in knowledge. His saying: 'the second' because it shares with the first the more honorable of the two premises, namely the minor. His saying: 'the third' because it shares with the first the less honorable of the two premises, namely the major. His saying: 'the fourth' because it is at the farthest extreme from the first. His saying: 'and its actuality' so that the judgment extends from the middle to the minor, because the judgment in the minor premise is only about individuals, not about the concept, and for this reason its actuality is required.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage from the Hashiya expands on the standard terms of the categorical syllogism: the minor term (asghar), major term (akbar), and middle term (awsat). It explains that the subject of the conclusion is called the minor term because it is usually more specific and has fewer individuals than the predicate (major term). The middle term is so named because it mediates between the two extremes. The premises are named after the terms they contain: the minor premise (sughra) contains the minor term, and the major premise (kubra) contains the major term. The commentator then notes that these terms are analogized to the 'limits' (hudud) in geometry, as they are endpoints of a ratio. The passage then moves to the classification of syllogisms according to the position of the middle term in the two premises. Four figures are enumerated: (1) middle term is predicate in the minor and subject in the major (first figure); (2) predicate in both (second figure); (3) subject in both (third figure); (4) the opposite of the first, i.e. subject in the minor and predicate in the major (fourth figure). The first figure is considered primary because its validity is self-evident, and the others are reduced to it. It shares the minor premise (more honorable) with the second, and the major premise (less honorable) with the third. The fourth is farthest from the first. Finally, a condition for the first figure is stated: the minor premise must be affirmative and actual (i.e., a judgment about actual individuals, not just the concept). This is because the judgment in the minor premise applies to individuals, and only then can the conclusion be drawn about them.

الأكبر

الكبرى

Key Takeaway
The minor term is the subject of the conclusion, the major term is the predicate, and the middle term appears in both premises but not the conclusion.

The Four Figures and the First Figure Condition
Commentary enumerates four figures based on middle term position and states condition for first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any categorical syllogism into one of the four figures based on the position of the middle term, and verify that the first figure's minor premise is affirmative and actual.

Core Idea
The first figure is the only one whose validity is self-evident; all others reduce to it.

Once you know the three terms, the next step is to see how they are arranged. The commentator explains four possible arrangements (figures) based on where the middle term appears in the premises. He also gives a special condition for the first figure, the most fundamental one. This lesson will teach you to classify syllogisms and ensure the first figure's requirement is met.

Guided Path
1
Write down the two premises
List the minor premise (contains minor term) and the major premise (contains major term). Identify the middle term in each.
2
Determine the position of the middle term
Check whether the middle term is a subject or predicate in each premise. There are four possibilities.
Use the mnemonic: first figure: M-P in minor, S-M in major; second: M-P in both; third: S-M in both; fourth: S-M in minor, M-P in major (opposite of first).
3
Compare to the four definitions
Match the positions to the commentator's list: (1) predicate in minor, subject in major → first; (2) predicate in both → second; (3) subject in both → third; (4) subject in minor, predicate in major → fourth.
The first figure is the most natural: middle is predicate of the minor and subject of the major.
4
Check the first figure condition
If the syllogism is first figure, verify that the minor premise is affirmative (not negative) and actual (not a mere concept).
The text says 'ويشترط في الاول ايجاب الصغرى وفعليتها' – the minor premise must be affirmative and actual. This ensures the judgment links individuals.
