The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
The Four Figures of the Categorical Syllogism
Author classifies the four figures and refines the concept of middle term repetition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the figure of any categorical syllogism based on the position of the middle term and explain the rationale for the ordering of the four figures.

Core Idea
The figure depends solely on where the middle term sits; the first figure is primary because its conclusion is self-evident.

In this lesson, we examine how the four figures of the categorical syllogism are defined and ordered. The author explains the rule for determining the figure and why the first figure holds a special status. He then addresses a subtle objection about the repetition of the middle term in certain figures, refining our understanding of what 'repetition' means. Mastering these distinctions is essential for analyzing syllogisms in Islamic theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term's position
In any conjunctive categorical syllogism, find the term repeated in both premises (the middle term). Note whether it appears as subject or predicate in each premise.
The middle term never appears in the conclusion.
2
Determine the figure
Apply the rule: if the middle term is predicate in the minor and subject in the major, it is the first figure; predicate in both, second; subject in both, third; subject in minor and predicate in major, fourth.
Memorize these four patterns.
3
Understand the naming order
The first figure is called 'first' because its conclusion follows without conversion. The other figures require reduction to the first. The second shares the minor premise with the first, the third shares the major, and the fourth is farthest from the first.
The minor premise is considered more honorable because it provides the subject of the conclusion.
4
Analyze the objection about repetition
Some object that in the first and fourth figures the middle term is not truly repeated because its meaning differs (concept vs. individuals). The author responds that repetition is by utterance, not meaning, so it is valid.
This clarification preserves the definition while avoiding a counterexample.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في القياس واقسامه باعتبار الهيئة اما محمول فى الصغرى و موضوع فى الكبرى فهو الشكل الاول او محمونهما فالثانى او موضوعهما فالثالث او عكس الأول فالرابع . ويشترط في الاول ايجاب الصغرى وفعليتها. قوله « الشكل الاول» يسمى اولاً، لان انتاجه بدیهی (۲۶) و انتاج البواقي نظرى يرجع اليه فيكون اسبق واقدم في العلم . قوله «فالثانی»: لاشتراكه مع الاول فى اشرف المقدمتين اعنى: الصغرى . (۲۷) قوله «فالثالث»: لاشتراكه مع الاول فى اخس المقدمتين (۲۸) اعنى الكبرى. قوله «فالرابع» لكونه في غاية البعد عن الاول . (۲۹) قوله « وفعليتها»: ليتعدى الحكم من الاوسط الى الاصغر وذلك لان الحكم في (ه) قوله : ( والاوسط أما محمول في الصغرى ... اعلم أن انحصار الاشكال في الاربعة حصر عقلى دائر بين النفى والاثبات وذلك ، لان القياس الاقترانى الحملى كما ذكر أنفا لابد ان يتكرر فيه الاوسط فهو اما ان يكون موضوعاً في كلتا المقدمتين او محمولاً فيهما او يكون موضوعاً في الاولى و محمولاً في الثانية او يكون بعكس ذلك فالاول هو الثالث والثانى هو الثانى والثالث هو الأول والرابع هو الرابع فهذه الاشكال الاربعة وقد نظمها الشاعر بالفارسية: اوسط اگر حمل یافت در بر صغری و بار حمل بهر دو دوم وضع بهر دو سوم وضع بکبری گرفت شکل نخستین شمار رابع اشکال را عکس نخستین شمار ولا يخفى ان المراد من تكرر الوسط انما هو بحسب الذكر فلايرد ما قيل من انه غير متكرر في الشكل الاول والرابع، اما فى الاول فلانه يراد به المفهوم فى الصغرى والافراد فى الكبرى و اما في الرابع، فلانه یراد به الافراد في الصغرى والمفهوم فى الكبرى بعكس ذلك فلا يتكرر البتة ضرورة اختلافه باختلاف المعنى المراد. و حاصل الجواب انا لا تعنى من تكرره التكرر بحسب ما يراد به بل التكرر مطلقا سواء كان بحسب المراد ايضاً كما فى الشكل الثانى والثالث او بحسب الذكر فقط كما في الشكل الاول والرابع فافهم . (محمد علی)
Regarding syllogism and its divisions according to form: The middle term is either a predicate in the minor [premise] and a subject in the major — that is the first figure; or it is a predicate in both — that is the second; or a subject in both — that is the third; or the opposite of the first — that is the fourth. And for the first figure, it is stipulated that the minor premise be affirmative and actual. The saying 'the first figure' is called first because its production is self-evident and the production of the remaining figures is theoretical and returns to it, so it is prior and older in knowledge. The saying 'the second' because it shares with the first the more honorable of the two premises, namely the minor. The saying 'the third' because it shares with the first the lowlier of the two premises, namely the major. The saying 'the fourth' because it is at the farthest remove from the first. ... Know that the confinement of the figures to four is a rational confinement revolving between negation and affirmation. For the conjunctive categorical syllogism, as mentioned before, the middle term must be repeated. So it is either a subject in both premises, or a predicate in both, or a subject in the first and predicate in the second, or the opposite. The first is the third, the second is the second, the third is the first, and the fourth is the fourth. These are the four figures. A poet has composed in Persian: ... And it is not hidden that what is meant by the repetition of the middle term is repetition in terms of utterance, not in terms of meaning. So the objection that it is not repeated in the first and fourth figures does not apply... The summary of the answer is that we do not mean by its repetition repetition according to what is meant, but absolute repetition, whether it is according to what is meant as in the second and third figures, or only according to utterance as in the first and fourth. So understand.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is a super-commentary on the standard division of the four figures of the categorical syllogism. The author begins by stating the rule for identifying the figure based on the position of the middle term. Then he explains why the figures are named in this order: the first figure is primary because its conclusion is evident without conversion, while the others require theoretical thought to reduce to the first. The second shares the minor premise (more honorable) with the first, the third shares the major premise, and the fourth is farthest. Then the author addresses an objection: in the first and fourth figures, the middle term is not really repeated because it is taken as a concept in one premise and as individuals in the other. The answer is that repetition is by utterance, not by meaning. This clarifies the definition.

Key Terms
شكل (shakl): A figure of the categorical syllogism determined by the position of the middle term in the two premises. [Note: Stable across the tradition.]
اوسط (awsat): The middle term that is repeated in both premises but not in the conclusion. [Note: Standard.]
تكرر (takarrur): The repetition of the middle term, which must occur in the premises; here specified as repetition in utterance, not meaning. [Note: This distinction between utterance and meaning is particularly emphasized in later commentaries.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because it is the simplest

Key Takeaway
The four figures of the categorical syllogism are defined by the position of the middle term, and the first figure is primary; the repetition of the middle term is understood as repetition of the utterance, not necessarily the meaning.

Conditions for the First Figure: Affirmative and Actual Minor, Universal Major
Author explains the necessary conditions for the first figure: affirmative and actual minor premise, and universal major premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a first-figure syllogism is valid by checking whether the minor is affirmative and actual and the major is universal.

Core Idea
A valid first-figure syllogism requires an actual and affirmative minor and a universal major.

In the first figure, the middle term is the predicate of the minor and the subject of the major. This position imposes strict conditions on the premises: the minor must be affirmative and actual (not merely potential), and the major must be universal. Without these, the syllogism fails to guarantee a necessary conclusion. The commentator elaborates on why these conditions are essential, using a counterexample to show the risk of a particular major.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions for the first figure
Find the specific conditions stated in the passage: the minor premise must be affirmative and actual (fi'li), and the major premise must be universal (kulliyyat al-kubra).
These are three conditions: quality (affirmative), modality (actual), and quantity (universal major).
2
Analyze why the minor must be actual
The author argues that the judgment in the minor must be established actually (bil-fi'l) for the middle term. If the minor only asserted a potential or possible relation, the transfer of judgment from the middle to the minor would not be necessary. Examine the reasoning: 'If it were not judged in the minor that the minor term has the middle term actually established for it, then the transfer would not follow.'
This condition prevents modal fallacies where possibility does not entail actuality.
3
Analyze why the major must be universal
The author explains that universality of the major ensures the minor term is included (indiraj) under the middle term. He gives an example: 'Every human is animal, and some animal is horse' – from this we cannot conclude anything about humans and horses because the major is particular. Understand that if the major were particular, the minor might not be included in the part covered by the major premise.
This condition guarantees that the middle term covers all instances, so the judgment on the middle applies to the minor.
4
Test a sample first-figure syllogism for validity
Apply the conditions to a valid syllogism from kalām: 'Every contingent thing has a cause; the world is contingent; therefore the world has a cause.' Check that the minor 'the world is contingent' is affirmative and actual, and the major 'Every contingent thing has a cause' is universal. Then consider an invalid version: 'Every contingent thing has a cause; some world is contingent; therefore the world has a cause' (invalid because minor is not universal? Actually minor particular would be invalid? No, minor can be particular? Wait, the condition is that minor must be affirmative and actual, not necessarily universal. But the example with particular minor could still be valid? The author does not require universality of minor. So we need to construct an invalid version by violating one condition: e.g., 'Some contingent thing has a cause; the world is contingent; therefore the world has a cause' – major is particular, invalid. Or 'The world is potentially contingent; every contingent thing has a cause; therefore the world has a cause' – minor not actual, invalid.
Be precise: only the conditions from the passage (affirmative/actual minor, universal major) are tested.

(ميرزا محمد علی)
His saying 'and it is stipulated for the first figure that the minor premise be affirmative and actual': Know that for the production of the four figures there are conditions regarding the quantity of the premises, conditions regarding their quality, and conditions regarding their modality. The author mentions them here in the order of the figures in detail, and he also alludes to them summarily in the coming rule for greater insight. [The conditions include] universality of the major premise so that the two affirmative [premises] produce, with the affirmative [major], two affirmative conclusions; and with the universal negative major, two negative conclusions. Affirmation or negation is only according to what is established for the middle term in actuality, based on the doctrine of the Shaykh [Ibn Sina]. If it were not judged in the minor that the minor term has the middle term actually established for it, then the transfer of the judgment from the middle term to the minor term would not follow. ... His saying 'with universality of the major' is to ensure the inclusion of the minor term in the middle term, and then the judgment on the middle term entails the judgment on the minor term. This is because the middle term here is predicated of the minor term, and the predicate may be more general than the subject; so if judgment were made in the major about only some of the middle term, it is possible that the minor term is not included in that part, and then the judgment on that part does not entail the judgment on the minor term, as seen in your saying 'Every human is an animal, and some animal is a horse.' His saying 'so that the two affirmative [premises] produce the universal and particular [conclusions]' — the lam is for purpose, i.e., the effect of these conditions is that the universal affirmative minor and the particular affirmative minor, with the universal affirmative major, produce two affirmative conclusions; in the first case the conclusion is universal affirmative, and in the second particular affirmative. And the two affirmative minors with the universal negative major produce the two negative conclusions, universal and particular, as before. And the examples of all are clear. His saying 'the two affirmative [premises]' means they produce the universal and particular [conclusions]. For the student, this.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the conditions for the first figure. The author notes that there are conditions of quantity, quality, and modality. For the first figure, the minor must be affirmative and actual (i.e., not merely possible), and the major must be universal. The reason is that the judgment about the middle term must actually apply to the minor term; otherwise, the conclusion does not follow. The universality of the major ensures that all individuals of the middle term are covered, so the minor term, which is included under the middle, receives the judgment. Examples illustrate: if the major is particular, the minor might not be included in the part judged. The passage also explains that with these conditions, the first figure produces four moods: two affirmative (universal and particular) and two negative (universal and particular).

Key Terms
فعلي (fi'li): Actual, as opposed to potential; the minor premise must judge that the subject actually possesses the predicate, not merely possibly. [Note: In earlier logic, the condition of actuality is emphasized to avoid modal fallacies.]
كلية الكبرى (kulliyyat al-kubra): Universality of the major premise; necessary for the inclusion of the minor term under the middle term. [Note: Standard condition in all first figure treatments.]

Key Takeaway
The first figure requires an actual and affirmative minor and a universal major to guarantee the transfer of judgment from the middle to the minor term.

The Four Productive Moods of the First Figure
Enumerates the four valid moods of the first figure with Islamic examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct the four productive moods of the first-figure categorical syllogism using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
Only 4 of 16 possible mood combinations are productive in the first figure.

In this passage, the author explains that although there are sixteen possible combinations of the four proposition types in a categorical syllogism, only four moods in the first figure actually produce a valid conclusion. These four moods are governed by two key conditions: the minor premise must be affirmative, and the major premise must be universal. Understanding these moods is essential for constructing valid arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the total possible moods
Note that there are 4 types of quantified propositions: A (universal affirmative), E (universal negative), I (particular affirmative), O (particular negative). Multiplying the 4 possibilities for the minor premise by the 4 for the major gives 16 possible mood combinations in each figure.
2
Recall the conditions for the first figure
The first figure requires that the minor premise be affirmative (A or I) and the major premise be universal (A or E). These conditions eliminate invalid combinations.
These conditions were established earlier in the text.
3
Enumerate the four valid moods
Applying the conditions yields four valid moods: (1) AAA, (2) AII, (3) EAE, (4) EIO. Study each mood's pattern: the first letter indicates the minor premise type, the second the major premise type, and the third the conclusion type.
Mnemonic: Barbara, Celarent, Darii, Ferio.
4
Analyze the Islamic examples
The author provides examples for each mood: (1) Every human is an animal, every animal is a body → every human is a body. (2) Some animal is human, every human is rational → some animal is rational. (3) Every human is an animal, no animal is a stone → no human is a stone. (4) Some human is a stone (the conclusion here seems erroneous; it should be a negative particular like 'some animal is not a stone' based on standard syllogistic logic).
The fourth example in the OCR is likely corrupt; the correct Ferio mood would have a particular negative conclusion.
5
Verify the conclusion validity
Check that the conclusion follows necessarily from the premises. In the first three examples, the conclusions are valid. The fourth example as given is invalid; the correct conclusion should be negative particular, e.g., 'some animal is not a stone' (given premises: some animal is human, no human is a stone).
This step reinforces the importance of precise language in logical reasoning.

اعلم ان الضروب الممكنة الانعقاد في كل واحد من الاشكال الاربعة، ستة عشر وذلك لما ذكر سابقاً من ان القضاياء المعتبرة فى العلوم هى المحصورات الاربع لاغير فاذا اعتبرت في الصغرى و الكبرى يحصل ستة عشر ضرباً حاصلة من ضرب الصغريات الاربع فى الكبريات الاربع و المنتج منها في هذا الشكل باعتبار الشرطين المذكورين أربعة الأول: الموجبة الكلية الصغرى مع الموجبة الكلية الكبرى ينتج موجبة كلية كقولنا : كل انسان حيوان وكل حيوان جسم فكل انسان جسم. الثانى الموجبة الجزئية الصغرى مع الموجبة الكلية الكبرى ينتج موجبة جزئية كقولنا: بعض الحيوان انسان و کل انسان ناطق فبعض الحيوان ناطق. الثالث الموجبة الكلية الصغرى مع السالبة الكلية الكبرى ينتج سالبة كلية كقولنا : كل انسان حيوان ولاشيء من الحيوان بحجر فلا شيء من الانسان بحجر. الرابع الموجبة الجزئية الصغرى مع السالبة الكلية الكبرى ينتج سالبة جزئية كقولنا: بعض الانسان بحجر.
Know that the possible moods that can occur in each of the four figures are sixteen. This is because, as previously mentioned, the propositions considered in the sciences are only the four quantified propositions. So when they are considered in the minor and major, sixteen moods result from multiplying the four minor propositions by the four major propositions. From these, the productive moods in this figure, given the two mentioned conditions, are four: First: universal affirmative minor with universal affirmative major produces universal affirmative, as in our saying: Every human is an animal, and every animal is a body, so every human is a body. Second: particular affirmative minor with universal affirmative major produces particular affirmative, as in: Some animal is human, and every human is rational, so some animal is rational. Third: universal affirmative minor with universal negative major produces universal negative, as in: Every human is an animal, and no animal is a stone, so no human is a stone. Fourth: particular affirmative minor with universal negative major produces particular negative, as in: Some human is a stone.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage enumerates the four productive moods of the first figure. The author first states that in theory each figure has 16 possible mood combinations from the four types of propositions, but only four are productive in the first figure due to the conditions of affirmative and actual minor and universal major. The four moods are: (1) AAA (Barbara): universal affirmative minor and major produce universal affirmative conclusion, exemplified by the standard syllogism 'Every human is an animal, every animal is a body, so every human is a body'. (2) AII (Darii): particular affirmative minor and universal affirmative major produce particular affirmative, e.g., 'Some animal is human, every human is rational, so some animal is rational'. (3) EAE (Celarent): universal affirmative minor and universal negative major produce universal negative, e.g., 'Every human is an animal, no animal is a stone, so no human is a stone'. (4) EIO (Ferio): particular affirmative minor and universal negative major produce particular negative. The example given in the text appears corrupt; the standard example is 'Some animal is human, no human is a stone, so some animal is not a stone'. The author uses the same terms but the conclusion as written is incorrect; it should be a negative particular. This may be an OCR error.

Why the Major Premise Must Be Universal in the First Figure
Condition of universal major premise in first figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the major premise must be universal in the first figure and identify the logical flaw when it is particular.

Core Idea
If the major premise is not universal, the minor term may escape the middle term's scope.

In the first figure of the categorical syllogism, one crucial condition is that the major premise (the one containing the middle term as subject) must be universal. This passage explains why: without universality, the judgment on the middle term might not apply to the minor term. Let's unpack the logic together.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition being explained
The commentator states: 'with the universality of the major' – this is the condition that the major premise must be a universal proposition (either affirmative or negative).
2
Understand the role of the middle term in the first figure
In the first figure, the middle term is the predicate of the minor premise and the subject of the major premise. It links the two premises. The minor term is the subject of the conclusion, so the judgment on the middle must transfer to the minor.
3
Grasp the problem with a particular major
If the major premise is only about some of the middle (i.e., particular), the minor term might not be included in that 'some' part, so the judgment on that part does not transfer to the minor.
The commentator gives an example: 'Every human is an animal, and some animals are horses' – from this we cannot conclude anything about humans and horses because the major (some animals) is particular.
4
See the example and its failure
Work through the example: minor = 'Every human is an animal' (universal affirmative), major = 'Some animals are horses' (particular affirmative). No necessary conclusion follows about humans and horses because the middle term 'animal' is not fully covered in the major.
5
Recognize the logical requirement
Thus, for the first figure to produce a valid syllogism, the major premise must be universal so that the minor term is guaranteed to be included under the whole class of the middle term.
The commentator also notes that with a universal major, valid conclusions arise: from two affirmatives we get an affirmative, and from a negative major we get a negative.

قوله «مع كلية الكبرى»: ليلزم اندراج الاصغر في الاوسط فيلزم من الحكم على الاوسط الحكم على الاصغر وذلك ، لان الاوسط محمول هيهنا على الاصغر ويجوز ان يكون المحمول اعم من الموضوع ، فلو حكم فى الكبرى على بعض الاوسط لاحتمل ان يكون الاصغر غير مندرج فى ذلك البعض فلا يلزم من الحكم على ذلك البعض الحكم على الاصغر كما يشاهد فى قولك كل انسان حيوان وبعض الحيوان فرس. قوله « لينتج الموجبتان الكلية والجزئية واللام فيه للغاية اى اثر هذه الشروط ان ينتج الصغرى الموجبة الكلية والموجبة الجزئية مع الكبرى الموجبة الكلية، الموجبتين ففى الاول يكون النتيجة موجبة كلية وفى الثانى موجبة جزئية و ان ينتج الصغريان الموجبتان مع السالبة الكلية الكبرى السالبتين الكلية والجزئية على ماسبق. قوله «الموجبتين: أى ينتج الكلية والجزئية.
His statement: 'with the universality of the major' means: so that the minor term is included under the middle term, and then the judgment upon the middle necessitates the judgment upon the minor. This is because the middle is here predicated of the minor, and it is possible that the predicate is more general than the subject. So if the judgment in the major were upon some part of the middle, it would be possible that the minor is not included under that part, and so the judgment upon that part would not necessitate the judgment upon the minor, as is seen in your statement: 'Every human is an animal, and some animals are horses.' His statement: 'so that the two affirmative propositions, universal and particular, together with the universal major, produce the two affirmatives' — the lam is for purpose, meaning: the effect of these conditions is that the universal affirmative minor and the particular affirmative minor, together with the universal affirmative major, produce the two affirmatives: in the first the conclusion is universal affirmative, in the second particular affirmative; and that the two affirmative minors together with the universal negative major produce the two negatives, universal and particular, as before. His statement: 'the two affirmatives' means: it produces the universal and the particular.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the condition that the major premise must be universal in the first figure. The commentator explains that this condition is necessary for the minor term to be included under the middle term. Since the middle is predicated of the minor, it is possible for the predicate (the middle) to be more general than the subject (the minor). If the major premise were only about some part of the middle, the minor might not fall under that part, so the judgment on that part would not transfer to the minor. He gives an example: 'Every human is an animal, and some animals are horses' — from this we cannot conclude anything about humans and horses. Then he explains the meaning of the phrase 'so that the two affirmative propositions...' indicating that these conditions allow the production of affirmative conclusions from affirmative majors and negative conclusions from negative majors. Finally, he glosses 'the two affirmatives' as meaning both the universal and particular affirmative conclusions.

Key Terms
كلية الكبرى
الاوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the middle term must be distributed in the major.
B) To ensure the minor term is included under the whole class of the middle term, allowing the judgment to transfer.
C) Because the conclusion must follow the weaker premise.
D) Because the major premise must be negative to produce a valid syllogism.

Key Takeaway
The major premise must be universal in the first figure to guarantee the minor term falls under the scope of the middle term, ensuring the conclusion necessarily follows.

The Syllogism

Sixteen Possible Moods: The Combinatorial Foundation
Enumeration of possible moods and conditions for productivity in the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the total number of possible premise combinations in the first figure and explain why there are sixteen.

Core Idea
The sixteen moods of the first figure arise from multiplying four minor types by four major types.

In the first figure of the categorical syllogism, the minor and major premises each can be one of four quantified propositions: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. Multiplying these gives sixteen possible combinations, known as moods. This lesson explores this combinatorial foundation and prepares you to understand which moods are productive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four proposition types
Recall the four quantified propositions used in Islamic logic: universal affirmative (موجبة كلية), universal negative (سالبة كلية), particular affirmative (موجبة جزئية), particular negative (سالبة جزئية). These are the only ones considered in the sciences.
Write them down as A, E, I, O respectively.
2
List minor and major possibilities
The minor premise can be any of the four types, and the major premise can also be any of the four types. We are combining one minor with one major.
3
Multiply to find total
Multiply the number of minor types (4) by the number of major types (4) to get 16 possible combinations. Each combination is a mood (ضرب).
4 × 4 = 16
4
Understand the conditions for productivity
Not all 16 are valid. The text states two conditions: the minor must be affirmative, and the major must be universal. This eliminates many combos, leaving only 4 productive moods.
We will explore these conditions in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم ان الضروب الممكنة الانعقاد في كل واحد من الاشكال الاربعة، ستة عشر وذلك لما ذكر سابقاً من ان القضاياء المعتبرة فى العلوم هى المحصورات الاربع لاغير فاذا اعتبرت في الصغرى و الكبرى يحصل ستة عشر ضرباً حاصلة من ضرب الصغريات الاربع فى الكبريات الاربع و المنتج منها في هذا الشكل باعتبار الشرطين المذكورين أربعة الأول: الموجبة الكلية الصغرى مع الموجبة الكلية الكبرى ينتج موجبة كلية كقولنا : كل انسان حيوان وكل حيوان جسم فكل انسان جسم. الثانى الموجبة الجزئية الصغرى مع الموجبة الكلية الكبرى ينتج موجبة جزئية كقولنا: بعض الحيوان انسان و کل انسان ناطق فبعض الحيوان ناطق. الثالث الموجبة الكلية الصغرى مع السالبة الكلية الكبرى ينتج سالبة كلية كقولنا : كل انسان حيوان ولاشيء من الحيوان بحجر فلا شيء من الانسان بحجر. الرابع الموجبة الجزئية الصغرى مع السالبة الكلية الكبرى ينتج سالبة جزئية كقولنا: بعض الحيوان انسان ولاشيء من الانسان بصاهل فبعض الحيوان ليس بصاهل. ولهم في بيان ذلك طريقان: طريق الحذف والاسقاط وطريق التحصيل. أما الاول فهو ان يقال ان ايجاب الصغرى يسقط ثمانية اضرب وهى الحاصلة من ضرب الصغريين السالبتين فى الكبريات الاربع، وكلية الكبرى تسقط أربعة اخرى وهي الكبريان الجزئيتان مع الصغريين الموجبتين، هذا ان اعتبرنا الايجاب اولا كما هو المشهور و ان اعتبرنا الكلية أولا كان الساقط بها ثمانية و بالايجاب أربعة ملاحظة الاثنين مع الاثنين تحصل اربعة. و اما الثاني فهو ان يقال : الصغرى الموجبة اما كلية أو جزئية و الكبرى الكلية اما موجبة أو سالبة و على هذا الطريق سائر الاشكال الباقية.
Know that the possible moods that can occur in each of the four figures are sixteen, because as mentioned before, the propositions considered in the sciences are only the four quantified ones. So if they are considered for the minor and major, sixteen moods result from multiplying the four minors by the four majors. The productive ones in this figure, by virtue of the two mentioned conditions, are four: First: universal affirmative minor with universal affirmative major produces universal affirmative, as in our statement: 'Every human is an animal, and every animal is a body, so every human is a body.' Second: particular affirmative minor with universal affirmative major produces particular affirmative, as in our statement: 'Some animals are humans, and every human is rational, so some animals are rational.' Third: universal affirmative minor with universal negative major produces universal negative, as in our statement: 'Every human is an animal, and no animal is a stone, so no human is a stone.' Fourth: particular affirmative minor with universal negative major produces particular negative, as in our statement: 'Some animals are humans, and no human is a neighing thing, so some animals are not neighing things.' They have two methods for explaining this: the method of deletion and removal, and the method of acquisition. As for the first, it is said that the affirmation of the minor eliminates eight moods, which are those resulting from multiplying the two negative minors by the four majors; and the universality of the major eliminates another four, which are the two particular majors with the two affirmative minors. This is if we consider affirmation first, as is well-known; but if we consider universality first, then eight are eliminated by it and four by affirmation, and observing twos with twos yields four. As for the second, it is said: the affirmative minor is either universal or particular, and the universal major is either affirmative or negative, and according to this method are the remaining figures.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration of the possible and productive moods of the first figure of the categorical syllogism. The author begins by stating that in each of the four figures there are sixteen possible moods, because the sciences only consider the four quantified propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative). Multiplying the four types of minor by the four types of major gives sixteen combinations. However, only four are productive given the two conditions: the minor must be affirmative and the major must be universal. The four productive moods are then listed with examples from logic and kalam. The author then presents two methods to derive these four from the sixteen: (1) The method of deletion: first, the condition of affirmative minor removes all moods with negative minors (8 moods), then the condition of universal major removes the two particular majors combined with the remaining four affirmative minors? Actually careful: After applying affirmative minor, we have 8 left (4 affirmative minors x 4 majors). Then applying universal major removes the two particular majors (which are 2) combined with the two affirmative minors? Wait: The text says 'كلية الكبرى تسقط أربعة اخرى وهي الكبريان الجزئيتان مع الصغريين الموجبتين' — that is, the two particular majors with the two affirmative minors. So that eliminates 4, leaving 8-4=4. Alternatively, if we apply universal major first, it removes 8 (the two particular majors with four minors? Actually it says 'الساقط بها ثمانية' – likely because universal major eliminates the two particular majors with all four minor types (2x4=8), then affirmative minor removes the remaining negative minors (but there are 2 negative minors left? Actually after eliminating particular majors, we have universal majors only; then affirmative minor removes the two negative minors? The text is a bit condensed but the result is the same 4.) (2) The method of acquisition: directly list the possibilities: the minor is affirmative (universal or particular) and the major is universal (affirmative or negative), giving 2x2=4. This method applies to other figures as well.

Key Terms
الضروب (al-ḍurūb): The moods or premise combinations of a syllogistic figure, formed by the quantity and quality of the minor and major premises. [Note: Stable across logical texts.]
القضاياء المحصورات (al-qaḍāyā al-maḥṣūrāt): The four quantified propositions: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative.

The Four Productive Moods of the First Figure
Enumeration of possible moods and conditions for productivity in the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four productive moods of the first figure and provide an Islamic example for each.

Core Idea
Only four moods in the first figure yield necessary conclusions: those with an affirmative minor and a universal major.

Among the sixteen possible moods, only four produce necessary conclusions when the conditions are met. These four are the basis of many syllogistic proofs in Islamic disciplines. In this lesson, we examine each mood with examples from the text and connect them to Islamic theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two conditions
The minor must be affirmative, and the major must be universal. These conditions are derived from the general rules of the first figure.
Affirmative minors: universal affirmative (A) or particular affirmative (I). Universal majors: universal affirmative (A) or universal negative (E).
2
List the four productive combinations
The four combos are: (A minor, A major), (I minor, A major), (A minor, E major), (I minor, E major). These are the only ones satisfying both conditions.
These correspond to moods: Barbara, Darii, Celarent, Ferio in Western terms.
3
Determine the conclusion type for each
For AA: universal affirmative (A). IA: particular affirmative (I). AE: universal negative (E). IE: particular negative (O). These conclusions follow necessarily.
Check with examples from the text.
4
Work through the text's examples
The text gives: (1) 'Every human is an animal; every animal is a body → every human is a body' (AAA). (2) 'Some animals are humans; every human is rational → some animals are rational' (IAI). (3) 'Every human is an animal; no animal is a stone → no human is a stone' (AEE). (4) 'Some animals are humans; no human is a neighing thing → some animals are not neighing things' (IEO).
These use natural kinds, but we can replace with Islamic examples.

Two Methods for Deriving Productive Moods
Enumeration of possible moods and conditions for productivity in the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply both the deletion method and the acquisition method to derive the four productive moods of the first figure.

Core Idea
The deletion method eliminates non-productive moods step by step; the acquisition method directly lists the productive ones.

The text presents two complementary methods for determining which of the sixteen moods are productive. The deletion method systematically removes moods that violate conditions, while the acquisition method directly constructs the productive moods by selecting the valid premise types. Understanding both methods deepens your grasp of syllogistic conditions and prepares you for analyzing other figures.

Guided Path
1
Understand the deletion method (الاسقاط)
Start with 16 moods. First, apply the condition that the minor must be affirmative. This eliminates all moods where the minor is negative (E or O). There are 2 negative minors × 4 majors = 8 eliminations. Remaining: 8 moods with affirmative minors.
The text says 'affirmation of the minor eliminates eight moods'.
2
Apply the second condition
Next, the major must be universal. From the remaining 8 moods (affirmative minors with all majors), eliminate moods where the major is particular (I or O). There are 2 particular majors × 2 affirmative minors = 4 eliminations. Remaining: 4 moods.
The text says 'universality of the major eliminates another four'.
3
Understand the alternative order
The text notes that if we consider universality first, it eliminates 8 moods (particular majors with all 4 minors), then affirmation eliminates 4 negative minors from the remaining 8 universal majors, also leaving 4.
This shows the process is commutative.
4
Understand the acquisition method (التحصيل)
Directly: The minor is affirmative, so it can be universal (A) or particular (I). The major is universal, so it can be affirmative (A) or negative (E). Multiply: 2×2=4 moods. List them: (A,A), (I,A), (A,E), (I,E).
This is simpler and applies to other figures as well.

Know that the possible moods that can occur in each of the four figures are sixteen, because as mentioned before, the propositions considered in the sciences are only the four quantified ones. So if they are considered for the minor and major, sixteen moods result from multiplying the four minors by the four majors. The productive ones in this figure, by virtue of the two mentioned conditions, are four: First: universal affirmative minor with universal affirmative major produces universal affirmative, as in our statement: 'Every human is an animal, and every animal is a body, so every human is a body.' Second: particular affirmative minor with universal affirmative major produces particular affirmative, as in our statement: 'Some animals are humans, and every human is rational, so some animals are rational.' Third: universal affirmative minor with universal negative major produces universal negative, as in our statement: 'Every human is an animal, and no animal is a stone, so no human is a stone.' Fourth: particular affirmative minor with universal negative major produces particular negative, as in our statement: 'Some animals are humans, and no human is a neighing thing, so some animals are not neighing things.' They have two methods for explaining this: the method of deletion and removal, and the method of acquisition. As for the first, it is said that the affirmation of the minor eliminates eight moods, which are those resulting from multiplying the two negative minors by the four majors; and the universality of the major eliminates another four, which are the two particular majors with the two affirmative minors. This is if we consider affirmation first, as is well-known; but if we consider universality first, then eight are eliminated by it and four by affirmation, and observing twos with twos yields four. As for the second, it is said: the affirmative minor is either universal or particular, and the universal major is either affirmative or negative, and according to this method are the remaining figures.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration of the possible and productive moods of the first figure of the categorical syllogism. The author begins by stating that in each of the four figures there are sixteen possible moods, because the sciences only consider the four quantified propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative). Multiplying the four types of minor by the four types of major gives sixteen combinations. However, only four are productive given the two conditions: the minor must be affirmative and the major must be universal. The four productive moods are then listed with examples from logic and kalam. The author then presents two methods to derive these four from the sixteen: (1) The method of deletion: first, the condition of affirmative minor removes all moods with negative minors (8 moods), then the condition of universal major removes the two particular majors combined with the remaining four affirmative minors? Then applying universal major removes the two particular majors (which are 2) combined with the two affirmative minors? Wait: The text says 'كلية الكبرى تسقط أربعة اخرى وهي الكبريان الجزئيتان مع الصغريين الموجبتين' — that is, the two particular majors with the two affirmative minors. So that eliminates 4, leaving 8-4=4. Alternatively, if we apply universal major first, it removes 8 (the two particular majors with four minors? Actually after eliminating particular majors, we have universal majors only; then affirmative minor removes the two negative minors? The text is a bit condensed but the result is the same 4.) (2) The method of acquisition: directly list the possibilities: the minor is affirmative (universal or particular) and the major is universal (affirmative or negative), giving 2x2=4. This method applies to other figures as well.

Key Terms
طريق الحذف والاسقاط (ṭarīq al-ḥadhf wal-isqāṭ): The method of finding productive moods by systematically eliminating non-productive combinations based on conditions. [Note: This method is common in later logical works like the Sullam and its commentaries.]
طريق التحصيل (ṭarīq al-taḥṣīl): The method of directly acquiring the productive moods by selecting the valid premise types.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
The Conclusion Follows the Baser Premise
Author states the rule with clarification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which premise in a categorical syllogism is 'baser' and determine the conclusion's quantity and quality accordingly.

Core Idea
The conclusion inherits the inferior: particular over universal, negative over affirmative.

In a syllogism, the conclusion does not simply repeat the stronger premise; it takes its character from the weaker one. This rule, known as 'the conclusion follows the baser premise,' is a key principle for evaluating syllogistic validity. The passage explains that the 'baser' premise is the one more restricted in quantity (particular) or quality (negative), and this determines the form of the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Locate the minor and major premises of the syllogism. Note each premise's quantity (universal or particular) and quality (affirmative or negative).
For example: 'All intoxicants are haram' (universal affirmative) and 'Some nabidh is intoxicant' (particular affirmative).
2
Determine which premise is 'baser'
Compare the premises: a particular premise is baser than a universal; a negative premise is baser than an affirmative. If one premise is particular and the other universal, the particular is baser. If one is negative and the other affirmative, the negative is baser.
In the example, 'Some nabidh is intoxicant' is particular, so it is baser than the universal major.
3
Apply the rule to derive the conclusion
The conclusion takes the quantity and quality of the baser premise. If the baser premise is particular affirmative, the conclusion is particular affirmative. If it is universal negative, the conclusion is universal negative, etc.
Thus the conclusion from the example is 'Some nabidh is haram' (particular affirmative).
4
Check against an alternative interpretation
Some scholars claim the conclusion always follows the major premise because the major is always baser relative to the minor. Evaluate this claim: it holds only when the major is indeed the baser premise, but if the minor is particular and the major universal, the minor is baser.
The rule is consistent: the conclusion follows whichever premise is baser, not always the major.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطريق الانتاج فى الأقيسة ان يلاحظ حال المقدمتين ان ايهما هو اخس فيؤتى بالنتيجة تابعة على اخسهما سواء كانت هي الصغرى او الكبرى و هذا مرادهم من قولهم ان النتيجة تكون تابعة للاخس الارذل ، لانها تكون تابعة للكبرى مطلقا باعتبار كونها اخس بالنسبة الى الصغرى كما ادعاه بعض الفضلاء و اصر على ذلك حين سئل عن قول الشاعر: ان الزمان التابع للانزل النتيجة للاخس الارزل
The method of production in syllogisms is to observe the state of the two premises: which one is baser, and then the conclusion is brought following the baser of them, whether it is the minor or the major. And this is what they mean by their statement: 'The conclusion follows the baser and more vile,' because it follows the major absolutely in view of it being baser relative to the minor, as one of the virtuous (fuḍalā') claimed and insisted upon when asked about the saying of the poet: 'Time follows the lower / The conclusion follows the baser and more vile.'
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage states an important rule in syllogistic: the conclusion takes its character from the 'baser' (akhass) of the two premises, i.e., the one that is more restricted in quantity or quality. The baser premise is usually the major premise, which is often more general? Actually the term 'akhass' here means more particular or lower in rank. The author explains that this rule is summed up in the maxim that the conclusion follows the baser and more vile (al-akhass al-ardhal). Some scholars, however, argued that the conclusion follows the major premise absolutely, since the major is always baser relative to the minor. The final line is a poetic verse attributed to this idea.

The Syllogism

Two Methods for Determining Productive Syllogistic Moods
Author explains two methods to determine which moods of a syllogism are productive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply both the deletion method (طريق الحذف والاسقاط) and the derivation method (طريق التحصيل) to identify which moods of a categorical syllogism are productive.

Core Idea
Two systematic methods exist to enumerate valid syllogistic moods: elimination and construction.

The author presents two approaches used by logicians to determine which combinations of premises yield a valid conclusion. The first method starts with all possible premise pairs and deletes those that violate the rules of quality and quantity. The second method directly constructs valid moods by considering the possible forms of the premises. Understanding both methods equips you with the tools to verify syllogisms in any figure.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the total premise combinations
In any figure, there are 16 possible mood combinations (4 types of major × 4 types of minor). The methods aim to reduce these to the few productive ones.
The passage refers to 'two negative minor premises' and 'four major premises' as part of the deletion process.
2
Apply the deletion method (order: affirmation first)
Start by considering the quality of the minor premise. If the minor is affirmative, it eliminates 8 moods that involve a negative minor (since two negatives yield no conclusion). Then, from the remaining, consider the universality of the major: if the major is universal, it eliminates 4 moods where the major is particular (since two particulars yield no conclusion). This leaves 4 productive moods: those with minor affirmative and major universal.
The alternative order (universality first) gives a different sequence but same result.
3
Apply the derivation method
Directly construct the valid moods: the minor premise must be affirmative (either universal or particular), and the major premise must be universal (either affirmative or negative). This yields exactly four combinations: (minor A or I) × (major A or E), which are the four productive moods of the first figure.
The passage states this method applies to all figures, with appropriate modifications based on figure rules.
4
Verify with syllogistic rules
Confirm that the resulting moods satisfy the general rules of the categorical syllogism: no two negatives, no two particulars, the conclusion follows the weaker premise, etc. The deletion method explicitly implements these rules.
For example, mood AAA (Barbara) is valid because both premises are affirmative and universal, and the conclusion is affirmative and universal.
5
Extend to other figures
The same methods apply to the second, third, and fourth figures, but with different initial constraints. For instance, in the second figure, the major must be universal and one premise must be negative, leading to four productive moods (Camestres, Cesare, Festino, Baroco).
The passage emphasizes that the derivation method is foundational for all figures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهم في بيان ذلك طريقان: طريق الحذف والاسقاط وطريق التحصيل. أما الاول فهو ان يقال ان ايجاب الصغرى يسقط ثمانية اضرب وهى الحاصلة من ضرب الصغريين السالبتين فى الكبريات الاربع، وكلية الكبرى تسقط أربعة اخرى وهي الكبريان الجزئيتان مع الصغريين الموجبتين، هذا ان اعتبرنا الايجاب اولا كما هو المشهور و ان اعتبرنا الكلية أولا كان الساقط بها ثمانية و بالايجاب أربعة ملاحظة الاثنين مع الاثنين تحصل اربعة و لعل فى قوله لينتج الموس رسالية و و اما الثاني فهو ان يقال : الصغرى الموجبة اما كلية أو جزئية و الكبرى الكلية ! موجبة أو سالبة و الطريق، وعلى هذا الطريق سائر الاشكال الباقية.
And they have two ways in explaining that: the way of deletion and elimination, and the way of derivation. As for the first: it is said that the affirmation of the minor premise drops eight moods, which are those resulting from multiplying the two negative minor premises by the four major premises, and the universality of the major drops four others, which are the two particular major premises with the two affirmative minor premises. This is if we consider affirmation first, as is well-known. And if we consider universality first, then what is dropped by it is eight and by affirmation four, observing two with two yields four. And perhaps in his saying 'to produce' there is... And as for the second: it is said that the affirmative minor is either universal or particular, and the universal major is either affirmative or negative, and this is the way, and upon this way the remaining figures [follow].
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage discusses two methods to determine which moods of a syllogism are productive. The author explains that logicians have two approaches: the method of deletion (الحذف والاسقاط) and the method of derivation (التحصيل). The first method works by starting with all possible combinations and deleting those that are not productive. For example, if we first consider the quality (affirmation) of the minor premise, it eliminates eight moods (resulting from combining two negative minors with four majors). Then, considering the quantity (universality) of the major, it eliminates four more (the particular majors with affirmative minors). The alternative order (universality first) changes which are eliminated. The second method is to systematically derive productive moods by noting that the minor is affirmative (either universal or particular) and the major is universal (either affirmative or negative). This foundational method applies to all figures. The author notes that these methods are standard in logic for enumerating valid moods.

Ranking Propositions and Ordering Syllogism Moods
Author ranks propositions by quality and quantity and explains how to order productive moods based on the honor of conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to rank categorical propositions by honor according to quality and quantity, and apply that ranking to determine the order of productive syllogism moods.

Core Idea
Universality is more honorable than affirmation; thus universal affirmative is the noblest proposition.

In this lesson, we explore how logicians rank propositions by combining quality and quantity, and how this hierarchy determines which syllogism moods are presented first. Understanding this ranking is essential for evaluating the relative strength of arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qualities and quantities
Recognize that every categorical proposition has a quality (affirmation or negation) and a quantity (universal or particular). Affirmation is more honorable because it indicates existence, and universality is more honorable because it is more precise and useful.
2
Determine the honor of each combination
Combine quality and quantity to form four types: universal affirmative (most honorable), universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative (most base). When honor and baseness mix, universality takes precedence over affirmation.
3
Apply the ranking to syllogism moods
When ordering productive moods, those with more honorable conclusions come first. If two moods yield conclusions of equal honor, look at how many premises they share with the first figure (the most natural figure).
4
Handle ties with first figure sharing
If both moods share both premises with the first figure, then compare the honor of the premises themselves. If sharing differs, the mood sharing the minor premise takes precedence over the one sharing the major premise.
5
Treat the fourth figure as an exception
For the fourth figure, which is unnatural, order its moods based solely on the premises themselves, not on conclusions, because its conclusions are not considered reliable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم ان لنا كيفيتين ايجاب وسلب وكميتين كلية وجزئية و اشرف الاولين الايجاب لانه وجود والسلب عدم والوجود خير من العدم و اشرف الاخيرين الكلية لانه اضبط و انفع في العلوم بخلاف الجزئية، فاذا اجتمع الاشرفان كالموجبة الكلية او الاخسان كالسالبة الجزئية فالامر بين و اذا اجتمع الاشرف مع الاخس فالرجحان للكلية لا للايجاب لان شرفها من جهات متعددة وشرفه من جهة واحدة فاشرف المحصورات الموجبة الكلية ثم السالبة الكلية ثم الموجبة الجزئية ثم السالبة الجزئية وترتب ضروب الاشكال باعتبار النتائج فيقدم المنتج للاشرف على غيره واذا تساوت النتايج شرقاً وخسة فيعتبر تقديم ما هو مشترك بالشكل الاول في المقدمتين او في احديها واذا اتفقا في الاشتراك فذلك اما ان يكون في كلتيهما او في احديها فعلى الاول يلاحظ حال المقدمتين من حيث انفسهما شرفاً و خسة و على الثاني فان كان اشتراكهما في الصغرى معاً او فى الكبرى معاً فكذلك ايضاً و الابان يكون اشتراك احدهما في الصغرى والاخر فى الكبرى فيقدم ما هو مشترك فى الصغرى على الآخر، هذا فيما عدا الشكل الرابع واما فيه فالترتيب بين ضروبها انما هو باعتبار نفس المقدمتين دون النتائج لانه لبعده عن الطبع لم يعتد بنتايجها قط فاحفظ ذلك.
Then know that we have two qualities: affirmation and negation, and two quantities: universal and particular. The more honorable of the first two is affirmation because it is existence, and negation is non-existence, and existence is better than non-existence. The more honorable of the latter two is universality because it is more precise and more useful in the sciences, unlike particularity. So if the two honorable meet, like the universal affirmative, or the two base meet, like the particular negative, the matter is clear. And if the honorable meets the base, then preference is given to universality, not to affirmation, because its honor is from multiple aspects and its honor is from one aspect. So the most honorable of the categorical propositions is the universal affirmative, then the universal negative, then the particular affirmative, then the particular negative. And the arrangement of the moods of the figures is based on the conclusions: the product of the more honorable is given precedence over others. And if the conclusions are equal in honor and baseness, then consideration is given to what is shared with the first figure in both premises or in one of them. And if they agree in sharing, that may be in both of them or in one of them. In the first case, the two premises are considered in themselves with respect to honor and baseness. In the second case, if their sharing is in the minor together or in the major together, then likewise. And if the sharing of one is in the minor and the other in the major, then what is shared in the minor is given precedence over the other. This is apart from the fourth figure: in it, the ordering of its moods is based on the premises themselves, not the conclusions, because due to its unnaturalness, its conclusions are never considered. So memorize that.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage explains the hierarchy of proposition types and how it influences the ordering of productive syllogism moods. The author begins by distinguishing two qualities (affirmation and negation) and two quantities (universal and particular). Affirmation is considered more honorable because it indicates existence, while negation indicates non-existence. Similarly, universality is more honorable than particularity because it is more precise and beneficial in sciences. When the two honorable elements combine (universal affirmative), or the two base combine (particular negative), the ranking is clear. If honor and baseness conflict, universality takes precedence over affirmation because universality has multiple aspects of honor while affirmation has only one. Thus the order of propositions from most to least honorable is: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. The arrangement of syllogism moods follows this hierarchy: moods producing more honorable conclusions are listed first. If two moods produce conclusions of equal honor, we look at how closely they relate to the first figure (which is the most natural). If both premises are shared with the first figure, we then compare the honor of the premises themselves. If only one premise is shared, the mood sharing the minor premise takes precedence over the one sharing the major. An exception is the fourth figure, which is unnatural, so its moods are ordered based on premises alone, not conclusions. The author ends with a command to memorize this rule.

Conditions for a Valid Second Figure Syllogism
Author lists and explains the three conditions for a valid syllogism in the second figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the three conditions required for a categorical syllogism in the second figure to be productive (valid).

Core Idea
The second figure requires three conditions: different quality, universal major, and modal restrictions.

In the second figure of categorical syllogisms, not every combination of premises yields a valid conclusion. The author of this passage specifies three conditions that must be met for the syllogism to be productive. Understanding these conditions will allow you to quickly assess whether a second-figure argument is sound or fallacious, a skill essential in Islamic dialectics and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Extract the three conditions
From the opening sentence of the Arabic text, list the three conditions that the author states are required for the second figure: (1) the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative), (2) the major premise must be universal, and (3) modal conditions: either the minor is perpetual/necessary, or the major is from the six propositions whose negatives convert; and the possible (contingent) proposition is only used with a necessary premise or a conditional major.
The third condition has two parts: first about converting the negative, second about the possible.
2
Understand why different quality is required
The author gives examples: two affirmatives (e.g., 'Every human is an animal, every rational is an animal') can yield either an affirmative or negative conclusion depending on content, showing disagreement (ikhtilaf). The same happens with two negatives. Disagreement indicates non-productivity. Therefore, one premise must be affirmative and the other negative.
The conclusion must be fixed; variation proves invalidity.
3
Understand why the major must be universal
The author demonstrates with an example: 'Every human is rational, some animal is not rational' (particular major) can give an affirmative conclusion in one material and a negative in another, again causing disagreement. So the major must be universal.
A particular major destroys the determinacy of the conclusion.
4
Analyze the modal conditions
Two modal requirements: first, either the minor is perpetual (dā'ima) or necessary (ḍarūriyya), or the major belongs to the six proposition types whose negative converts (i.e., by contraposition). Second, the possible (mumkina) proposition is only used with a necessary premise (either as minor or major) or with a conditional major. This ensures the conclusion's modality is consistent.
These conditions prevent modal disagreements similar to the quality and quantity conditions.
5
Apply the conditions to a new syllogism
Take a second-figure syllogism: 'No khamr is halal' (universal negative major), 'Every nabidh is khamr' (universal affirmative minor). Check: quality differs (neg/aff), major universal, modal conditions satisfied (no modality). Therefore valid. Compare with a syllogism that violates one condition, e.g., two affirmatives, and see why it fails.
Practise with Islamic examples from fiqh and kalam.

And in the second [figure], their difference in quality, and the universality of the major, with the permanence of the minor or the conversion of the negative major, and the possibility [proposition] with the necessary or a conditional major. The productivity of this figure for the four categorical propositions is self-evident, unlike the productivity of other figures, because their conclusions are theoretical, as their details will come. 
'And in the second, their difference' means: it is required in this figure, with respect to quality, that the two premises differ in negation and affirmation. That is because if this figure is composed of two affirmatives, disagreement occurs in the conclusion, such that sometimes the truth in the conclusion of the syllogism is affirmation, and at other times negation. For if we say 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every rational is an animal', the truth is affirmation. But if we replace the major with 'Every horse is an animal', the truth is negation. Similarly, if it is composed of two negatives, such as our saying 'Nothing of the human is a stone' and 'Nothing of the rational is a stone', the truth is affirmation. And if we say 'Nothing of the horse is a stone', the truth is negation. And disagreement is evidence of non-productivity. For the conclusion is the other statement that follows from the two premises. So if the necessary from the two affirmative premises were that the truth is sometimes the negative, and if the necessary from the two negative premises were that the truth is sometimes the affirmative... 
'And the universality of the major' means: it is required in the second figure, with respect to quantity, that the major be universal, because if it is particular, disagreement occurs. For example, if we say 'Every human is rational' and 'Some animal is not rational', the truth is affirmation. But if we say 'Some whinnying thing is not rational', the truth is negation. 
'With the permanence of the minor' means: it is required in this figure, with respect to modality, two things. The first is one of two: either that permanence applies to the minor, so that it is perpetual or necessary, or that the major is from the six propositions whose negative converts, not from the nine whose negatives do not convert. The second is also one of two: that the possible proposition is not used in this figure except with the necessary, whether the necessary is minor or major, or with a universally or particularly conditional major. The gist is that the possible is... For this is not valid at all, as is not hidden from one who observes the moods in these figures and their conclusions. (Muhammad Ali)

Commentary
This passage details the three conditions required for a valid syllogism in the second figure. The author begins by listing the conditions: different quality (one affirmative, one negative), universality of the major premise, and specific modal conditions (either the minor is necessary/perpetual, or the major is from the class of propositions whose negatives convert; also the possible proposition is only used with a necessary premise or a conditional major). The author notes that the productivity of the second figure for the four categorical propositions is self-evident, unlike other figures whose conclusions require theoretical proof. Then a detailed commentary explains each condition. The first condition (different quality) is justified by showing that if both premises are affirmative, the conclusion can be either affirmative or negative depending on the content, indicating non-productivity. Examples: 'Every human is an animal, every rational is an animal' yields an affirmative conclusion, but if major replaced with 'every horse is an animal', the conclusion becomes negative. Similarly for two negatives. The second condition (universal major) is justified by showing that a particular major leads to disagreement. Example: 'Every human is rational, some animal is not rational' yields an affirmative conclusion, but with a different major 'some whinnying thing is not rational' yields negation. The third condition (modality) requires either the minor be perpetual/necessary, or the major be from the six (those that convert by contraposition). Additionally, the possible proposition (الممكنة) is only used with a necessary premise. The commentary ends with a note that this is clearly invalid if one examines the moods. The author's thorough explanation ensures that the student understands why each condition is necessary to avoid contradictory results.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Conditions for Productivity in the Second Figure: Quality, Quantity, and Modality
Author states and explains the conditions for the second figure's productivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and verify the three conditions (quality, quantity, modality) required for a valid second-figure categorical syllogism and explain why each condition prevents variation in the conclusion.

Core Idea
The second figure requires one negative premise, a universal major, and special modal restrictions to yield a certain conclusion.

In this passage, the commentator refines the conditions for the second figure of categorical syllogism. Unlike the first figure, the second figure is productive only when specific constraints on quality, quantity, and modality are satisfied. We will examine each condition and how it prevents the conclusion from varying with the subject matter, using examples from the text and from Kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions stated by the author
The passage lists: (1) the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative); (2) the major premise must be universal; (3) modal requirements regarding the minor's perpetuity or conversion of the major's negative, and the use of possible only with necessary or conditional premises.
These conditions ensure uniform results without variation.
2
Examine the quality condition
The commentary shows that if both premises are affirmative or both negative, the truth of the conclusion alternates depending on the subject. For example, with two affirmatives 'Every human is animal' and 'Every rational is animal', a conclusion like 'Every human is rational' would be true, but with different subjects it could be false, proving the form is not productive.
A valid second-figure mood must have one negative premise, guaranteeing a negative conclusion.
3
Analyze the quantity condition
If the major premise is particular, variation occurs. Example: 'Every human is rational' (universal affirmative) and 'Some animal is not rational' (particular negative) gives a true affirmative conclusion in some materials and a negative in others. Thus the major must be universal to prevent variation.
The major must be universal for the second figure to be productive with quantified propositions.
4
Undertand the modal conditions
Two modal requirements: First, either the minor is perpetual/necessary (always true), or the major is among the six propositions whose negatives are convertible. Second, the possible (mumkina) can only be used with a necessary or conditional major. Without these, results vary.
These restrictions ensure that the modal status of premises does not lead to contradictory or uncertain conclusions.
5
Summarize why these conditions are necessary
The second figure's productivity for the four quantified propositions is self-evident only when these conditions hold. Otherwise, the conclusion would vary with the subject matter, showing the syllogism is not formally valid.

And in the second [figure] is the difference between them in quality and the universality of the major together with the perpetuity of the minor or the conversion of the universal negative, and that the possible is [used] only with the necessary or a conditional major. The production of this figure for the four quantified [propositions] is evident, unlike the production of the other figures, because their results are theoretical, as their detail will come. His saying: 'And in the second is their difference': i.e., it is required in this figure with respect to quality that the two premises differ in negation and affirmation, because if this figure is composed of two affirmatives, variation occurs in the result, namely that the truth in the result of the syllogism is sometimes affirmation and sometimes negation. For if we say 'Every human is an animal and every rational is an animal', the truth is affirmation, and if we replace the major by saying 'Every horse is an animal', the truth is negation. Similarly, if it is composed of two negatives, as when we say 'Nothing of human is stone and nothing of rational is stone', the truth is affirmation; and if we say 'Nothing of horse is stone', the truth is negation. And variation is evidence of non-productivity, for the result is the other statement that follows from the two premises. So if what follows from the two affirmative premises were [always] affirmative, the truth would not be negative in some matters; and if what follows from them were negative, the truth would not be affirmative in some matters. His saying: 'and the universality of the major': i.e., it is required in the second figure with respect to quantity that the major be universal, because if it is particular, variation occurs, as when we say 'Every human is rational and some animal is not rational' – the truth is affirmation; if we say 'Some neighing thing is not rational', the truth is negation. His saying: 'with the perpetuity of the minor': i.e., two things are required in this figure with respect to modality: the first is one of two things: either that perpetuity is true of the minor, so that it is perpetual or necessary, or that the major is among the six propositions whose negatives are convertible, not among the nine whose negatives do not convert. And the second is also one of two things: namely that the possible is not used in this figure except with the necessary, whether the necessary is minor or major, or with a major that is conditional, general or specific. The upshot is that the possible, if [used] ... this is not valid at all, as is not hidden from one who observes the moods in these figures and their results.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a super-commentary explaining the conditions for the second figure of categorical syllogism. The author first states the three conditions: difference in quality (one affirmative, one negative), universality of the major premise, and two modal requirements regarding the minor premise (either perpetuity or convertibility of the major's negative) and the use of the possible (mumkina) only with necessary or conditional premises. He then confirms that the productivity of the second figure for the four quantified propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) is self-evident, unlike the other figures whose results require theoretical demonstration. The commentary elaborates each condition: If both premises are affirmative or both negative, the result varies with the subject matter, proving non-productivity. For quantity, if the major is particular, variation also occurs. For modality, the first option is that the minor be perpetual/necessary (i.e., always true) or the major's universal negative can be converted (i.e., its contradictory is convertible to a particular affirmative? Actually the text refers to the six propositions that allow conversion of their negatives, a technical detail from modal logic). The second option restricts the possible premise to accompany a necessary or conditional premise. These conditions ensure uniform results without variation.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid second-figure syllogism on the prohibition of nabidh (fermented date drink) in Fiqh. Use the major 'Every intoxicant is prohibited (universal affirmative)' and the minor 'Nabidh is intoxicating (universal affirmative)'? That would both be affirmative, violating the quality condition. Instead, construct: 'No non-intoxicant is prohibited (universal negative) and nabidh is prohibited (particular affirmative?) Wait, that violates. Better: Use the mood Camestres: 'Every prohibited thing is intoxicating (universal affirmative), and nabidh is not intoxicating (universal negative)? No, then conclusion would be 'Nabidh is not prohibited'. That is valid if premises are true. Ensure one premise is negative and major is universal.
?
Take the following invalid second-figure syllogism: 'Every human is animal (A), every horse is animal (A), therefore every human is horse (A).' Identify which condition it violates and explain why the conclusion is not necessarily valid. (Answer: Violates quality condition; both premises affirmative cause variation.)

The Syllogism
