The Four Productive Moods of the Second Figure
Author enumerates the four productive moods and explains how they yield negative conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four productive moods of the second figure and determine whether the conclusion is a universal or particular negative.

Core Idea
The second figure yields only negative conclusions; universality requires both premises universal.

In this lesson, we examine the four valid moods of the second figure as enumerated by the author. You will learn to recognize the premise combinations that produce a universal or particular negative conclusion, and understand the proof methods including conversion and contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Recall the structure of the second figure
In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises. For example, in 'No A is B' and 'Every J is B', the middle term is B.
2
Identify the two universal negative moods
When both premises are universal and one is negative, the conclusion is universal negative. There are two such moods: one with affirmative minor (Cesare) and one with negative minor (Camestres).
Example given: 'Every J is B' and 'Nothing of A is B' yields 'Nothing of J is A'.
3
Identify the two particular negative moods
When the major is universal and the minor is particular, the conclusion is particular negative. There are two such moods: one with affirmative minor (Festino) and one with negative minor (Baroco).
Example given: 'Some J is B' and 'Nothing of A is B' yields 'Some J is not A'.
4
Verify the conclusion type
The conclusion is always negative because one premise is negative. Universality comes from both premises being universal; particularity from the minor being particular.
5
Understand proof methods
The author mentions proof by contradiction (al-khalf) or by conversion of the major or minor, followed by arrangement and then conversion of the result.
These methods are standard for verifying validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لينتج الكليتان سالبة كلية و المختلفتان فى الكم ايضاً سالبة جزئية بالخلف او عكس الكبرى او الصغرى ثم الترتيب ثم عكس النتيجة. قوله لينتج الكليتان اى الضروب المنتجة في هذا الشكل ايضاً اربعة حاصلة من ضرب الكبرى الكلية الموجبة في الصغريين السالبتين الجزئية و الكلية وضرب الكبرى الكلية السالبة فى الصغريين الموجبتين، فالضرب الاول هو المركب من كليتين و الصغرى موجبة نحو كلج ، ب ولا شيء من الف ، ب و الضرب الثانى هو المركب من كليتين والصغرى سالبة كلية نحو: لاشيء من ج ، ب و كل الف، ب والنتيجة فيهما سالبة كلية نحو: لاشيء من ج ، الف و اليهما اشار المصنف بقوله : لينتج الكليتان سالبة كلية والضرب الثالث هو المركب من صغرى موجبة جزئية و كبرى سالبة كلية نحو: بعض ج ، ب ولاشيء من الف ، ب والضرب الرابع هو المركب من صغرى سالبة جزئية وكبرى موجبة كلية نحو بعض ج ليس ب وكل الف ب والنتيجة فيهما سالبة جزئية نحو: بعض جليس الف و اليهما اشار المصنف بقوله و المختلفتان فى الكم ايضاً أى كما انهما مختلفتان في الكيف : بناء على ما سبق في الشرايط «سالبة جزئية». قوله « بالخلف» یعنى دليل انتاج هذه الضروب لهاتين النتيجتين امور: (٥) قوله لينتج الكليتان» اى الصغرى والكبرى الكليتان فتارة تكون الصغرى منهما موجبة والكبرى سالبة فهذا شكل وتارة تكون الصغرى منهما سالبة والكبرى موجبة فهذا شكل آخر نتيجة سالبة كلية، أما كونها سالبة فلان السلب الموجود في احدى المقدمتين يسرى الى النتيجة، واما كونها كلية فلان مقدمتيها جميعاً موصوفتان بالكلية فمن أين تأتى الجزئية للنتيجة؟ (التقريب ص ۹۶) (۰) قوله و المختلفتان فى الكم ايضاً» اى كما هما مختلفتان فى الكيف، وقد سبق في شروط هذا الشكل كلية الكبرى، فالجزئية انما تكون فى الصغرى، فتارة الصغرى الجزئية موجبة وقرينتها تكون سالبة وتارة تكون الصغرى الجزئية سالبة وقرينتها تكون موجبة تنتجان سالبة جزئية، أما كونها سالبة، فلان سلب احدى المقدمتين يسرى الى النتيجة و اما الجزئية، فلكون الموضوع في الصغرى جزئياً و
The two universals produce a universal negative, and those differing in quantity also [produce] a particular negative, by [proof by] contradiction or conversion of the major or the minor, then arrangement, then conversion of the result. His saying: 'The two universals produce' — i.e., the productive moods in this figure are also four, obtained from multiplying the universal affirmative major by the two negative minors, particular and universal, and multiplying the universal negative major by the two affirmative minors. So the first mood is composed of two universals with affirmative minor, e.g., 'Every J is B' and 'Nothing of A is B'. The second mood is composed of two universals with universal negative minor, e.g., 'Nothing of J is B' and 'Every A is B'. The result in both is a universal negative, e.g., 'Nothing of J is A'. The author referred to both by his saying: 'The two universals produce a universal negative.' The third mood is composed of a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, e.g., 'Some J is B' and 'Nothing of A is B'. The fourth mood is composed of a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major, e.g., 'Some J is not B' and 'Every A is B'. The result in both is a particular negative, e.g., 'Some J is not A'. The author referred to both by his saying: 'and those differing in quantity also' — i.e., just as they differ in quality, based on what preceded in the conditions, 'a particular negative'. His saying: 'by contradiction' — i.e., the proof of the productivity of these moods for these two results are affairs: (5) His saying: 'The two universals produce' — i.e., the minor and major are both universal; sometimes the minor is affirmative and the major negative — this is one form; sometimes the minor is negative and the major affirmative — this is another form. The result is a universal negative. As for it being negative, because the negation present in one of the two premises extends to the result; and as for it being universal, because both premises are described as universal, so how can particularity come to the result? (al-Taqrib p. 96) (0) His saying: 'and those differing in quantity also' — i.e., just as they differ in quality, and it preceded in the conditions of this figure that the major is universal, so particularity is only in the minor. Sometimes the particular minor is affirmative and its counterpart is negative, and sometimes the particular minor is negative and its counterpart is affirmative. They produce a particular negative. As for it being negative, because the negation of one of the premises extends to the result; and as for particularity, because the subject in the minor is particular, and
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a super-commentary explaining the four productive moods of the second figure and their proofs. The author first states that when both premises are universal, the conclusion is a universal negative; when they differ in quantity (major universal, minor particular), the conclusion is a particular negative. The conclusion can be proven by contradiction (al-khalf) or by conversion of the major or minor, followed by rearrangement and then conversion of the result. The commentary then details the four moods: two from universal premises (one with affirmative minor, one with negative minor) yielding 'No J is A'; and two from mixed quantity (universal major and particular minor) yielding 'Some J is not A'. It explains that the negativity of the conclusion comes from the negation in one premise spreading to the result, and universality comes from both premises being universal. For the mixed moods, particularity comes from the minor being particular. The commentary also notes that the proof methods include conversion and contradiction, which are standard techniques in Aristotelian logic.

Four Productive Combinations of the Second Figure
Enumeration of four second-figure combinations and their conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four productive combinations of the second figure and state their conclusions.

Core Idea
Second figure has four valid moods, all yielding negative conclusions: universal or particular.

This lesson unpacks the four valid combinations (moods) of the second figure of the categorical syllogism. The matn states that two universal premises yield a universal negative, and premises differing in quantity yield a particular negative. The commentary clarifies the specific combinations. Mastering these is essential for understanding how the second figure is used in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major and minor terms
In each combination, label the terms: the middle term (B) appears in both premises, the major term (A) is the predicate of the conclusion, and the minor term (C) is the subject of the conclusion.
The second figure has the middle term as predicate of both premises.
2
Distinguish the four combinations
The four combinations arise from two factors: the quality of the minor premise (affirmative vs. negative) and the quantity of the minor premise (universal vs. particular). The major premise's quality and quantity are fixed in each pair.
First combination: minor universal affirmative, major universal negative. Second: minor universal negative, major universal affirmative. Third: minor particular affirmative, major universal negative. Fourth: minor particular negative, major universal affirmative.
3
Determine the conclusion for each
For the first two combinations (both universals), the conclusion is a universal negative. For the last two (differing in quantity), the conclusion is a particular negative.
All conclusions are negative because the second figure requires a negative premise.
4
Practice with Islamic examples
Construct a sample syllogism for each combination using terms from Islamic law (fiqh) or theology (kalam). For instance: 'Every Khamr is an intoxicant; no intoxicant is permissible; therefore, no Khamr is permissible.'
Ensure the mood matches the combination.

The two universal [premises] produce a universal negative, and the two differing in quantity also produce a particular negative, by reductio or by conversion of the major or the minor, then transposition, then conversion of the result. He said, 'the two universal [premises] produce' i.e., the productive combinations in this figure are also four, resulting from multiplying the universal affirmative major by the two negative minors (particular and universal) and multiplying the universal negative major by the two affirmative minors. The first combination is composed of two universals with an affirmative minor, e.g., 'Every C is B' and 'No A is B'. The second combination is composed of two universals with a universal negative minor, e.g., 'No C is B' and 'Every A is B'. The result in both is a universal negative, e.g., 'No C is A'. The author referred to these by his saying 'the two universals produce a universal negative'. The third combination is composed of a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, e.g., 'Some C is B' and 'No A is B'. The fourth combination is composed of a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major, e.g., 'Some C is not B' and 'Every A is B'. The result in both is a particular negative, e.g., 'Some C is not A'. The author referred to these by his saying 'and the two differing in quantity also [produce] a particular negative' i.e., just as they differ in quality, based on what preceded in the conditions. He said, 'by reductio' means that the proof of these combinations producing these two results are matters: First: reductio, namely, to take the contradictory of the conclusion and combine it with the minor and major of the syllogism as a major because of their universality, so that from the first figure there results what contradicts the minor, and this applies to all four combinations. Second: conversion of the major to reduce it to the first figure to produce the desired result. That applies only to the first and third combinations because their major is a universal negative which converts as itself. As for the last two, their major is a universal affirmative which converts only to a particular affirmative, not suitable as a major for the first figure, while their minor is also negative, not suitable as a minor for the first figure. Third: to convert the minor so it becomes a fourth figure, then to transpose the order, i.e., make the converted minor a major and the major a minor, so it becomes a first figure that produces a result which converts to the desired result. That is only conceivable when the conversion of the minor is universal, to be suitable as a major for the first figure. That is only in the second combination because its minor is a universal negative which converts as itself. As for the first and third, their minor is affirmative and does not convert except particularly. As for the fourth, its minor is a particular negative and does not convert at all; if its conversion were supposed, it would be only particular, so consider carefully.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn statement regarding the productive combinations of the second figure of the syllogism. It first explains that the matn's mention of 'the two universal [premises] produce a universal negative' refers to two specific combinations: one with a universal affirmative major and a universal negative minor (first combination), and one with a universal negative major and a universal affirmative minor (second combination). Both yield a universal negative conclusion. The matn's mention of 'the two differing in quantity' refers to the other two combinations: one with a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major (third combination), and one with a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major (fourth combination). Both yield a particular negative conclusion. The commentary then discusses the three proof methods for these conclusions. The first method is reductio (khalf): take the contradictory of the conclusion and combine it with the original premises (using the universal major) to produce via the first figure a contradiction with the minor premise. This works for all four combinations. The second method is conversion of the major premise (aks al-kubrā): by converting the major, the syllogism reduces to the first figure, which directly yields the required conclusion. This works only for the first and third combinations because only their major (universal negative) converts to itself; the fourth combination's major (universal affirmative) converts only to a particular affirmative, which is not universal enough to serve as a major in the first figure. The third method is conversion of the minor premise (aks al-sughrā) followed by transposition of the premises to form a first figure: convert the minor (making it the new major), swap the premises, then after obtaining a conclusion, convert that to the desired one. This works only for the second combination, because its minor (universal negative) converts to itself, making the new major universal; the other combinations' minors either convert only particularly or not at all. Thus the commentator carefully explains the scope of each method.

عكس الكبرى
عكس الصغرى

The Syllogism

Three Proof Methods for Second-Figure Syllogisms
Explanation of three proof methods and their applicability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three proof methods for second-figure syllogisms and identify which methods apply to which combinations.

Core Idea
Al-Khalf works for all four, aks al-kubrā only for first and third, aks al-sughrā only for second.

How does a logician prove that a second-figure syllogism is valid? The commentator presents three methods: al-khalf (reductio), aks al-kubrā (conversion of the major), and aks al-sughrā (conversion of the minor with transposition). Each has its scope, depending on the quantity and quality of the premises. This lesson clarifies these methods.

Guided Path
1
Understand the goal of each method
Each method aims to show that the conclusion necessarily follows from the premises by reducing the second-figure syllogism to a known valid form (first figure) or by deriving a contradiction.
2
Analyze al-khalf (reductio)
Assume the contradictory of the conclusion. Combine it with one of the original premises (the one that is universal) to form a first-figure syllogism. This yields a contradiction with the other premise, proving the original conclusion.
Works for all four combinations because we can always choose the universal premise.
3
Analyze aks al-kubrā (conversion of the major)
Convert the major premise. If the major is universal negative, it converts to itself; then the syllogism becomes a valid first-figure mood. This works only for combinations where the major is universal negative (first and third).
If the major is universal affirmative, it converts only to particular, which cannot serve as a major in the first figure.
4
Analyze aks al-sughrā (conversion of the minor with transposition)
Convert the minor premise, then transpose so that the converted minor becomes the new major and the original major becomes the new minor, forming a first-figure syllogism. The conclusion of that syllogism is then converted to get the desired conclusion. This works only when the minor is universal negative (second combination), because it converts to itself.
Affirmative minors convert only particularly, and particular negatives do not convert.

The Syllogism

Conditions for the Minor Premise in the Second Figure
Author explains two conditions for the minor premise in the second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to verify whether a second figure categorical syllogism satisfies the conditions of an affirmative and actual minor premise and at least one universal premise, and identify when the conclusion fails due to violation of these conditions.

Core Idea
In the second figure, a valid conclusion requires the minor premise to be affirmative, actual, and at least one premise universal.

In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises. For a conclusion to follow necessarily, special conditions bind the minor premise: it must be both affirmative and actual (not merely possible), and one of the two premises must be universal. This passage from al-Yazdi's Hashiya explains the logical rationale for these conditions, using a counterexample to show what goes wrong when they are violated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second figure structure
In the second figure, the middle term is predicate in both premises (P-M, S-M). The minor premise links the minor term (S) to the middle (M), and the major premise links the major term (P) to the middle (M).
2
Check the minor premise's quality and modality
The minor premise must be affirmative (not negative) and actual (not potential or possible). If it is negative, there is no union between S and M. If it is possible, the union is not actual, and the judgment about M (from the major premise) cannot extend to S.
The judgment in the major premise is about M in actuality.
3
Check universality of one premise
At least one premise must be universal. If both are particular, the middle term might refer to different subsets: the part of M that relates to S may be different from the part that relates to P, so no common ground guarantees the conclusion.
Example: 'Some animal is human' and 'Some animal is horse' both true, but 'Some human is horse' does not follow.
4
Test a syllogism for validity
Apply both conditions to a given second figure syllogism. Verify the minor is affirmative and actual, and at least one premise is universal. If either condition fails, the syllogism is invalid.
Remember: these are necessary conditions, not sufficient for all cases, but crucial in the second figure.

قوله ایجاب الصغرى و فعليتها لان الحكم في كبراه سواء كان ايجاباً أو سلباً على ما هو اوسط بالفعل كما مر فلو لم يتحد الاصغر مع الأوسط بالفعل بان لا يتحد اصلاً ويكون الصغرى سالبة او يتحد لكن لا بالفعل ويكون الصغرى موجبة ممكنة، لم يتعد الحكم من الاوسط بالفعل الى الاصغر. قوله مع كلية احديها لانه لو كانت المقدمتان جزئيتين لجاز ان يكون البعض من الاوسط المحكوم عليه بالاصغر غير البعض المحكوم عليه بالاكبر فلا يلزم تعدية الحكم من الاكبر الى الاصغر مثلاً يصدق بعض الحيوان انسان و بعض الحيوان فرس و لا يصدق بعض الانسان فرس.
His saying, 'the affirmation and actuality of the minor', because the judgment in its major, whether affirmative or negative, is about the middle term in actuality as has passed. So if the minor term is not united with the middle term in actuality—either by not being united at all, making the minor negative, or being united but not in actuality, making the minor a possible affirmative—the judgment will not extend from the middle term in actuality to the minor term. His saying, 'with the universality of one of them' , because if both premises were particular, it would be possible that some of the middle term judged about the minor is other than that judged about the major, so the extension of the judgment from the major to the minor would not be necessary. For example, 'Some animal is human' and 'Some animal is horse' are true, but 'Some human is horse' is not true.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage explains two conditions for the minor premise in the second figure. First, the minor must be affirmative and actual (i.e., asserting a real, not potential, connection). The reason is that the major premise's judgment (affirmative or negative) applies to the middle term in actuality. If the minor term is not actually united with the middle—either because it is not united at all (so the minor is negative) or because the union is possible but not actual (so the minor is a possible affirmative)—then the judgment about the middle term cannot be transferred to the minor term. Second, one of the premises must be universal. If both were particular, the middle term could be partially related to the subject of the minor and partially to the predicate of the major, so that no common middle term guarantees the conclusion. The example given: 'Some animal is human' and 'Some animal is horse' do not entail 'Some human is horse' because the middle term 'animal' is divided. Thus these conditions ensure the validity of the second figure.

Conditions and Reduction Methods in the Third Figure
Explains conditions and reduction methods for third figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two conditions for a valid third-figure syllogism and apply the three reduction methods to convert it to the first figure.

Core Idea
The third figure requires specific conditions and offers three reduction routes to the certain first figure.

The third figure of categorical syllogisms has unique conditions that must be satisfied for a valid conclusion. This passage from a classical hashia explains these conditions and then presents three methods to reduce third-figure syllogisms to the first figure, which is the only figure yielding certain conclusions directly. Understanding these reductions allows you to transform any valid third-figure argument into a more transparent form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
Find where the author states the conditions: the minor premise must be affirmative and actual (not possible or negative), and one of the premises must be universal. Read the explanation that follows.
These conditions ensure that the middle term is distributed and the conclusion follows necessarily.
2
Understand the first reduction method: khalf
Locate the description of khalf (reductio per impossibile). Note that it uses the contradictory of the conclusion along with the original premises to derive a contradiction, and works for all four moods.
Khalf is the most general method.
3
Analyze the second method: conversion of the major
Read when conversion of the major premise works: only when the major is a universal negative (first and third moods). Understand why universal affirmatives fail because they convert only to particulars.
Conversion of major directly yields a first-figure syllogism.
4
Examine the third method: conversion of the minor with reversal
This method involves converting the minor, swapping premises to form a first-figure, then converting the conclusion. Note that it works only for the second mood (universal negative minor).
This indirect method uses the fourth figure as an intermediate step.
5
Apply the conditions to an example
Consider the author's example: 'Some animal is human, some animal is horse' does not imply 'some human is horse'. Explain why this violates the universality condition because both premises are particular.
This illustrates the necessity of at least one universal premise.

و في الثالث ايجاب الصغرى وفعليتها مع كلية احديهما الأول: الخلف وهو: ان يجعل نقيض النتيجة لا يجابه صغرى وكبرى القياس لكليتها كبرى لينتج من الشكل الاول ماينا فى الصغرى و هذا جار فى الضروب الاربعة كلها . والثانى عكس الكبرى ليرتد إلى الشكل الاول لينتج النتيجة المطلوبة وذلك انما يجرى فى الضرب الاول و الثالث لان كبربهما سالبة كلية تنعكس كنفسها و أما الاخيران فكبريهما موجبة كلية لا تنعكس إلا إلى موجبة جزئية لا تصلح لكبروية الشكل الاول مع ان صغريهما ايضاً سالبة لا تصلح صغرى للشكل الاول . والثالث: ان ينعكس الصغرى فيصير شكلاً رابعاً ثم ينعكس الترتيب يعنى: يجعل عكس الصغرى كبرى والكبرى صغرى فيصير شكلاً اولا ينتج نتيجة تنعكس الى النتيجة المطلوبة و ذلك انما يتصور فيها يكون عكس الصغرى كلية ليصلح لكبروية الشكل الاول و هذا انما في الضرب الثانى فان صغراه سالية كلية تنعكس كنفسها واما الاول والثالث فصغريهما موجبة لا تنعكس الاجزئية و اما الرابع فصغراه سالبة جزئية لا تنعكس اصلاً ولو فرض انعكاسها لا يكون هو الا جزئية فتدبر. قوله ایجاب الصغرى و فعليتها لان الحكم في كبراه سواء كان ايجاباً أو سلباً على ما هو اوسط بالفعل كما مر فلو لم يتحد الاصغر مع الأوسط بالفعل بان لا يتحد اصلاً وتكون الصغرى سالبة او يتحد لكن لا بالفعل وتكون الصغرى موجبة ممكنة، لم يتعد الحكم من الاوسط بالفعل الى الاصغر قوله «مع كلية احديها لانه لو كانت المقدمتان جزئيتين لجاز ان يكون البعض من الاوسط المحكوم عليه بالاصغر غير البعض المحكوم عليه بالاكبر فلا يلزم تعدية الحكم من الاكبر الى الاصغر مثلاً يصدق بعض الحيوان انسان و بعض الحيوان فرس و لا يصدق بعض الانسان فرس
And in the third [figure]: the affirmative affirmation of the minor and its actuality together with the universality of one of them. The first [method] is khalf: which is to take the contradictory of the conclusion together with the minor and major premises of the syllogism for their universality as major, so that from the first figure is produced what contradicts the major. And this applies to all four moods. The second [method] is conversion of the major so that it reverts to the first figure to produce the desired conclusion. This applies only to the first and third moods, because their majors are universal negatives that convert as themselves. As for the remaining two, their majors are universal affirmatives that only convert to a particular affirmative, unfit to be major of the first figure, while their minors are also negative, unfit to be minor of the first figure. The third [method] is to convert the minor so that it becomes a fourth figure, then reverse the order: that is, make the converse of the minor the major and the major the minor, so that it becomes a first figure and produces a conclusion whose converse is the desired conclusion. This is conceivable only when the converse of the minor is universal to be suitable as major of the first figure; this occurs only in the second mood, for its minor is a universal negative that converts as itself. As for the first and third moods, their minors are affirmative and convert only to a particular; as for the fourth, its minor is a particular negative that does not convert at all; and if its conversion is assumed, it would only be particular, so reflect. His statement: 'the affirmative affirmation of the minor and its actuality' because the judgment in its major, whether affirmative or negative, concerns the middle actually, as previously. For if the minor were not actually united with the middle—either not at all, so the minor is negative, or united but not actually, so the minor is a possible affirmative—the judgment would not transfer from the middle actually to the minor. His statement: 'with the universality of one of them' because if both premises were particular, it would be possible that some of the middle judged by the minor is not the same as some judged by the major, so the transfer of the judgment from the major to the minor would not hold. For example, 'Some animal is human' and 'Some animal is horse' are true, but 'Some human is horse' is not true.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions and reduction methods for syllogisms in the third figure. The author first states two conditions: the minor premise must be affirmative and actual (i.e., a categorical affirmative, not a possible or negative), and one of the premises must be universal. Then he explains three methods to reduce third-figure syllogisms to the first figure, which is the only figure that yields certain conclusions directly. The first method is reductio per impossibile (khalf): take the contradictory of the conclusion along with the original minor and major as premises (making both universal) to derive from the first figure a contradiction with the original major. This works for all four moods. The second method is conversion of the major premise: this works only for moods where the major is a universal negative (first and third moods), because universal negatives convert universally, while universal affirmatives only convert partially. The third method involves converting the minor premise to get a fourth figure, then swapping the premises so that the converted minor becomes the major and the original major becomes the minor, resulting in a first-figure syllogism whose conclusion is then converted to the desired conclusion. This method works only for the second mood (minor universal negative), because its minor converts universally. The author then elaborates on the condition that the minor must be affirmative and actual: the judgment about the middle (distributed in the major) must apply to the minor actually, which requires the minor to affirm actual union (not mere possibility) of the subject with the middle. He also explains the need for one universal premise: if both were particular, the middle term might not be distributed, so the inference fails, as shown by the example of 'some animal is human' and 'some animal is horse' not implying 'some human is horse'. This passage is an expansion of the matn's condensed rules, typical of a hashiya.

Six Productive Moods of the Third Figure and Their Proofs
Enumerates six moods and three proof methods for third figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the six productive moods of the third figure and verify each using khalf, conversion of the minor, or conversion of the major with reversal.

Core Idea
The third figure yields only particular conclusions, verified by three distinct reduction methods.

In this passage, the commentator expands on the matn's brief statement about the third figure's productive moods. You will learn not only which moods are productive, but also how to prove that they yield the correct conclusions using three different reduction techniques: reductio per impossibile (khalf), conversion of the minor premise, and conversion of the major followed by premise reversal. These techniques are essential for reducing any valid syllogism to the perfect first figure, ensuring logical certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six moods from the combinations
The passage explains that the six moods arise by combining a universal affirmative minor with all four possible majors (A, E, I, O) and a particular affirmative minor with two universal majors (A and E). List each mood with its premise types and quantifiers.
Remember: only particular conclusions are produced in the third figure.
2
Classify moods as affirmative or negative
Among the six, three yield affirmative conclusions (when both premises are affirmative) and three yield negative conclusions (when one premise is negative). Identify which moods belong to each group.
The conclusion's quality matches the presence of a negative premise.
3
Apply the proof methods: khalf
Khalf works for all six moods: assume the contradictory of the conclusion, combine it with the original minor to form a first-figure syllogism that contradicts the original major. Trace this for one affirmative mood.
This is a general method but less direct than conversion.
4
Apply conversion of the minor
When the major is universal (moods 1,2,4,5), convert the minor premise to get a first-figure syllogism. Check that the conversion preserves truth and yields the desired conclusion after conversion.
Conversion of E requires careful handling; here the minor is always affirmative.
5
Apply conversion of the major with reversal
When the major is affirmative and the minor is universal (moods 1 and 3 only), convert the major, then swap premises to form a fourth-figure syllogism, then convert the conclusion to obtain the original desired conclusion.
This is a two-step reduction: first to fourth figure, then to first.

لينتج الموجبتان مع الموجبة الكلية او بالعكس موجبة جزئية و مع السالبة الكلية او الكلية مع الجزئية، سالبة جزئية بالخلف او عكس الصغرى والكبرى ثم قوله « لينتج الموجبتان»: الضروب المنتجة في هذا الشكل بحسب الشرايط المذكورة ستة حاصلة من ضم الصغرى الموجبة الكلية الى الكبريات الاربع و ضم الصغرى الموجبة الجزئية الى الكبريين الكليتين الموجبة والسالبة. و هذه الضروب كلها مشتركة فى انها لا تنتج الاجزئية لكن ثلاثة منها تنتج الايجاب و ثلاثة منها تنتج السلب و اما المنتجة للايجاب فاولها المركب من موجبتين كليتين نحو كل ج ، ب وكل ج ، الف فبعض ب ، الف وثانيها المركب من موجبة جزئية صغرى و موجبة كلية كبرى و الى هذين اشار المصنف بقوله: لينتج الموجبتان ای: الصغرى الموجبة مع الموجبة الكلية اى الكبرى و الثالث عكس الثاني اعنى: المركب من موجبة كلية صغرى و موجبة جزئية كبرى و اليه اشار بقوله: «او بالعكس» فليس المراد بالعکس، عكس الضربين المذكورين اذ ليس عكس الأول الا الاول فتأمل. و اما المنتجة للسلب فاولها المركب من موجبة كلية صغرى و سالبة كلية كبرى. و الثانى من موجبة جزئية صغرى و سالبة كلية كبرى واليهما اشار بقوله: «و مع السالبة الكلية والثالث من موجبة كلية صغرى وسالبة جزئية كبرى كما قال المصنف : او الكلية مع الجزئية اى الموجبة الكلية مع السالبة الجزئية. قوله «بالخلف» یعنی بيان انتاج هذه الضروب لهذه النتايج اما بالخلف و هو هيهنا ان يؤخذ نقيض النتيجة ويجعل لكليته كبرى وصغرى القياس لا يجابها صغرى لينتج من الشكل الاول ماينا في الكبرى و هذا يجرى في هذه الضروب كلها و اما بعكس الصغرى ليرجع الى الشكل الاول وذلك حيث تكون الكبرى كلية كما فى الاول والثانى والرابع و الخامس و اما بعكس الكبرى ليصير شكلا رابعاً ثم عكس الترتيب ليرتد شكلا اولاً و ينتج نتيجة ثم يعكس هذه النتيجة فانه المطلوب و ذلك حيث تكون الكبرى موجبة ليصلح عكسه صغرى للشكل الاول و يكون الصغرى كلية ليصلح كبرى نه كما فى الضرب الاول و الثالث لا غير.
So that the two affirmatives produce with the universal affirmative, or by reversal, a particular affirmative, and with the universal negative, or the universal with the particular, a particular negative, by khalf or conversion of the minor and major. Then his statement 'So that the two affirmatives produce': the productive moods in this figure according to the mentioned conditions are six, obtained by combining the universal affirmative minor with the four majors, and the particular affirmative minor with the two universal majors, affirmative and negative. And these moods all share that they produce only particulars, but three produce affirmation and three produce negation. As for the affirmative-yielding: the first is composed of two universal affirmatives, e.g., 'Every J is B' and 'Every J is A', so 'Some B is A'; the second is composed of a particular affirmative minor and a universal affirmative major—and the author pointed to these two by his saying 'the two affirmatives produce', i.e., the affirmative minor with the universal affirmative major; the third is the converse of the second, i.e., composed of a universal affirmative minor and a particular affirmative major—the author pointed to it by his saying 'or by reversal', so 'reversal' here does not mean the converse of the two mentioned moods, since the converse of the first is only the first, so reflect. As for the negative-yielding: the first is composed of a universal affirmative minor and a universal negative major; the second of a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major—the author pointed to these two by his saying 'and with the universal negative'; the third is composed of a universal affirmative minor and a particular negative major, as the author said: 'or the universal with the particular', i.e., the universal affirmative with the particular negative. His statement 'by khalf' means the demonstration of these moods producing these conclusions is either by khalf, which here is to take the contradictory of the conclusion, make it universal as the major, and [the original] minor and major as the minor, to produce from the first figure a contradiction in the major—this applies to all these moods; or by conversion of the minor to revert to the first figure, and that is where the major is universal, as in the first, second, fourth, and fifth; or by conversion of the major to become a fourth figure, then reversal of order to revert to a first figure, and produce a conclusion, then convert that conclusion—that is the desired result, and that is where the major is affirmative so its converse is suitable as minor of the first figure, and the minor is universal so as to be suitable as major—as in the first and third moods only.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage enumerates the six productive moods of the third figure and explains how to prove that they yield the stated conclusions. The author begins by summarizing: two affirmatives (affirmative minor and major) produce a particular affirmative when combined with a universal affirmative or its reverse; with a universal negative or a universal with a particular, they produce a particular negative; and these results are verified by khalf, conversion of minor, or conversion of major with reversal. Then the commentary expands: the six moods arise from combining a universal affirmative minor with all four possible majors (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) and a particular affirmative minor with two universal majors (affirmative and negative). All six yield only particular conclusions: three affirmative, three negative. The affirmative moods are: (1) both premises universal affirmative (e.g., Every J is B, Every J is A → Some B is A); (2) minor particular affirmative, major universal affirmative; (3) minor universal affirmative, major particular affirmative (this is the 'reverse' of the second). The negative moods are: (1) minor universal affirmative, major universal negative; (2) minor particular affirmative, major universal negative; (3) minor universal affirmative, major particular negative. Then the proof methods: khalf works for all six; conversion of the minor (to first figure) works when the major is universal (moods 1, 2, 4, 5); conversion of the major with reversal (to fourth then first figure) works when the major is affirmative and the minor is universal (moods 1 and 3 only). This passage is a detailed expansion of a matn statement, typical of a hashiya, giving the full list and verification.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Productive moods of the third figure
Author explains the six productive moods of the third figure and their proof by reductio or conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the six productive moods of the third figure and identify the proof method used for each.

Core Idea
The third figure yields only particular conclusions, proved by reductio or conversion.

In this passage, the commentator expands on the matn's rule about the third figure. He lays out six valid moods—three affirmative, three negative—all producing particular conclusions. He then explains two proof strategies: reductio ad absurdum and conversion to the first figure. Understanding these moods is essential for evaluating syllogistic arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence, where many inferences are framed in the third figure.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the third figure structure
The third figure has the middle term as the subject of both premises. For example: Every J is B (minor) and Every J is A (major) yields Some B is A.
The middle term (J) is the subject in both premises.
2
List the six productive moods
From the passage, there are three affirmative moods: (1) both universal affirmative, (2) particular affirmative minor + universal affirmative major, (3) universal affirmative minor + particular affirmative major. Three negative moods: (4) universal affirmative minor + universal negative major, (5) particular affirmative minor + universal negative major, (6) universal affirmative minor + particular negative major.
All conclusions are particular.
3
Identify the proof method for each mood
The commentator states that reductio applies to all moods. Conversion of the minor works when the major is universal (moods 1, 2, 4, 5). Conversion of the major works when the major is affirmative and the minor universal (moods 1 and 3 only).
Reductio is always an option; conversion is more efficient but has conditions.
4
Practice converting a mood
Take the first affirmative mood: Both premises universal affirmative. Convert the minor (Every J is B → Some B is J) and place in first figure with the major unchanged: Some B is J, Every J is A → Some B is A. This yields the same conclusion by conversion to first figure.
Conversion of the minor changes a universal affirmative to a particular affirmative.

في القياس واقسامه باعتبار الهيئة
لينتج الموجبتان مع الموجبة الكلية او بالعكس موجبة جزئية و مع السالبة الكلية او الكلية مع الجزئية، سالبة جزئية بالخلف او عكس الصغرى والكبرى ثم

In qiyas and its divisions with respect to form: The two affirmatives produce with the universal affirmative or by the opposite a particular affirmative, and with the universal negative or the universal with the particular, a particular negative by reductio or by conversion of the minor and major. Then his saying: 'The two affirmatives produce' — the productive moods in this figure according to the mentioned conditions are six, arising from combining the universal affirmative minor with the four majors, and combining the particular affirmative minor with the two universal majors, the affirmative and negative. These moods all share that they produce only particulars, but three of them produce affirmation and three produce negation. As for those producing affirmation, the first is composed of two universal affirmatives, e.g., 'Every J is B' and 'Every J is A' so 'Some B is A'. The second is composed of a particular affirmative minor and a universal affirmative major. The author referred to these two by his saying: 'The two affirmatives produce', i.e., the affirmative minor with the universal affirmative (the major). The third is the converse of the second, i.e., composed of a universal affirmative minor and a particular affirmative major. He referred to it by his saying: 'or by the opposite'. The intended opposite is not the conversion of the two mentioned moods, since the converse of the first is only the first, so ponder. As for those producing negation, the first is composed of a universal affirmative minor and a universal negative major. The second is composed of a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major. He referred to both by his saying: 'and with the universal negative'. The third is composed of a universal affirmative minor and a particular negative major, as the author said: 'or the universal with the particular', i.e., the universal affirmative with the particular negative. His saying: 'by reductio' means the explanation of the production of these moods for these conclusions is either by reductio (khalf), which here is that the contradictory of the conclusion is taken and made universal as major and minor of the syllogism for its affirmation as minor, so that from the first figure there results what contradicts the major. And this applies to all these moods. Or by conversion of the minor to return to the first figure, and that is when the major is universal, as in the first, second, fourth, and fifth. Or by conversion of the major to become a fourth figure, then conversion of the order to return to a first figure, and it produces a conclusion, then that conclusion is converted, and that is what is sought. And that is when the major is affirmative so that its conversion is suitable as minor for the first figure, and the minor is universal so that it is suitable as major for it, as in the first and third moods only.

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary (hashiya) on the matn discussing the productive moods of the third figure (al-shakl al-thalith) of the categorical syllogism. The author first quotes the matn statement: 'The two affirmatives produce...' which is a general rule about the third figure. Then the commentator expands by enumerating exactly six productive moods, three affirmative and three negative, all producing particular conclusions. He gives examples using placeholder letters (ج، ب، الف) to illustrate both universal and particular premises. The three affirmative moods are: (1) two universal affirmatives; (2) particular affirmative minor with universal affirmative major; (3) universal affirmative minor with particular affirmative major. The three negative moods are: (1) universal affirmative minor with universal negative major; (2) particular affirmative minor with universal negative major; (3) universal affirmative minor with particular negative major. The commentator then explains the methods of proof for these moods: either by reductio (khalf) where the contradictory of the conclusion is assumed to derive a contradiction in the first figure; or by conversion of the minor premise (which works when the major is universal); or by conversion of the major premise (which works when the major is affirmative and the minor universal) to convert to a fourth figure, then reverse order to first figure, then convert the conclusion. These methods apply to specific moods as indicated. The examples from the text use categorical statements such as 'Every J is B' and 'Every J is A' to yield 'Some B is A', which follows the pattern of the third figure where the middle term is the subject of both premises.

The Syllogism

Conditions for Productive Moods in the Fourth Figure
Author states the two conditions for valid syllogisms in the fourth figure and explains why other combinations are sterile.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which premise combinations produce valid conclusions in the fourth figure of categorical syllogisms and explain why other combinations are sterile.

Core Idea
The fourth figure is unnatural: only two conditions yield productive moods.

In this lesson, we examine the strict conditions for the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism. The author explains that only two premise patterns are productive, and all others lead to sterility. Through concrete examples using everyday terms, you will learn to distinguish valid from invalid combinations and understand why the fourth figure is considered less natural.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two valid conditions
The author states that the fourth figure produces a conclusion if and only if either (a) both premises are affirmative and the minor premise is universal, or (b) the premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and at least one premise is universal.
These are the only two cases that yield a valid mood in this figure.
2
Identify the three sterile patterns
If neither condition holds, three sterile cases arise: (1) both premises negative, (2) both affirmative with minor particular, (3) both particular differing in quality. The author labels these as sterile because the same pair of premises can lead to different contradictory conclusions depending on the middle term.
Sterility here means no categorical conclusion necessarily follows.
3
Analyze the sterile examples given
For case (1): two negatives like 'No stone is a human' and 'No rational is a stone' yield an affirmative conclusion ('Some stone is not rational' is not forced; different substitutions give different truth values). For case (2): 'Some animal is human' and 'Every rational is animal' leads to uncertainty. For case (3): 'Some horse is animal' and 'Some body is not animal' also leads to difference.
The examples show that the truth values of possible conclusions vary, indicating sterility.
4
Apply the conditions to new premise pairs
Test a pair of premises: e.g., 'Every scholar is knowledgeable' (universal affirmative minor) and 'Every knowledgeable person is respected' (universal affirmative major) — condition (a) holds, so a valid conclusion 'Some respected person is a scholar' follows after conversion. If the minor were particular, no valid conclusion would follow.
Practice with various combinations to internalize the conditions.

His saying: 'And in the fourth' — i.e., the condition for the production of the fourth figure according to quantity and quality is one of two things: either both premises are affirmative with the universality of the minor, or the premises differ in quality with the universality of one of them. That is because if neither one [of these conditions] holds, it results in either both premises being negative, or both affirmative with the minor being particular, or both particular differing in quality. Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on the conditions for productive moods in the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism. The commentator begins by quoting the matn: 'And in the fourth...' and then explains that the condition for production (intaj) in the fourth figure with respect to quantity and quality is one of two: either (a) both premises are affirmative and the minor premise is universal, or (b) the two premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and at least one of them is universal. If neither condition is met, three sterile cases arise: (1) both premises negative, (2) both affirmative with the minor particular, or (3) both particular but differing in quality. The commentator illustrates each sterile case with examples using terms like 'stone', 'human', 'rational', 'horse', 'animal', 'body'. For example, two negative premises like 'No stone is a human' and 'No rational is a stone' actually yield an affirmative conclusion (since no conclusion follows from two negatives); the truth here is affirmative because the conclusion would be 'Some stone is not human'? Actually, the explanation is a bit terse but shows that such combinations lead to inconclusiveness. The commentator then notes that the author omitted conditions for modality (jiha) because the fourth figure is rarely used due to its unnaturalness, and also omitted discussion of mixtures of modalized premises in all four figures due to length. Finally, a Persian mnemonic verse is provided to help memorize the conditions for all four figures. The verse uses coded abbreviations like 'mughkab', 'khum khab', etc., to stand for the conditions of each figure.

The Eight Productive Moods of the Fourth Figure
Author enumerates the eight productive moods of the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the eight productive moods of the fourth figure and identify the type of conclusion (affirmative/negative, universal/particular) each produces.

Core Idea
The fourth figure has eight productive moods; only mood III yields a universal negative.

In categorical syllogisms, the fourth figure has specific valid combinations of premises. Here the author lists all eight productive moods and their conclusions, correcting a common mistake. Understanding these moods is essential for evaluating syllogistic arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the origin of the eight moods
The passage states that the eight moods arise from joining a universal affirmative minor with the four possible majors, plus other combinations. Note that they are based on two unnamed conditions from earlier.
The conditions are not detailed here; focus on the enumeration.
2
List the eight moods explicitly
The eight moods are: I) two universal affirmatives; II) universal affirmative minor + particular affirmative major; III) universal negative minor + universal affirmative major; IV) universal affirmative minor + universal negative major; V) particular affirmative minor + universal negative major; VI) particular negative minor + universal affirmative major; VII) universal affirmative minor + particular negative major; VIII) universal negative minor + particular affirmative major.
Moods IV-VIII are also described as 'the opposite of that' for IV and then the rest.
3
Determine the conclusion for each mood
Moods I and II produce a particular affirmative. Mood III produces a universal negative. All other moods (IV-VIII) produce a particular negative.
The author criticizes the original text for claiming all non-first-two moods give particular negative, which ignores mood III.
4
Memorize the exception
Mood III is the only one that yields a universal negative; all other negative-containing moods yield particular negatives.
This is the key correction the commentator makes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله « لينتج»: الضروب المنتجة في هذا الشكل بحسب احد الشرطين السابقين ثمانية حاصلة من ضم الصغرى الموجبة الكلية الكبريات الأربع و جزئية موجبة ان لم يكن سلب والا فسالبة بالخلف او بعكس الترتيب ثم عكس الصغرى الموجبة الجزئية مع الكبرى السالبة الكلية وضم الصغريين السالبتين الكلية و الجزئية مع الكبرى الموجبة الكلية وضم كليتها اى الصغرى السالبة الكلية مع الكبرى الموجبة الجزئية. ف الاولان من هذه الضروب وهما المؤلف من موجبتين كليتين والمؤلف من موجبة كلية صغرى و موجبة جزئية كبرى، ينتجان موجبة جزئية والبواق المشتملة على السلب تنتج سالبة جزئية في جميعها الا في ضرب واحد و هو المركب من صغرى سالبة كليه وكبرى موجبة كلية فانه ينتج سالبة كلية . وفي عبارة المصنف تسامح حيث توهم ان ما سوى الاولين من هذه الضروب ينتج السلب الجزئى و ليس كذلك كما عرفت ولو قدم لفظ «موجبة» على «جزئية» لكان أولى. والتفصيل هيهنا ان ضروب هذا الشكل ثمانية، الأول: من موجبتين كليتين . الثانى من موجبة كلية صغرى و موجبة جزئية كبرى ينتجان موجبة جزئية. الثالث من صغرى سالبة كلية وكبرى موجبة كلية ينتج سالبة كلية . الرابع: عكس ذلك الخامس من صغرى موجبة جزئية و كبرى سالبة كلية السادس من سالبة جزئية صغرى و موجبة كلية كبرى السابع من موجبة كلية صغرى و سالبة جزئية كبرى الثامن : من سالبة كلية صغرى و موجبة جزئية كبرى و هذه الضروب الخمسة الباقية تنتج سالبة جزئية فاحفظ هذا التفصيل فانه نافع فيما سيجيء. قوله « بالخلف»: و هو في هذا الشكل ان يؤخذ نقيض النتيجة ويضم الى احدى المقدمتين لينتج ما ينعكس الى ما ينافي المقدمة الأخرى، وذلك انما يجرى في الضرب الاول والثانى والثالث و الرابع و الخامس دون البواقى و قال المصنف فى شرح الرسالة بجريانه فى السادس وهو سهو. قوله «او» بعكس الترتيب: وذلك انما يجرى حيث يكون الكبرى موجبة و الصغرى كلية و النتيجة مع ذلك قابلة للانعكاس كما في الاول والثانى والثالث و الثامن ايضاً ان انعكست السالبة الجزئية كما اذا كانت احدى الخاصتين دون البواقى.
His statement 'so that it produces': the productive moods in this figure according to one of the two previous conditions are eight, resulting from joining the universal affirmative minor with the four majors, and [if there is no negation then] a particular affirmative, otherwise a negative by contradiction or by inversion of order, then inversion of the particular affirmative minor with the universal negative major, and joining the two negative minors (universal and particular) with the universal affirmative major, and joining its universal—i.e., the universal negative minor—with the particular affirmative major. So the first two of these moods—the one composed of two universal affirmatives and the one composed of a universal affirmative minor and a particular affirmative major—produce a particular affirmative, and the rest that include negation produce a particular negative in all of them except one mood, which is the one composed of a universal negative minor and a universal affirmative major, for it produces a universal negative. And in the author's expression there is a looseness, for he imagined that other than the first two of these moods produce the particular negative, but it is not so as you have known, and if he had placed the word 'affirmative' before 'particular' it would have been better. The detail here is that the moods of this figure are eight: first, from two universal affirmatives; second, from a universal affirmative minor and a particular affirmative major—both produce a particular affirmative; third, from a universal negative minor and a universal affirmative major—it produces a universal negative; fourth, the opposite of that; fifth, from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major; sixth, from a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major; seventh, from a universal affirmative minor and a particular negative major; eighth, from a universal negative minor and a particular affirmative major—and these remaining five moods produce a particular negative. So memorize this detail, for it is useful for what will come. His statement 'by contradiction': it is in this figure that one takes the contradictory of the conclusion and joins it to one of the premises so that it produces something that, when inverted, contradicts the other premise. This applies in the first, second, third, fourth, and fifth moods, not the rest. And the author said in his commentary on the Risāla that it applies in the sixth, but that is an oversight. His statement 'or by inversion of order': this applies where the major is affirmative and the minor is universal, and the conclusion is reversible, as in the first, second, third, and also the eighth if the particular negative is reversed, as when one of the two conditions is present without the rest.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn, detailing the eight productive moods of the fourth figure. The author begins by stating that according to the two conditions explained earlier, there are eight moods. They are formed by combining the universal affirmative minor with the four possible majors (universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative) — but if the major is negative, the conclusion is negative and proven by contradiction or inversion of order. Then the author mentions other combinations: the particular affirmative minor with the universal negative major, the two negative minors (universal and particular) with the universal affirmative major, and the universal negative minor with the particular affirmative major. The first two moods (I: two universal affirmatives; II: universal affirmative minor + particular affirmative major) produce a particular affirmative. The rest produce a particular negative except mood III (universal negative minor + universal affirmative major), which produces a universal negative. The author then critiques the original author's phrasing as sloppy, since he suggested all non-first-two moods produce particular negative, which is false due to mood III. He prefers placing 'affirmative' before 'particular' to avoid confusion. After listing all eight moods explicitly (I–VIII), he emphasizes memorizing them. Then he explains two proof techniques: proof by contradiction (باعتبار الخلف) and proof by inversion of order (بعكس الترتيب). Proof by contradiction: assume the opposite of the conclusion, combine with one premise to derive something that contradicts the other premise. This works for moods I–V. The author notes that the original writer mistakenly claimed it works for mood VI. Inversion of order: reversing the order of premises (minor becomes major and vice versa) — only works when the major is affirmative, the minor is universal, and the conclusion is reversible. This applies to moods I, II, III, and possibly VIII if the particular negative conclusion can be converted.

Proof by Contradiction and Inversion of Order in the Fourth Figure
Author explains proof by contradiction and inversion of order in the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how proof by contradiction (bil-khalf) and inversion of order ('aks al-tartib) validate moods in the fourth figure and identify which moods each method applies to.

Core Idea
Proof by contradiction works for moods I-V; inversion of order works for moods with affirmative major, universal minor, and reversible conclusion.

After listing the eight moods, the commentator explains two techniques to prove their validity without constructing full syllogisms. These shortcuts help verify conclusions quickly, but they have specific conditions. Understanding them deepens your grasp of syllogistic structure.

Guided Path
1
Understand proof by contradiction (bil-khalf)
The method: assume the opposite (contradictory) of the conclusion, add it to one premise, and derive something that contradicts the other premise. This proves the original conclusion by reductio.
The text says this works for moods I–V, not VI–VIII. The author corrects a mistake that said it works for VI.
2
Identify the moods where contradiction applies
Moods I, II, III, IV, V are valid for khalf; moods VI, VII, VIII are not. The original writer erroneously included VI.
Memorize the exception: khalf fails for moods VI–VIII.
3
Understand inversion of order ('aks al-tartib)
Swap the minor and major premises (so the minor becomes the major, and vice versa), then convert the conclusion. This only works if the original major is affirmative, the minor is universal, and the conclusion can be inverted (i.e., converted validly).
Conversion of the conclusion means applying immediate inference (e.g., converting 'Some A is B' to 'Some B is A').
4
List the moods where inversion applies
It applies to moods I, II, III, and also VIII if the particular negative conclusion can be converted (controversially). The text notes that inversion works 'where the major is affirmative and the minor is universal and the conclusion is reversible'.
Mood VIII has a particular negative conclusion; its conversion is not generally valid in Aristotelian logic, so the condition may be specialized.

Eight Moods of the Second Figure
Enumeration of eight moods of the second figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each mood of the second figure from its premise descriptions and state the type of conclusion (universal/particular, affirmative/negative).

Core Idea
The second figure has eight premise combinations, but only a subset yield valid conclusions.

In this passage, the author systematically lists all eight moods of the second figure, specifying the premises and conclusion for each. This enumeration is essential for recognizing valid syllogistic forms in Islamic logic. Mastering these moods prepares you for reducing syllogisms to the first figure and evaluating arguments in usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Understand the second figure's structure
The second figure has the middle term as the predicate of both premises. Recall the four proposition types: universal affirmative (A), universal negative (E), particular affirmative (I), particular negative (O).
The figure is defined by the position of the middle term, not by mood.
2
Identify the first two moods
Both have affirmative premises: first mood (AA) from two universal affirmatives; second mood (AI) from universal affirmative minor and particular affirmative major. Both yield a particular affirmative (I) conclusion.
These are the only moods in this figure that produce an affirmative conclusion.
3
Analyze the third and fourth moods
Third mood (EA): universal negative minor, universal affirmative major → universal negative (E) conclusion. Fourth mood (AE): the opposite (universal affirmative minor, universal negative major) → part of the five moods that yield particular negative (O).
The fourth is the reverse of the third; the author groups it with the remaining moods.
4
Examine the remaining five moods (fifth to eighth)
These are: IE (particular affirmative minor, universal negative major), OA (particular negative minor, universal affirmative major), AO (universal affirmative minor, particular negative major), EI (universal negative minor, particular affirmative major), and the fourth (AE) already mentioned. All produce a particular negative (O) conclusion.
Memorize: moods 4–8 all give O conclusions.
5
Memorize the pattern for quick recall
First two → I, third → E, fourth to eighth → O. This pattern is useful when checking validity or reducing syllogisms to the first figure.
Focus on the conclusion type; the validity of each mood can be verified later.

Commentary
This passage is a classification: the author enumerates all eight possible moods of the second figure according to the quantity and quality of the premises, and then specifies which moods produce what conclusion. The first two moods (both having affirmative premises) yield a particular affirmative conclusion. The third mood (universal negative minor, universal affirmative major) yields a universal negative conclusion. The remaining five moods (fourth through eighth) all yield a particular negative conclusion. The author emphasizes memorizing this because these moods will be referenced in reductions and other logical operations. In Islamic logic, the second figure is defined as one where the middle term is the predicate of both premises. These eight moods represent all possible combinations of quantity and quality in that figure, but only a subset are valid according to standard logic; however, the author treats them all as producing conclusions, possibly considering the figure's rules differently. For example, a mood with two universal affirmatives (first) is actually invalid in the second figure because the middle term is not distributed; but the author may be describing a broader set of moods that include invalid ones, or he may be following a different logical framework.

Conditions for Reducing Syllogisms to the Three Figures
Author explains three methods of reduction and their validity conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which reduction method applies to a given categorical syllogism based on the quantity and quality of premises.

Core Idea
Reduction is not arbitrary; each method has strict conditions based on premise quality and quantity.

This passage from the commentary clarifies the precise conditions under which a syllogism can be reduced to the first, second, or third figure by conversion. Understanding these conditions is essential for determining whether a syllogism in an imperfect figure is valid. The commentator details three methods and their applicability to specific moods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three reduction methods
The passage lists three ways to reduce a syllogism: (1) converting both premises to revert to the first figure, (2) reducing to the second figure by converting the minor premise, and (3) reducing to the third figure by converting the major premise.
2
Examine conditions for converting both premises
For method (1), the minor must be affirmative and the major must be universal negative, so that after conversion the major remains universal negative. This applies only to certain moods, e.g., the fourth and fifth moods (of the first figure).
The 'fourth and fifth' refer to later moods in the standard enumeration.
3
Examine conditions for reduction to the second figure
For method (2), the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative), the major must be universal, and the minor must be convertible (i.e., particular affirmative). This applies to the third, fourth, fifth, and sixth moods, but only if the particular negative is convertible.
Conversion of a particular negative (Some S is not P) is not always valid in standard logic; the commentator notes this restriction.
4
Examine conditions for reduction to the third figure
For method (3), the minor must be affirmative, the major must be convertible, and either the minor itself or the converse of the major must be universal. This applies to the first, second, fourth, fifth, and seventh moods, again with the condition that the particular negative is converted.
The last condition is necessary for the first two moods of the third figure.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining three methods of reducing categorical syllogisms to the perfect figures (first, second, third). The author first lists three options: (1) converting both premises, (2) reducing to the second by converting the minor premise, (3) reducing to the third by converting the major premise. The commentator then specifies the exact conditions under which each method is valid. For converting both premises to the first figure: the minor must be affirmative and the major must be universal negative so that after conversion it remains universal negative. For reduction to the second figure by converting the minor: the premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative), the major must be universal, and the minor must be convertible (i.e., particular affirmative). For reduction to the third figure by converting the major: the minor must be affirmative, the major must be convertible, and either the minor itself or the converse of the major must be universal. The commentator notes that the last condition is necessary for the first two moods of the third figure to be reduced. These conditions ensure that after conversion, the syllogism fits the required form of the target figure. An example from usul al-fiqh: If we have a syllogism in the fourth figure (e.g., Every a is b, Some b is c, therefore Some a is c?) we may need to convert premises to bring it to the first figure. The commentator emphasizes that only certain moods are reducible under each method.

The Master Rule (Dabita) for Productive Syllogisms
Author formulates the universal rule for productive syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two disjuncts of the dabita rule and identify which moods of the four figures satisfy each disjunct.

Core Idea
The dabita condenses all conditions for productive syllogisms into two alternative requirements: universality of the middle subject plus actual meeting, or universality of the major.

The commentator presents a comprehensive rule that governs all four figures of categorical syllogism. This rule, known as the dabita, systematizes the scattered conditions for each figure into a single formula. Understanding this rule allows you to quickly determine whether a given syllogism is productive without memorizing each figure's separate conditions.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the scope of the dabita
The dabita applies to all four figures. It is not figure-specific but covers all productive moods.
2
Identify the two disjuncts
The rule states: either (1) the middle term is universal in subject and actually meets the minor or is predicated on the major, OR (2) the middle term is universal in subject (like the major in the first figure).
3
Map the disjuncts to actual moods
First figure moods satisfy the first disjunct (meeting of middle with minor). Third figure moods satisfy the second disjunct (universality of the major). Fourth figure moods vary.
4
Test the rule with a sample syllogism
Take a valid first figure: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; all nabidh is intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden.' Check that the middle term 'intoxicant' is universal in the minor premise and actually predicated of the minor (nabidh). The rule holds.

ضابطة شرايط الاربعة انه لابد اما من عموم موضوعية الاوسط مع ملاقاته للاصغر بالفعل او حمله على الاكبر و اما من عموم موضوعية الاوسط كالكبرى فى الشكل الاول وكاحدى المقدمتين فى الشكل الثالث و كالصغرى في الضرب الاول والثانى والثالث والرابع والسابع والثامن من الشكل الرابع. قوله «مع ملاقاته اى اما بان يحمل الاوسط ايجاباً على الاصغر بالفعل كما فى صغرى الشكل الأول و اما بان يحمل الاصغر على الاوسط ايجاباً بالفعل كما في صغرى الشكل الثالث وكما في صغرى الضرب الاول و الثاني والرابع و السابع من الشكل الرابع. ففى الكلام اشارة استطرادية الى اشتراط فعلية الصغرى فى هذه الضروب ايضاً. قوله او حمله على الاكبر» اى مع حمل الاوسط على الاكبر ايجاباً فان السلب سلب الحمل و انما الحمل هو الايجاب و ذلك كما فى كبرى الضرب الاول و الثانى والثالث و الثامن من الشكل الرابع فالضربان الاولان قد اندرجا تحت كلا شقى الترديد الثانى فهو ايضا على سبيل منع الخلو كالاول و هيهنا تمت الاشارة الى شرایط انتاج جميع ضروب الشكل الاول و الثالث وستة ضروب من الشكل الرابع فاحفظ . واعلم انه لم يقل: «اوللاكبر اى او مع ملاقاته للاكبر، حتى يكون اخصر، لان الملاقات تشتمل الوضع والحمل كما تقدم فيلزم كون القياس المرتب على هيئة الشكل الاول من كبرى موجبة كلية مع صغرى سالبة، منتجاً هف. و يلزم ايضاً كون القياس المرتب على هيئة الشكل الثالث من صغرى سالبة و كبرى موجبة مع كلية احدى المقدمتين منتجاً. وقد اشتبه ذلك على بعض الفحول فاعرفه . قوله و اما من عموم موضوعية الاكبر»: هذا هو الامر الثاني من الامرين اللذين ذكرنا اولاً انه لابد فى انتاج القياس من احدهما. وحاصله كلية كبرى حيث موضوعية الاوسط ان يكون كل قضية موضوعها الاوسط كلياً حتى يرد ان احدى المقدمتين في الشكل الثالث و كذا الصغرى في الضرب الخامس والسادس من الشكل الرابع جزئية مع كون الاوسط موضوعاً فيها كما هو ظاهر للعالم بسياق الكلام.
The rule for the conditions of the four [figures]: it is necessary either from the universality of the subject of the middle [term] with its meeting with the minor actually, or its predication on the major, or from the universality of the subject of the middle [term] like the major in the first figure, and like one of the two premises in the third figure, and like the minor in the first, second, third, fourth, seventh and eighth moods of the fourth figure. His statement 'with its meeting' means either by predicating the middle affirmatively on the minor actually as in the minor of the first figure, or by predicating the minor on the middle affirmatively actually as in the minor of the third figure and in the minor of the first, second, fourth and seventh moods of the fourth figure. In the statement there is a parenthetical indication that actuality of the minor is also required in these moods. His statement 'or its predication on the major' means with the middle predicated on the major affirmatively, for negation is negation of predication and predication is only affirmation, and that as in the major of the first, second, third and eighth moods of the fourth figure. So the first two moods have fallen under both segments of the second disjunction, so it also is on the basis of excluding emptiness like the first. And here the conditions for producing all moods of the first and third figures and six moods of the fourth figure have been indicated, so memorize. And know that he did not say 'or on the major' i.e. 'or with its meeting with the major' to be more concise, because meeting includes position and predication as previously, so it would necessitate that the syllogism arranged in the form of the first figure with a universal affirmative major and a negative minor is productive? And it would also necessitate that the syllogism arranged in the form of the third figure with a negative minor and affirmative major with universality of one premise is productive. And this was confused by some eminent scholars, so know it. His statement 'and either from the universality of the subject of the major': this is the second of the two matters we mentioned first that in the production of the syllogism it is necessary from one of them. Its summary is the universality of the major where the subject of the middle is that every proposition whose subject is the middle is universal, so that it be accepted that one of the premises in the third figure and likewise the minor in the fifth and sixth moods of the fourth figure is particular while the middle is subject in them, as is clear to one familiar with the context of the speech.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents the master rule (ضابطة) for the conditions of productive categorical syllogisms in the four figures. The commentator first states the rule: every productive syllogism must satisfy either (A) the middle term is universal in subject and actually meets (i.e., is predicated affirmatively upon) the minor or is predicated affirmatively upon the major, or (B) the middle term is universal in subject (i.e., its subject is universal) like the major in the first figure. He then explains each clause. Under (A), 'meeting' means either the middle is predicated affirmatively of the minor (as in first figure minor) or the minor is predicated of the middle (as in third figure minor). The commentator notes that this implicitly requires the minor to be actual (not potential). For the major, 'predication' means the middle is predicated affirmatively of the major (as in certain moods of the fourth figure). The first two moods of the fourth figure fall under both disjuncts, so they are productive. Under (B), the second condition is simply that the major premise be universal (since the middle is subject of the major). The commentator then warns against a potential mistake: if we said 'meeting with the major' instead of 'predication on the major', it could wrongly validate invalid syllogisms, e.g., a first figure with universal affirmative major and negative minor, or a third figure with negative minor and affirmative major. This confusion occurred among some scholars. The commentator also clarifies that the second condition (universality of the major) covers cases like the third figure where one premise is particular but the middle is subject. An example: In the first figure, if we have 'Every a is b' (universal affirmative major) and 'No c is a' (negative minor), the 'meeting' condition would wrongly imply productivity; but the correct rule prevents this. This shows the precision of the hashiya in refining the conditions.

Key Terms
ضابطة
عموم موضوعية
ملاقاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first figure, which condition of the dabita is primarily satisfied?
A) Universality of the middle subject only
B) Meeting of the middle with the minor
C) Both A and B
D) Neither, because first figure has its own rule

Key Takeaway
The dabita unifies all conditions for productive syllogisms into two alternative disjuncts: the middle term being universal and actually meeting the minor or major, or the major being universal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the dabita, check whether the following syllogism is productive: 'Every beneficial act is recommended; some silent prayers are not beneficial; therefore some silent prayers are not recommended.' Which condition applies? (Hint: identify the figure first.)
?
Explain why the third figure mood with a universal affirmation major and a particular minor is productive according to the dabita, using a fiqh example.
