The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Understanding 'Meeting' (Mulaqat) and Its Implications
The commentator clarifies the meaning of 'meeting' and its actuality requirement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between 'meeting with the minor' and 'predication on the major' as used in the dabita rule, and explain why actuality is required.

Core Idea
'Meeting' is not mere predication but actual, affirmative predication that ensures the syllogism's productivity.

The commentator distinguishes between two ways the middle term can relate to the minor and major. 'Meeting' (mulaqat) involves the middle being actually predicated of the minor or the minor predicated of the middle. This actuality is crucial because potential predication would not yield a conclusion. Understanding this distinction prevents a common error that even some eminent scholars made.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of meeting
The commentator says meeting means either the middle is predicated affirmatively of the minor (first figure) or the minor is predicated of the middle (third figure).
2
Notice the requirement of actuality
The commentator explicitly says 'bil-fi'l' (actually), indicating that the predication must be real, not potential.
3
Contrast with predication on the major
For the major, the commentator uses 'haml' (predication) which is also affirmative. He emphasizes that negation is not predication.
4
Apply the distinction to a concrete example
Take a first figure: 'Every a is b; all c is a; therefore all c is b.' Here the middle a is actually predicated of c in the minor. That is meeting. If the minor were potential (e.g., 'c could be a'), the syllogism would fail.

Avoiding Common Confusions: The Precision of the Rule
The commentator warns against a misinterpretation that would validate invalid syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacious inference that would result from replacing 'predication on the major' with 'meeting with the major', and explain why the rule is formulated as it is.

Core Idea
A subtle wording change would make the rule accept invalid syllogisms, showing the precision required in logical formulation.

The commentator warns against a potential misinterpretation of the dabita. If one were to understand the second disjunct as 'or with its meeting with the major' instead of 'or its predication on the major', then certain invalid syllogisms would wrongly be considered productive. This warning reveals how carefully the conditions must be stated to avoid error even among skilled logicians.

Guided Path
1
Identify the potential error
The commentator says: he did not say 'with its meeting with the major' because that would include the case where the middle meets the major but not by predication (e.g., via negation or other relation).
2
Understand the counterexample
A first figure syllogism with universal affirmative major and negative minor: 'Every a is b; no c is a; therefore no c is b' is actually invalid. But 'meeting' might wrongly suggest it is valid because the middle a 'meets' the major b (affirmatively) and also meets the minor c (negatively? Actually the minor is negative so no meeting).
The commentator's point is that 'meeting' includes both position and predication, so it could be misinterpreted as including negative relations.
3
Trace the logic
The commentator says 'meeting includes position and predication' which would allow a universal affirmative major and negative minor to be considered productive. But the correct rule requires predication (affirmative) on the major and actual meeting with the minor, so the negative minor fails.
4
Recognize the real-world confusion
The commentator says some eminent scholars fell into this error, so you must guard against it.

C) Because meeting is only for the minor
D) Because predication is a stronger condition

Key Takeaway
The choice of words in logical rules is critical: using 'predication' instead of 'meeting' excludes invalid syllogisms and preserves the rule's correctness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The First Condition for Syllogistic Production: Meeting or Predication
Explanation of first syllogistic condition and enumeration of moods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which moods in the four figures satisfy the first condition of syllogistic production and explain why.

Core Idea
A syllogism produces its conclusion only if the middle term meets the minor or is predicated on the major.

In this passage, the author explains the first of two conditions that make a categorical syllogism productive. The condition revolves around the relation of the middle term to the minor and major terms. Understanding this condition is crucial for evaluating the validity of syllogisms used in Islamic theological and jurisprudential arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two disjuncts
The first condition has two parts: (a) the middle actually meets the minor, or (b) the middle is predicated on the major. Determine what each means in terms of predication.
Meeting can be either the middle predicated on the minor or the minor predicated on the middle.
2
Locate where each part applies
The author lists specific moods for each part. For part (a), find the moods in the first figure, third figure, and fourth figure. For part (b), find the moods in the fourth figure.
Part (a) appears in the minor premise; part (b) in the major premise.
3
Check for overlap
Note that the first two moods of the fourth figure satisfy both parts; the condition is satisfied in a 'non-vacuous' manner.
4
Memorize the list
The author enumerates all moods of the first and third figures and six moods of the fourth figure. Create a mnemonic to remember which moods satisfy the condition.
The sixth mood of the fourth figure is not included; only 1,2,3,4,7,8.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage explains the first condition for a syllogism to be productive (muntij), which is that the middle term must either actually meet the minor term (i.e., be predicated of it or the minor be predicated of it) or be predicated on the major term. The author then enumerates which moods across the four figures satisfy this condition. For the first condition, the middle meets the minor actually in the minor premise of the first figure, the third figure, and certain moods of the fourth figure. For the second condition, the middle is predicated on the major in the major premise of the first, second, third, and eighth moods of the fourth figure. The author also notes that the first two moods of the fourth figure fall under both disjuncts, so the condition is satisfied in a 'non-vacuous' manner. This is a technical discussion typical of later logical commentaries, enumerating the moods to ensure that the conditions for production are fully understood.

Why Precision Matters in Logical Conditions: Critiquing a Shorter Formulation
Critique of a proposed shorter formulation for the first condition of a syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how an ambiguous term in a syllogistic condition can accidentally validate invalid mood-figure combinations.

Core Idea
Brevity can introduce ambiguity that undermines logical validity.

In this lesson, we examine a critical refinement from a hashiya on syllogistic conditions. The author warns against using a shorter phrasing for the first condition of a categorical syllogism because the word 'meeting' (ملاقات) is ambiguous. If adopted, this shorter phrasing would incorrectly imply that certain invalid syllogisms are productive. This example shows why technical terms must be precisely defined in logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original condition
Recall that the first condition for a productive syllogism requires that the middle term 'meet' the major in at least one premise, either as subject (position) or as predicate (predication).
2
Understand the proposed shorter phrasing
Instead of specifying both position and predication, one might say 'or with its meeting the major' (أو مع ملاقاته للأكبر), which is shorter but ambiguous.
3
Recognize the ambiguity of 'meeting'
The term 'meeting' (ملاقات) includes both position (الوضع) and predication (الحمل). The shorter phrase does not restrict the kind of meeting, leading to unintended validity.
4
Trace the erroneous consequences
If the condition were just 'meeting', then a first-figure syllogism with a universal affirmative major and a negative minor would be considered valid (though it is not), and similarly a third-figure syllogism with a negative minor and affirmative major would be considered valid (though it is not).
These are known invalid mood-figure combinations.
5
Evaluate the logic of the critique
The author's point is that a good condition must exclude all invalid forms. The shorter phrasing fails because it includes forms that are actually invalid. This shows the importance of precise wording in logical definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم انه لم يقل: «اوللاكبر اى او مع ملاقاته للاكبر، حتى يكون اخصر، لان الملاقات تشتمل الوضع والحمل كما تقدم فيلزم كون القياس المرتب على هيئة الشكل الاول من كبرى موجبة كلية مع صغرى سالبة، منتجاً هف. و يلزم ايضاً كون القياس المرتب على هيئة الشكل الثالث من صغرى سالبة و كبرى موجبة مع كلية احدى المقدمتين منتجاً. وقد اشتبه ذلك على بعض الفحول فاعرفه
And know that he did not say: 'or with its meeting the major' so that it would be briefer, because 'meeting' includes position and predication as previously, and it would necessitate that a syllogism arranged in the form of the first figure from a universal affirmative major with a negative minor would be productive, which is false. And it would also necessitate that a syllogism arranged in the form of the third figure from a negative minor and a positive major with universality of one of the two premises would be productive. And this has been confused by some eminent scholars, so know it.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement typical of a hashiya. The author points out that the original author of the matn deliberately did not phrase the condition as 'or with its meeting the major' (even though it would be shorter) because 'meeting' includes both position (putting the middle as subject) and predication (putting it as predicate). If that shorter phrasing were used, it would wrongly imply that a first-figure syllogism with a universal affirmative major and a negative minor (which is invalid in first figure) would be productive, and similarly a third-figure syllogism with a negative minor and affirmative major would be productive (invalid in third figure). The author notes that this confusion has afflicted some eminent scholars, so the student should be aware.

Key Takeaway
A condition in logic must be precise enough to exclude all invalid forms; ambiguous abbreviations can introduce logical errors.

The Second Condition: Universality of the Major Premise
Author explains the second condition for syllogistic production: the major premise must be universal in terms of subject-generality of the middle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second condition for syllogistic production (subject-universality of the major) and determine which moods of which figures satisfy it.

Core Idea
The major premise must have a universal subject (the middle term) to avoid particularity in certain figures.

We have learned the first condition for syllogistic production. Now we examine the second condition, which concerns the subject of the major premise. The author explains that the major premise must be universal in terms of its subject, which is the middle term in some figures. This condition is necessary for syllogisms where the middle is subject, such as in the third figure and some moods of the fourth.

Guided Path
1
Define subject-universality of the major
Understand that this means the subject term of the major premise (which is the middle term in certain figures) must be universal in quantity. This condition excludes particular premises where the middle is subject.
This is sometimes called عموم موضوعية الأكبر.
2
Identify which moods require this condition
The author states that the second condition excludes particular premises in the third figure and in the fifth and sixth moods of the fourth figure. Additionally, the major is subject in all moods of the second figure and in the third, fourth, fifth, and sixth moods of the fourth figure.
See the text for the listing.
3
Distinguish between the two conditions
The first condition relates to the subject-universality of the minor premise or the conclusion; this second condition relates to the subject-universality of the major. They are complementary for valid syllogisms.
4
Apply to a concrete syllogism
Take a syllogism from Islamic theology: 'Everything contingent has a cause; the world is contingent; therefore the world has a cause.' Check if the major premise ('Everything contingent has a cause') has a universal subject (yes, 'everything contingent'). So this condition is satisfied.
In the third figure, the middle is subject of the major premise, so the major premise must be universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله و اما من عموم موضوعية الاكبر»: هذا هو الامر الثاني من الامرين اللذين ذكرنا اولاً انه لابد فى انتاج القياس من احدهما. وحاصله كلية كبرى حيث موضوعية الاوسط ان يكون كل قضية موضوعها الاوسط كلياً حتى يرد ان احدى المقدمتين في الشكل الثالث و كذا الصغرى في الضرب الخامس والسادس من الشكل الرابع جزئية مع كون الاوسط موضوعاً فيها كما هو ظاهر للعالم بسياق الكلام. يكون الاكبر موضوعاً فيها مع اختلاف المقدمتين فى الكيف وذلك كما في جميع ضروب الشكل الثانى و كما فى الضرب الثالث والرابع و الخامس والسادس من الشكل الرابع. فقد اشتمل الضرب الثالث والرابع منه على كلا الامرين و لذا حملنا الترديد الأول على منع الخلو. فقد اشير الى جميع شرايط الشكل الاول و الثالث بحسب الكم والكيف والجهة و الى شرايط الشكل الثاني والرابع كماً وكيفاً و بقيت شرايط الشكل الثانى بحسب الجهة فاشار اليها بقوله : مع منافاة الخ.
His saying: 'and as for from the aspect of the subject-universality of the major': this is the second of the two matters that we mentioned first that in the production of a syllogism one of them is necessary. Its summary is the universality of the major in terms of the subject-universality of the middle: that every proposition whose subject is the middle be universal, so that it is excluded that one of the two premises in the third figure and likewise the minor in the fifth and sixth moods of the fourth figure be particular while the middle is subject in them, as is apparent to one aware of the context of speech. The major is subject in them with difference in quality between the two premises, and that is as in all moods of the second figure and as in the third, fourth, fifth, and sixth moods of the fourth figure. So the third and fourth moods of it have comprised both matters, and therefore we carried the first disjunction on prevention of vacuity. So all the conditions of the first and third figures in terms of quantity and quality and modality have been indicated, and the conditions of the second and fourth figures in terms of quantity and quality, and the conditions of the second figure with respect to modality remain, so he indicated them by his saying: 'with incompatibility of...' etc.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage explains the second condition for syllogistic production, which is that the major premise must be universal in the sense that its subject (which is the middle term in some figures) is universal. This ensures that no particular premise occurs where the middle is subject, such as in the third figure or in certain moods of the fourth figure. The author then specifies which moods of which figures satisfy this condition: the major is subject in all moods of the second figure and in the third, fourth, fifth, and sixth moods of the fourth figure. The third and fourth moods of the fourth figure satisfy both conditions (the first condition also), so they are termed 'non-vacuous'. The summary indicates that conditions for the first and third figures have been fully given regarding quantity, quality, and modality, while for the second and fourth figures only quantity and quality have been given, with modality remaining for the second figure, which leads to the next condition.

The Third Condition for the Second Figure: Incompatibility of Relations
Author explains the third condition for the second figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third condition for the second figure and determine whether a given pair of modal premises satisfies the incompatibility of relations.

Core Idea
The middle term's relations must be modally incompatible for valid second‑figure syllogisms.

You already know that the second figure requires the major premise to be universal and the premises to differ in quality. But there is a third condition: the relations of the middle term to the major and minor must be incompatible in such a way that they cannot both be true even if the terms were hypothetically unified. This condition is tied to the modal properties of the premises, as explained in the passage.

Guided Path
1
Recall the second figure structure
In the second figure, the middle term is predicated in both premises. The major premise must be universal and the two premises must have opposite quality (one affirmative, one negative). This much you know from the first two conditions.
2
Understand the third condition: incompatibility of relations
The author says there must be a 'munāfāt' between the relation of the middle term's description (when predicated in the minor) to the major term's description in the major, and the relation of the same middle description to the minor subject. In simpler terms, the two relations must be so opposed that they cannot both be true under any hypothetical unification of their subjects.
This goes beyond mere difference in quality; it involves modal strength.
3
Link the condition to modality
The incompatibility coextends with the modal conditions of the premises. For instance, if the minor premise is a perpetual affirmative (always true), and the major premise is an absolute general (which indicates actual negation of the middle from the major), then the relation to the minor is 'perpetual affirmation' and to the major is 'actual negation'. These are incompatible: perpetual and actual cannot both hold.
The absolute general is broader than other modal propositions except the possible ones, which have their own rule.
4
Apply the principle of incompatibility with the more general
The author argues that if a relation is incompatible with a more general proposition (like absolute general), it is also incompatible with any more specific proposition of the same type. So checking incompatibility with the absolute general suffices for most cases.
This allows a systematic test.
5
Test a concrete example from Islamic law
Take the minor: 'Every obligatory prayer is an act of worship at all times' (perpetual affirmative). Major: 'No act of worship is a merely habitual action at this moment' (actual negative of the middle 'worship' from 'habitual action'? Actually need an example: 'Every act of worship is perpetually commanded' vs 'No act of worship is actually performed without intention' – but the middle must be the same. Better: Minor: 'Every Friday prayer is performed with congregation perpetually' (dawām al-ījāb). Major: 'No Friday prayer is actually performed without ablution' (fiʿliyyat al-salb). The relation to the minor is perpetual affirmation, to the major is actual negation; they are incompatible, so the condition is satisfied. If the relation to the major were also perpetual affirmation, the condition would fail.
This is a constructed example for illustration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله مع منافاة» یعنى ان القياس المنتج المشتمل على الامر الثاني اعنى: « عموم موضوعية الاكبر مع الاختلاف فى الكيف اذا كان الاوسط منسوباً و محمولاً في كلتا مقدمتيه كما في الشكل الثاني فحينئذ لابد فى انتاجه من شرط ثالث و هو منافاة نسبة وصف الاوسط المحمول فى الصغرى الى وصف الاكبر الموضوع في الكبرى لنسبة وصف الاوسط المحمول كذلك إلى ذات الاصغر الموضوع فى الصغرى يعنى لابد ان تكون النسبتان المذكورتان مكيفتين بكيفيتين بحيث يمتنع اجتماع هاتين النسبتين فى الصدق لو اتحد طرفا هما فرضاً و هذه المنافاة دايرة وجوداً و عدماً مع مامر من شرطي الشكل الثانى بحسب الجهة فبتحققها يتحقق الانتاج و بانتفائها ينتفى الانتاج اما انها دايرة مع الشرطين وجوداً اى كلما وجد الشرطان المذكوران تحقق المنافاة المذكورة، فلانه اذا كانت الصغرى مما يصدق عليه الدوام والكبرى التى قضية كانت من الموجهات ما عدا الممكنتين فان لهما حكماً عليحدة سیجی فلاشك انه حينئذ يكون نسبة وصف الاوسط الى ذات الاصغر بدوام الايجاب مثلاً ولا اقل من ان يكون نسبة وصف الاوسط الى وصف الاكبر بفعلية السلب ضرورة ان المطلقة العامة اعم من تلك الكبريات و المطلقة العامة تدل على سلب الاوسط عن ذات الاكبر بالفعل و اذا كان مسلوباً عن الاكبر بالفعل كان مسلوباً عن وصفه بالفعل قطعاً ولا خفاء في المنافات بين دوام الايجاب و فعلية السلب و اذا تحققت المنافات بين شيء وبين الاعم، لزم المنافات بينه و بين الاخص بالضرورة و كذا اذا كانت الكبرى مما تنعكس سالبتها
His saying: 'with incompatibility' means that the productive syllogism that includes the second matter, i.e., 'subject-universality of the major with difference in quality', when the middle is related and predicated in both its premises as in the second figure, then in its production a third condition is necessary: the incompatibility of the relation of the description of the middle predicated in the minor to the description of the major subject in the major, compared to the relation of the description of the middle likewise predicated to the essence of the minor subject in the minor. That is, it is necessary that the two mentioned relations be qualified by two qualities such that the conjunction of these two relations in truth is impossible if their two sides were hypothetically unified. And this incompatibility coextends in existence and non-existence with the two conditions of the second figure that have passed in terms of modality. So by its realization, production is realized, and by its non-existence, production is negated. As for its coextension with the two conditions in existence, i.e., whenever the two mentioned conditions are found, the mentioned incompatibility is realized, because if the minor is of the type that is characterized by perpetuity [of affirmation], and the major is whatever proposition it may be from the modal propositions except the two possible ones, for they have a separate rule that will come, then there is no doubt that then the relation of the description of the middle to the essence of the minor is by perpetuation of affirmation, for example, and at the very least the relation of the description of the middle to the description of the major is by actual negation, of necessity, because the absolute general is more general than those majors, and the absolute general indicates the negation of the middle from the essence of the major actually, and if it is negated from the major actually, it is negated from its description actually necessarily. And there is no hiddenness in the incompatibility between perpetuation of affirmation and actual negation. And if incompatibility is realized between something and the more general, incompatibility between it and the more specific follows necessarily. And similarly if the major is such that its negative converts...
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage explains the third condition necessary for the second figure, in addition to the second condition (subject-universality of the major with difference in quality). The condition is an incompatibility (munāfāt) between the relation of the middle term to the major term and its relation to the minor term. More precisely, the middle term is predicated in both premises, but one relation must be affirmative and the other negative in such a way that they cannot both be true simultaneously if the terms were hypothetically identical. The author then argues that this incompatibility coextends with the two modal conditions of the second figure (perpetuity and actuality). For example, if the minor premise is perpetual (affirmative with 'always') and the major is an absolute general proposition (which indicates actual negation), then there is incompatibility between the perpetual affirmation and the actual negation. The argument uses the principle that if a thing is incompatible with a more general proposition, it is incompatible with the specific one as well. This detailed modal analysis is typical of later logical works that incorporate modality into syllogistic conditions.

Key Terms
منافاة
دوام الايجاب
فعلية السلب
مطلقة العامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes the third condition for the second figure?
A) The major premise must be universal and the premises must differ in quality.
B) The middle term must be predicated in both premises.
C) The relation of the middle to the minor must be incompatible with its relation to the major in terms of modality, such that they cannot both be true.
D) The conclusion must be negative.

Key Takeaway
The third condition for the second figure is a modal incompatibility between the middle term's relations to the extremes, which is automatically satisfied when the minor is perpetual and the major is an actual absolute negative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a second‑figure syllogism about Islamic obligations where the minor is perpetual affirmative and the major is perpetual negative. Does it satisfy the incompatibility condition? Explain why or why not.
?
Given the minor 'Every act of prayer is an occasion of submission perpetually' and the major 'No act of prayer is a moment of pride actually', test the third condition by checking if the relation to the minor (perpetual affirmation) and to the major (actual negation) are incompatible according to the passage.

The Syllogism

The Third Condition for the Second Figure Syllogism
Explanation of the third condition for second figure syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third condition (incompatibility of relations) for a productive second figure syllogism and verify its presence by checking the modalities of the premises.

Core Idea
In the second figure, the middle's relations must be incompatible to prevent both premises being true without the conclusion.

In previous lessons, you learned that the second figure requires the major to be the subject and the premises to differ in quality. However, a productive second figure syllogism also requires a third condition related to modality: an incompatibility between the relation of the middle's description to the major's description and its relation to the minor's essence. This lesson unpacks that condition and shows how it ensures the conclusion follows.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two relations
Focus on the two relations that must be incompatible: (1) the relation of the description of the middle to the description of the major (from the major premise), and (2) the relation of the description of the middle to the essence of the minor (from the minor premise). Both premises predicate the middle, so these relations are defined by the predicates of each premise.
2
State the incompatibility condition
The two relations must be qualified such that they cannot both be true if the two extremes (the minor's essence and the major's description) were hypothetically unified. This ensures that the premises cannot both be true without the conclusion following.
The author uses the term 'munāfāh' to describe this logical opposition.
3
Check the existence case
Consider when the minor premise is perpetual (e.g., necessary or perpetual) and the major is any modalized proposition except the possible. Then the relation to the minor's essence is perpetual affirmation, while the relation to the major's description is actual negation (since the absolute general indicates actual negation). These are incompatible. Also consider when the major's negative converts.
The author also handles the case where the major's negative converts and the minor is not possible, leading to necessary/perpetual affirmation versus actual negation.
4
Check the non-existence case
When the minor is not perpetual and the major's negative does not convert, the specific modalities on both sides are such that incompatibility does not necessarily hold. For example, if the minor is possible and the major is temporal, there is no incompatibility. The author gives several modality combinations to show when incompatibility is absent.
This shows that the incompatibility condition is necessary and sufficient for productivity given the other two conditions.
5
Apply the condition to a real syllogism
Given a second figure categorical syllogism from Islamic sciences, determine the modalities of the premises (e.g., perpetual, necessary, possible, absolute). Check whether the two relations are incompatible according to the rules discussed. If they are incompatible, the syllogism is productive; otherwise, it is not.
For example, 'All prohibited things are harmful; no beneficial thing is harmful; therefore, no beneficial thing is prohibited.' Here, if the minor 'beneficial' is taken as perpetual and the major 'harmful' as absolute, the condition holds.

The major is subject in it with difference in quality and with incompatibility of the relation of the description of the middle to the description of the major for its relation to the essence of the minor. The major is subject in it with difference in quality, and that is as in all moods of the second figure and as in the third, fourth, fifth, and sixth moods of the fourth figure. The third and fourth moods of it have included both matters (112). Hence we interpreted the first disjunction as preventing emptiness (113). All conditions of the first and third figures have been indicated with respect to quantity, quality, and modality (114), and the conditions of the second and fourth figures with respect to quantity and quality. And the conditions of the second figure with respect to modality remained (115). So he pointed to them with his saying: 'with incompatibility, etc.' His saying 'with incompatibility' means that the productive syllogism containing the second matter, namely the generality of the subjecthood of the major with difference in quality, when the middle (116) is relative and predicated in both its premises as in the second figure, then in its production a third condition is necessary: the incompatibility of the relation of the description of the middle predicated in the minor (117) to the description of the major subject in the major, with the relation (118) of the description of the middle similarly predicated (119) to the essence of the minor subject in the minor. That is, the two mentioned relations must be qualified with two qualities such that the conjunction of these two relations in truth is impossible if their two extremes were hypothetically unified (120). And this incompatibility circulates in existence and non-existence with what passed of the two conditions of the second figure with respect to modality. By its realization, production is realized; by its negation, production is negated. As for its circulation with the two conditions in existence: i.e., whenever the two mentioned conditions exist, the mentioned incompatibility is realized. Because if the minor is of that to which perpetuity applies, and the major — whatever proposition it is from the modalized except the two possible propositions — for they have a separate ruling to come, then there is no doubt that then the relation of the description of the middle to the essence of the minor will be with perpetual affirmation, for example (121), and at least the relation of the description of the middle to the description of the major will be with actual negation, necessarily, because the absolute general is more general than those majors (122), and the absolute general indicates the negation of the middle from the essence of the major actually, and if it is negated from the major actually, it is negated from its description actually certainly (123). And there is no concealment of the incompatibility between perpetual affirmation and actual negation, and when incompatibility is realized between a thing and the more general, incompatibility necessarily follows between it and the more specific (124). Similarly, if the major is such that its negative converts (125). [Page 96: In the rule of conditions of the four figures: its incompatibility] And the minor is any proposition except the possible due to what passed (126), for then the relation of the description of the middle to the description of the major is with necessary affirmation (127) for example, or its perpetuity, and there is no concealment in the relation of the description of the middle to the essence of the minor with actual negation or more specific than it. Similarly, if the minor is possible (128) and the major is necessary or conditional (129), for then the relation of the description of the middle to the essence of the minor is with possible affirmation (130) for example, and the relation of the description of the middle to the description of the major with necessary negation. As for the conditional major (131), it is apparent; as for the necessary, because if the predicate is necessary for the essence as long as it exists (132), it is necessary for its attributive description, because the essence is necessary for the description and the predicate is necessary for the essence, and the necessary of the necessary is necessary. Similarly, if the major is possible and the minor is necessary (133), similar to what passed. And as for its circulation with the two conditions in non-existence: i.e., whenever one of the two mentioned conditions is negated, the mentioned incompatibility is not realized. Because if the minor is not of that to which perpetuity applies (134) nor the major such that its negative converts, then the most specific in the minors is not more than the particular conditional (135) nor in the majors more than the temporal, and there is no incompatibility between necessary affirmation (136) for example, in relation to the description not perpetually, and necessary negation at a specific time not perpetually, for perhaps that time is not the times of the attributive description. And when incompatibility is removed between the two specific things, it is removed between what is more general than them necessarily. Similarly, (137) if the major is not necessary nor conditional while the minor is possible, the most specific of the majors is the perpetual or the particular customary or the temporal (138), and there is no incompatibility between possible affirmation and perpetual negation as long as the essence exists, nor between it and perpetual negation with respect to the description not perpetually, nor between it and necessary negation at a specific time not perpetually. Similarly, if the minor is not necessary on the supposition that the major is possible, the most specific of the minors is the particular conditional or the perpetual (139), and there is no incompatibility between possible affirmation and necessary negation with respect to the description not perpetually, nor between it and perpetual negation as long as the essence exists, certainly. And the verification of this research in this excellent manner is something I alone have accomplished with the help of Allah, the Majestic. And Allah guides whom He wills to the straight path, and He is sufficient for me and the best guardian. (140)
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary from the Yazdi Hashiya on the conditions for productive syllogisms in the second figure. The author first reviews that the major term must be the subject in the second figure with a difference in quality between the premises, and that this condition applies to all moods of the second figure and some moods of the fourth. He then notes that conditions for the first and third figures have been fully discussed, while for the second and fourth only quantity and quality conditions have been given, leaving modality conditions for the second figure to be addressed. The third condition for the second figure is introduced via the phrase 'with incompatibility' from the base text. The author explains that when the middle term is predicated in both premises (as in the second figure), there must be an incompatibility between the relation of the middle's description to the major's description and the middle's description to the minor's essence. This incompatibility prevents the conjunction of the two relations. He then analyses the existence and non-existence of this incompatibility with respect to the earlier modality conditions. He shows that if the minor is perpetual and the major is any modalized proposition except the possible, then the relation of the middle to the minor's essence is perpetual affirmation, and the relation to the major's description is actual negation, which are incompatible. Similarly, if the major's negative converts and the minor is not possible, then the relation to the major's description is necessary or perpetual affirmation, and to the minor's essence is actual negation. He also treats cases where the minor is possible and the major necessary or conditional, leading to possible affirmation and necessary negation. For non-existence, he argues that if the minor is not perpetual nor the major such that its negative converts, then the most specific modalities on both sides are such that incompatibility does not arise. He gives several examples with different modalities to show that when the conditions are not met, the necessary incompatibility is absent. This rigorous treatment is claimed as the author's unique contribution.

Checkpoint
In the second figure, what is the third condition besides the major being subject and the premises differing in quality?
A) The middle term must be subject in both premises.
B) There must be incompatibility between the relation of the middle's description to the major's description and its relation to the minor's essence.
C) The major must be possible.
D) The minor must be necessary.

Key Takeaway
The third condition for a productive second figure syllogism is the incompatibility of the middle's relation to the major's description and to the minor's essence, which must hold given the modalities of the premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a second figure categorical syllogism using Islamic terms (e.g., from fiqh or kalam) where the minor premise is a perpetual proposition (e.g., 'Every prophet is truthful') and the major premise is an absolute general proposition (e.g., 'No liar is truthful'). Verify that the incompatibility condition holds by checking that the relation of the middle to the major's description and to the minor's essence are contradictory.

The Rule for Avoiding Contradiction: Minor Premise Cannot Be Possible
Author establishes the rule for avoiding contradiction with modal premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a pair of modal premises (necessary, possible, conditional) avoids contradiction in a categorical syllogism, using the rule that the minor premise cannot be possible unless the major is necessary or conditional.

Core Idea
The minor premise cannot be possible unless the major is necessary or conditional—otherwise the syllogism fails.

In this passage, the author refines the standard conditions for avoiding contradiction (munāfāt) between premises in categorical syllogisms. He states that the minor premise can be any proposition except the possible, unless the major is necessary or conditional. This rule is crucial for ensuring that the relation between terms yields a valid conclusion. We will unpack the rule and see how it applies to modal syllogisms used in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule stated at the beginning
The author says: 'The minor premise is any proposition except the possible, as before.' This is the core rule. Understand that the possible proposition (al-mumkina) is one that is neither necessary nor impossible, like 'Some humans are writing possibly.' The rule excludes it as a minor premise in most cases.
The exception is when the major is necessary or conditional.
2
Understand why the possible minor is problematic
If the minor were possible, the relation of the middle term's description to the major term's description would be uncertain. For a valid conclusion, we need a firm relation—necessity or perpetuity—between descriptions.
The author refers to 'relation of the middle term's description to the major term's description by necessity of affirmation.'
3
Apply the exception: possible minor with necessary/conditional major
When the minor is possible and the major is necessary or conditional, contradiction is avoided. In this case, the middle's description relates to the subject's essence by possibility of affirmation, and to the major's description by necessity of negation.
Necessity of negation arises because the necessary predicate is binding on the description.
4
Check the two conditions for contradiction
The author explains that contradiction depends on two conditions: the minor must be such that perpetuity applies, and the major must be such that its negative converts. If either condition is absent, no contradiction occurs. Practice identifying these conditions in sample syllogisms.
For example, if the minor is 'some humans are writing' (particular possible), perpetuity does not apply.

Source Text

في ضابطة شرايط الاشكال الاربعة منافاته والصغرى اى قضية كانت سوى الممكنة لمامر (۱۲۶) اذ حينئذ يكون نسبة وصف الاوسط الى وصف الاكبر بضرورة الايجاب (۱۲۷) مثلا او بدوامه ولاخفاء في مع نسبة وصف الاوسط الى ذات الاصغر بفعلية السلب او اخص منها . و كذا اذا كانت الصغرى ممكنة (۱۲۸) والكبرى ضرورية او مشروطة (۱۲۹) اذ حينئذ يكون نسبة وصف الاوسط الى ذات الاصغر بأمكان الايجاب (۱۳۰) مثلاً، و نسبة وصف الاوسط الى وصف الاكبر بضرورة السلب، اما فى الكبرى المشروطة (۱۳۱) فظاهر و اما فى الضرورية، فلان المحمول اذا كان ضرورياً للذات ما دامت موجودة (۱۳۲) كان ضرورياً لوصفها العنوانى لان الذات لازمة للوصف والمحمول لازم للذات و لازم اللازم لازم و كذا اذا كانت الكبرى ممكنة والصغرى ضرورية (۱۳۳) بمثل مامر. و اما انها دائرة مع الشرطين عدماً اى كلما انتفى احد الشرطين المذكورين لم يتحقق المنافاة المذكورة، فلانه اذا لم يكن الصغرى مما يصدق عليه الدوام (۱۳۴) ولا الكبرى مما تنعكس سالبتها ، لم يكن فى الصغريات اخص من المشروطة الخاصة (۱۳۵) ولا فى الكبريات اخص من الوقتية، ولا منافاة بين ضرورة الايجاب (۱۳۶) مثلاً بحسب الوصف لا دائماً وبين ضرورة السلب فى وقت معين لا دائماً اذ لعل ذلك الوقت غير اوقات الوصف العنوانى و اذا ارتفعت المنافاة بين الاخصين ارتفعت بين ما هو اعم منهما ضرورة و كذا (۱۳۷) اذا لم يكن الكبرى ضرورية ولا مشروطة حين كون الصغرى ممكنة كان اخص الكبريات الدائمة او العرفية الخاصة او الوقتية (۱۳۸) ولا منافاة بين امكان الايجاب و دوام السلب مادام الذات ولا بينه و بين دوام السلب بحسب الوصف لا دائماً ولا بينه و بين ضرورة السلب فى وقت معين لا دائماً و كذا اذا لم يكن الصغرى ضرورية على تقدير كون الكبرى ممكنة، كان اخص الصغريات المشروطة الخاصة او الدائمة (۱۳۹) ولا منافاة بين امكان الايجاب و بين ضرورة السلب بحسب الوصف لا دائماً ولابينه و بين دوام السلب مادام الذات قطعاً. و تحقيق هذا البحث على هذا الوجه الوجيه مما تفردت به بعون الله الجليل و الله يهدي من يشاء الى سواء السبيل و هو حسبي ونعم الوكيل . (۱۴۰)
On the rule of conditions for the four figures: the lack of contradiction. The minor premise is any proposition except the possible, as before (126), because then the relation of the middle term's description to the major term's description is by necessity of affirmation (127) for example, or by its perpetuity, and there is no concealment in the relation of the middle term's description to the subject's essence by actual negation or more specific than it. Similarly, if the minor is possible (128) and the major is necessary or conditional (129), then the relation of the middle term's description to the subject's essence is by possibility of affirmation (130) for example, and the relation of the middle term's description to the major term's description is by necessity of negation. As for the conditional major (131), it is obvious; as for the necessary, because the predicate, if necessary for the essence as long as it exists (132), is necessary for its attributive description, because the essence is necessary for the description and the predicate is necessary for the essence, and the necessary of the necessary is necessary. Likewise, if the major is possible and the minor is necessary (133), by similar reasoning. And as for it being contingent upon the two conditions being negated, i.e., whenever one of the two mentioned conditions is negated, the mentioned contradiction does not occur, because if the minor is not of the type to which perpetuity applies (134) and the major is not of the type whose negative converts, then there is no minor more specific than the particular conditional (135) and no major more specific than the temporal, and there is no contradiction between the necessity of affirmation (136) for example by description not perpetually and the necessity of negation at a specific time not perpetually, because perhaps that time is not the times of the attributive description. And if the contradiction is removed between the two more specific ones, it is necessarily removed between what is more general than them. Likewise (137), if the major is neither necessary nor conditional when the minor is possible, then the most specific of majors are the perpetual or the particular conventional or the temporal (138), and there is no contradiction between the possibility of affirmation and the perpetuity of negation as long as the essence exists, nor between it and the perpetuity of negation by description not perpetually, nor between it and the necessity of negation at a specific time not perpetually. Similarly, if the minor is not necessary on the assumption of the major being possible, then the most specific of minors are the particular conditional or the perpetual (139), and there is no contradiction between the possibility of affirmation and the necessity of negation by description not perpetually, nor between it and the perpetuity of negation as long as the essence exists, absolutely. And the verification of this research in this excellent manner is something I uniquely accomplished with the help of Allah the Majestic, and Allah guides whom He wills to the straight path, and He is sufficient for me and the best trustee. (140)
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a detailed investigation into the conditions under which contradiction (manafaat) between premises in syllogistic figures is avoided. The author, in his Hashiya, refines the standard account of modal conditions. He begins by stating the rule: the minor premise can be any proposition except the possible (mumkina). This is because if the minor were possible, the required relation between the middle term's description and the major term's description would be problematic. He then examines specific cases: first, when the minor is possible and the major is necessary or conditional (shartiyya). In that case, the middle's description relates to the subject's essence by possibility of affirmation, and to the major's description by necessity of negation. He justifies the necessity of negation in the major: if the predicate is necessary for the essence as long as it exists, it is also necessary for the attributive description (wasf al-'unwani) because the essence is inseparable from the description, and necessity transfers. Next, if the major is possible and the minor is necessary, the reasoning is analogous. The author then explains that contradiction is contingent on the negation of two conditions: the minor must be such that perpetuity (dawam) applies to it, and the major must be such that its negative converts (i.e., the contradictory of the major is something like a particular proposition). By analyzing the most specific cases (e.g., particular conditional, temporal, perpetual), he shows that when the two conditions are absent, the contradiction does not arise. For instance, there is no contradiction between a necessary affirmation by description not perpetually (like 'every human is white by description not always') and a necessary negation at a specific time, because the time of the description may differ. The author concludes that this verification is his unique contribution. The entire discussion assumes the standard Aristotelian modal categories (necessary, possible, perpetual, temporal) and applies them to the relation of terms in the figures.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Modal Transfer: From Essence to Attributive Description
Author explains why necessity transfers from essence to attributive description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a predicate that is necessary for an essence is also necessary for its attributive description (waṣf al-‘unwānī), using the principle that the essence is inseparable from the description.

Core Idea
Necessity transfers from essence to description because the essence is inseparable from its description.

In the previous lesson, we saw that when the major premise is necessary and the minor is possible, the middle term's description relates to the major's description by necessity of negation. The author justifies this by a modal transfer argument: if the predicate is necessary for the essence, it is necessary for the attributive description of that essence. This principle is essential for understanding how modal properties propagate in syllogisms. Let's analyze this transfer step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The author states: 'the predicate, if necessary for the essence as long as it exists, is necessary for its attributive description.' This is the claim we need to unpack.
The predicate is something like 'created' or 'mortal'; the essence is the subject itself; the attributive description is a property that designates the subject (e.g., 'human' as a description).
2
Understand the chain of necessity
The author gives a chain: the essence is necessary for the description (the description depends on the essence), and the predicate is necessary for the essence. Therefore, the predicate is necessary for the description, because 'the necessary of the necessary is necessary' (lāzim al-lāzim lāzim).
This is a logical principle: if A is necessary for B, and B is necessary for C, then A is necessary for C.
3
Apply to a concrete Islamic example
Consider the essence 'human' and the attributive description 'the one who walks on two legs' (a typical description). If the predicate 'mortal' is necessary for the essence 'human' (i.e., all humans are mortal necessarily), then 'mortal' is also necessary for the description 'the one who walks on two legs', because 'human' is necessary for that description. So whenever something is described as 'the one who walks on two legs', it must be mortal.
This justifies the 'necessity of negation' in the earlier rule: the middle's description negates the major's description necessarily.
4
Generalize the principle
The principle is not limited to this specific chain. It shows why modal transfer works in any syllogism where the major premise is necessary. It also applies when the major is conditional (mashrūṭa), as the author notes, though the reasoning is similar.
The author says 'as for the conditional major, it is obvious' because conditionality already builds in the dependency.

The Five Types of Conditional Syllogism
Enumeration of five types with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the five types of conditional syllogism from their premises and conclusion patterns.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms combine conditionals, disjunctives, and categoricals in five distinct ways.

The author classifies conditional syllogisms into five types based on the kinds of premises used. This taxonomy is essential for recognizing valid argument forms in Islamic sciences, where conditional reasoning appears in usul al-fiqh (e.g., conditional rulings) and kalam (e.g., hypothetical arguments). Let's walk through each type.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Look at the two premises of the syllogism. Are they both conditionals (muttasila), both disjunctives (munfasila), or a mix with a categorical (hamliyya)? The author gives five combinations.
Use the Arabic terms: muttasila = conditional, munfasila = disjunctive, hamliyya = categorical.
2
Check the conclusion pattern
The conclusion mirrors the type: two conditionals yield a conditional, two disjunctives yield a disjunctive, categorical+conditional yields a categorical, categorical+disjunctive yields a disjunctive, conditional+disjunctive yields a conditional with a disjunctive consequent.
3
Apply to Islamic examples
For instance, in usul al-fiqh: 'If the debtor is mature, then his contract is valid; if his contract is valid, then the sale is binding' yields 'If the debtor is mature, the sale is binding.' This is a conditional syllogism from two conditionals.
Think of other Islamic examples: rulings conditional on purity, prayer times, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter: The conditional syllogism among the conjunctive [types] is either composed of two conditionals, or two disjunctives, or a categorical and a conditional, or a categorical and a disjunctive, or a conditional and a disjunctive. And [the syllogism] is concluded. The conditional syllogism: His statement 'from two conditionals' like our saying: 'Whenever the sun is shining, the daytime exists, and whenever the daytime exists, the world is illuminated' yields: 'Whenever the sun is shining, the world is illuminated.' His statement 'or two disjunctives' like our saying: 'Either the number is even or it is odd, and always either the even is the double of an even or the double of an odd' yields: 'Either the number is the double of an even, or the double of an odd, or it is odd.' His statement 'or a categorical and a conditional' like: 'This thing is a human, and whenever a thing is a human, it is an animal' yields: 'This thing is an animal.' His statement 'or a categorical and a disjunctive' like: 'This is a number, and always either the number is even or it is odd' yields: 'This is either even or odd.' His statement 'or a conditional and a disjunctive' like: 'Whenever this thing is three, it is a number, and always either the number is even or odd' yields: 'Whenever this thing is three, it is either even or odd.' His statement 'and it is concluded' means that in these sections there must be commonality between the two premises (141) (5) in that the categorical is minor and the conditional is major, as in the example mentioned by the commentator (al-muhashshi), or the reverse, like our saying: 'Whenever a thing is rational, it is human, and every human is an animal' yields: 'Whenever a thing is rational, it is an animal.' (Muhammad Ali) (5) In that the categorical is minor and the disjunctive is major, as in the example mentioned by the commentator, or the reverse, like our saying: 'Either the number is even or odd, and every even is divisible into two equal parts' yields: 'Either the number is divisible into two equal parts or it is odd.' (Muhammad Ali) () And for this also there are two forms: one is that the conditional is minor and the disjunctive is major, as in the example mentioned by the commentator, and the second is the reverse of this, like our saying: 'Either the number is even or it is odd, and whenever the number is even, it is divisible into two equal parts' yields: 'Either the number is divisible into two equal parts or it is odd.' (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a classification and exemplification of types of conditional syllogism (al-qiyas al-sharti). The author defines five possible compositions: (1) from two conditionals (muttasilatayn), (2) from two disjunctives (munfasilatayn), (3) from a categorical and a conditional, (4) from a categorical and a disjunctive, and (5) from a conditional and a disjunctive. For each, an example is given. Then the author explains that for the syllogism to conclude, the two premises must share something. In the footnotes by Muhammad Ali, he elaborates that in the cases mixing categorical and conditional/disjunctive, there are two possible orders: the categorical can be minor and the conditional/disjunctive major, or vice versa. For each permutation, an example is provided. For instance, a categorical as minor and conditional as major: 'This thing is a human, and whenever a thing is human, it is an animal; therefore, this thing is an animal.' The reverse (conditional as minor, categorical as major): 'Whenever a thing is rational, it is human; and every human is an animal; therefore, whenever a thing is rational, it is an animal.' Similarly for categorical and disjunctive, and for conditional and disjunctive. The examples use familiar logical concepts: sun and daytime, numbers being even or odd, humans and animals. The passage is purely expository, providing the standard typology of compound syllogisms in the Aristotelian tradition as absorbed by Islamic logicians.

Order of Premises in Mixed Conditional Syllogisms
Commentary on premise order permutations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to rearrange the order of premises (minor/major) in mixed syllogisms to produce valid conclusions.

Core Idea
In mixed syllogisms, the categorical premise can be either minor or major, and the conditional/disjunctive takes the other role.

The commentator Muhammad Ali elaborates on the base text by showing that the categorical premise in mixed syllogisms can be placed as the minor premise or as the major premise, with the conditional or disjunctive taking the opposite role. This flexibility shows the robustness of the syllogistic form.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the mixed type
Identify which of the five types you have: categorical+conditional, categorical+disjunctive, or conditional+disjunctive. These are the mixed types.
2
Identify the premise roles
In the base text, the categorical is given as minor and the conditional as major. But the commentator shows the reverse is also valid: the conditional can be minor and the categorical major.
The key condition is that the premises share a term (yan'aqid).
3
Construct the reverse order
For example, categorical+conditional: the base example uses categorical minor 'This thing is a human' and conditional major 'Whenever a thing is human, it is animal'. The reverse would be conditional minor 'Whenever a thing is rational, it is human' and categorical major 'Every human is an animal'. The conclusion changes accordingly.
Practice this with a fiqh example: conditional minor 'If someone is in ihram, he cannot hunt', categorical major 'Everyone in ihram is a pilgrim', conclusion 'If someone is a pilgrim, he cannot hunt'? Actually need to check consistency.

(محمد على) (٥) بان تكون الحملية صغرى والمنفصلة كبرى كالمثال الذي ذكره المحشى او يكون بعكس ذلك كقولنا : اما ان يكون العدد زوجاً او فرداً و كل زوج منقسم بمتساويين فاما ان يكون العدد منقسماً بمتساو بين او فرداً. (محمد علی) () و لهذا ايضاً صورتان احديهها ان تكون المتصلة صغرى والمنفصلة كبرى كالمثال الذي ذكره المحشى و الثانية عكس هذا كقولنا اما ان يكون العدد زوجاً و اما ان يكون فرداً و كلما كان العدد زوجاً كان منقسماً بمتساويين ينتج: اما ان يكون العدد منقسماً بمتساويين واما ان يكون فرداً. Chapter: The conditional syllogism among the conjunctive [types] is either composed of two conditionals, or two disjunctives, or a categorical and a conditional, or a categorical and a disjunctive, or a conditional and a disjunctive. And [the syllogism] is concluded. The conditional syllogism: His statement 'from two conditionals' like our saying: 'Whenever the sun is shining, the daytime exists, and whenever the daytime exists, the world is illuminated' yields: 'Whenever the sun is shining, the world is illuminated.' His statement 'or two disjunctives' like our saying: 'Either the number is even or it is odd, and always either the even is the double of an even or the double of an odd' yields: 'Either the number is the double of an even, or the double of an odd, or it is odd.' His statement 'or a categorical and a conditional' like: 'This thing is a human, and whenever a thing is a human, it is an animal' yields: 'This thing is an animal.' His statement 'or a categorical and a disjunctive' like: 'This is a number, and always either the number is even or it is odd' yields: 'This is either even or odd.' His statement 'or a conditional and a disjunctive' like: 'Whenever this thing is three, it is a number, and always either the number is even or odd' yields: 'Whenever this thing is three, it is either even or odd.' His statement 'and it is concluded' means that in these sections there must be commonality between the two premises (141) (5) in that the categorical is minor and the conditional is major, as in the example mentioned by the commentator (al-muhashshi), or the reverse, like our saying: 'Whenever a thing is rational, it is human, and every human is an animal' yields: 'Whenever a thing is rational, it is an animal.' (Muhammad Ali) (5) In that the categorical is minor and the disjunctive is major, as in the example mentioned by the commentator, or the reverse, like our saying: 'Either the number is even or odd, and every even is divisible into two equal parts' yields: 'Either the number is divisible into two equal parts or it is odd.' (Muhammad Ali) () And for this also there are two forms: one is that the conditional is minor and the disjunctive is major, as in the example mentioned by the commentator, and the second is the reverse of this, like our saying: 'Either the number is even or it is odd, and whenever the number is even, it is divisible into two equal parts' yields: 'Either the number is divisible into two equal parts or it is odd.' (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a classification and exemplification of types of conditional syllogism (al-qiyas al-sharti). The author defines five possible compositions: (1) from two conditionals (muttasilatayn), (2) from two disjunctives (munfasilatayn), (3) from a categorical and a conditional, (4) from a categorical and a disjunctive, and (5) from a conditional and a disjunctive. For each, an example is given. Then the author explains that for the syllogism to conclude, the two premises must share something. In the footnotes by Muhammad Ali, he elaborates that in the cases mixing categorical and conditional/disjunctive, there are two possible orders: the categorical can be minor and the conditional/disjunctive major, or vice versa. For each permutation, an example is provided. For instance, a categorical as minor and conditional as major: 'This thing is a human, and whenever a thing is human, it is an animal; therefore, this thing is an animal.' The reverse (conditional as minor, categorical as major): 'Whenever a thing is rational, it is human; and every human is an animal; therefore, whenever a thing is rational, it is an animal.' Similarly for categorical and disjunctive, and for conditional and disjunctive. The examples use familiar logical concepts: sun and daytime, numbers being even or odd, humans and animals. The passage is purely expository, providing the standard typology of compound syllogisms in the Aristotelian tradition as absorbed by Islamic logicians.

صغرى
كبرى
حملية
متصلة
منفصلة

Key Takeaway
Mixed conditional syllogisms allow two valid orderings of premises, giving flexibility in argument construction.

Five Types of Conditional Conjunctive Syllogisms
Author enumerates five types of conditional conjunctive syllogisms and provides examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify the five types of conditional conjunctive syllogisms based on the composition of their premises.

Core Idea
Conditional conjunctive syllogisms have five possible premise compositions: two conditionals, two disjunctives, or mixed.

The author of this text expands on the types of conditional conjunctive syllogisms (al-sharti min al-iqtirani). He lists five possible combinations of premises and gives concrete examples for each. Understanding these types will help you recognize the structure of arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence that employ conditional reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five compositions
List the five types of premise combinations mentioned by the author: two conditionals, two disjunctives, categorical+conditional, categorical+disjunctive, conditional+disjunctive.
The author uses Arabic terms: متصلتين (two conditionals), منفصلتين (two disjunctives), etc.
2
Examine each example
For each type, read the example given and note the form of the premises and how the conclusion follows. For instance, the example of two conditionals uses the sun-daytime relationship.
Pay attention to the words 'كلما' (whenever) and 'اما' (either) which signal conditional and disjunctive propositions.
3
Classify the conclusion type
Determine whether the conclusion is a conditional (with 'كلما'), a disjunctive (with 'اما'), or a categorical (simple 'هذا'). Compare this to the premise types.
The conclusion type matches the dominant premise type in the composition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Section: The conditional conjunctive syllogism is either composed of two conditionals, or two disjunctives, or a categorical and a conditional, or a categorical and a disjunctive, or a conditional and a disjunctive. And it is established. The conditional syllogism: His saying 'of two conditionals' as our saying: Whenever the sun is shining, daytime exists, and whenever daytime exists, the world is illuminated, yields: Whenever the sun is shining, the world is illuminated. His saying 'or of two disjunctives' as our saying: Either the number is even or it is odd, and always either the even is the even of an even or the even of an odd, yields: Either the number is the even of an even, or the even of an odd, or it is odd. His saying 'or a categorical and a conditional' like: This thing is a human, and whenever something is a human it is an animal, yields: This thing is an animal. His saying 'or a categorical and a disjunctive' like: This is a number, and always either the number is even or odd, yields: So this is either even or odd [unclear phrase]. His saying 'or a conditional and a disjunctive' like: Whenever this thing is three, it is a number, and always either the number is even or odd, yields: Whenever this thing is three, then it is either even or odd. His saying 'and it is established' means it is necessary in those divisions that the two premises share a part which is the middle term. So either it is predicated in both premises, or it is subject in both, or it is subject in the minor and predicated in the major, or vice versa. The first is the third figure, the second is the second, the third is the first, and the fourth is the fourth. And detailing the four figures in those five divisions according to the conditions, moods, and conclusions is lengthy, not suitable for summaries, so let it be sought from the detailed works of the later scholars.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the types of conditional conjunctive syllogisms (al-sharti min al-iqtirani). The author first enumerates five possible compositions of the two premises: both conditionals (mutasilataan), both disjunctives (munfasilataan), a categorical with a conditional, a categorical with a disjunctive, and a conditional with a disjunctive. For each, he provides an example drawn from natural or mathematical phenomena, such as the relation of sunlight to daytime and the even-odd classification of numbers. These examples illustrate how the conclusion follows the form of the premises. After listing the types, he emphasizes the crucial condition that the two premises must share a common part—the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat)—which may function as subject or predicate in different positions, giving rise to the four figures (ashkal). The first figure corresponds to the middle term being subject in the minor and predicate in the major, the second to being predicate in both, the third to being subject in both, and the fourth to being predicate in the minor and subject in the major. The author then notes that a full exposition of the four figures for these five types, including their conditions and valid moods, would be too lengthy for a concise work, so he refers the reader to more comprehensive treatments by later scholars. Throughout, the focus is on the structural necessity of a middle term, mirroring the requirement in categorical syllogisms.

الحد الاوسط

Key Takeaway
Conditional conjunctive syllogisms are classified by the types of their two premises into five standard compositions, each with a corresponding conclusion form.

The Middle Term in Conditional Conjunctive Syllogisms
Author explains the necessity of a middle term and the four figures in conditional conjunctive syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the necessity of a shared middle term in conditional conjunctive syllogisms and identify the four figures based on its position in the premises.

Core Idea
A shared middle term is essential for all conditional conjunctive syllogisms, just as in categorical ones.

After listing the five types, the author emphasizes a crucial condition: the two premises must share a common part—the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat). He then explains how the position of this middle term determines the figure (shakl) of the syllogism, mirroring the classification in categorical logic.

Guided Path
1
Locate the key condition
Find the sentence where the author states that it is necessary for the premises to share a part. In the Arabic, look for 'ينعقد' meaning 'it is established' and the phrase 'لابد فى تلك الاقسام من اشتراك المقدمتين فى جزء'.
This is the crucial passage that extends the concept of middle term from categorical to conditional syllogisms.
2
Understand the four positions of the middle term
List the four possibilities: (1) middle term is predicate in both premises, (2) subject in both, (3) subject in minor and predicate in major, (4) predicate in minor and subject in major.
The author uses 'محكوماً عليه' (subject) and 'محكوماً به' (predicate).
3
Match positions to figures
According to the author, which figure corresponds to which position? The first figure has middle as subject in minor and predicate in major; second figure has middle as predicate in both; third figure has middle as subject in both; fourth figure has middle as predicate in minor and subject in major.
This matches the classical ordering in categorical syllogistics.

The Exceptive Syllogism: Definition and Valid Inference Patterns
Author defines exceptive syllogism and explains valid inference patterns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify valid inference patterns in an exceptive syllogism (both conditional and disjunctive) and distinguish them from invalid ones.

Core Idea
Only specific forms of the exceptive syllogism yield certain conclusions.

This passage defines the exceptive syllogism and explains the precise conditions for valid inference. Mastering these rules is essential for evaluating arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh, where conditional and disjunctive reasoning is common.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure
Locate the conditional premise (shartiyya) and the categorical premise (hamliyya) that excepts one part or its contradictory.
The categorical premise is the 'istithna' (exception).
2
Check the type of conditional
Determine if it is implicative (mutasila luzumiyya) or disjunctive (munfasila 'anadiyya). This decides which patterns are valid.
The author specifies that only exclusive disjunctives are meant.
3
Apply implicative rules
For implicative, only affirming antecedent and denying consequent are valid. The other two are invalid because the consequent may be more general.
Example: 'If it is a human, it is an animal.' Affirm human to get animal; deny animal to get not human.
4
Apply disjunctive rules
For disjunctive, identify the type: man'at al-jam' (mutually exclusive), man'at al-khuluw (exhaustive), or haqiqiyya (both). Each has different valid forms.
Man'at al-jam' only yields from affirming one to denying the other; man'at al-khuluw does the opposite; haqiqiyya yields all four.
5
Test with an Islamic example
Take a kalam proof: 'If the world were eternal, it would not require a cause; but it requires a cause; therefore it is not eternal.' This is valid modus tollens (denying consequent).
Ensure the example matches the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القياس الاستثنائى قوله «الاستثنائى» اى القياس الاستثنائى و هو الذى تكون النتيجة مذكورة فيه بمادته و هيئته ابداً يتركب من مقدمة شرطية و مقدمة حملية يستثنى فيها عين احد جزئى الشرطية او نقيضه لينتج عين الآخر او نقيضه فالاحتمالات المتصورة فى انتاج كل استثنائى اربعة : وضع كل و رفع كل لكن المنتج فى كل قسم شيء. وتفصيله ما افاده المصنف: من ان الشرطية ان كانت متصلة ينتج منها احتمالان لان وضع المقدم ينتج وضع التالى لاستلزام تحقق الملزوم تحقق اللازم ورفع التالى ينتج رفع المقدم لاستلزام انتفاء اللازم انتفاء الملزوم واما وضع التالى فلا ينتج وضع المقدم ولا رفع المقدم ينتج رفع التالي الجوازان يكون اللازم اعم فلا يلزم من تحققه تحقق الملزوم ولامن انتفاء الملزوم انتفائه . وقد عرفت من هذا ان المراد بالمتصلة فى هذا الباب اللزومية واعلم ايضاً: ان المراد بالمنفصلة هيهنا العنادية و ان كانت الشرطية منفصلة. فمانعة الجمع تنتج من وضع كل جزء رفع الاخر لامتناع اجتماعهما ولا تنتج من رفع كل جزء وضع الآخر لعدم امتناع الخلو بينها ومانعة الخلو بالعكس و اما الحقيقية فلما اشتملت على منع الجمع و الخلومعاً تنتج فى الصور الاربع النتائج الاربع. قوله «وضع المقدم و رفع التالى»: نحو: ان كان هذا انساناً كان حيواناً لكنه انسان فهو حيوان لكنه ليس بحيوان فهو ليس بانسان قوله « والحقيقية»: كقولنا : اما ان يكون هذا العدد زوجاً او فرداً لكنه زوج
The exceptive syllogism. His saying 'the exceptive' means the exceptive syllogism, which is that in which the conclusion is mentioned in its matter and form always. It is composed of a conditional premise and a categorical premise in which one of the two parts of the conditional or its contradictory is excepted, so as to yield the other part or its contradictory. The conceivable possibilities in producing every exceptive are four: affirming both and denying both, but what is produced in each division is a thing. Its detail is what the author stated: that if the conditional is implicative, two possibilities produce from it, because affirming the antecedent produces affirming the consequent due to the entailment that the realization of the entailer necessitates the realization of the entailed, and denying the consequent produces denying the antecedent because the non-existence of the entailed necessitates the non-existence of the entailer. But affirming the consequent does not produce affirming the antecedent, nor does denying the antecedent produce denying the consequent, because the entailed may be more general, so its realization does not necessitate the realization of the entailer, nor does the non-existence of the entailer necessitate its non-existence. You have known from this that what is meant by 'conditional' in this chapter is the implicative. And know also that what is meant by 'disjunctive' here is the exclusive. And if the conditional is disjunctive, then the mutually exclusive (mani'at al-jam') produces, from affirming each part, the denial of the other, due to the impossibility of their conjunction, and does not produce from denying each part the affirmation of the other, due to the possibility of their both being absent. The exhaustive but not exclusive (mani'at al-khuluw) is the opposite. As for the true disjunctive (haqiqiyya), since it combines both mutual exclusion and exhaustiveness, it produces, in the four forms, the four conclusions. His saying 'affirming the antecedent and denying the consequent' like: If this is a human, it is an animal; but it is a human, so it is an animal; but it is not an animal, so it is not a human. His saying 'and the true disjunctive' like our saying: Either this number is even or odd, but it is even [then it is not odd; the text cuts off].
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the exceptive syllogism (qiyas istithna'i), a form where the conclusion is explicitly contained in the premises. The author begins by defining it: it consists of a conditional premise (shartiyya) and a categorical premise (hamliyya) that excepts (istithna') either one part of the conditional or its contradictory, yielding the other part or its contradictory. He then states that there are four conceivable possibilities: affirming both parts, denying both parts, but only specific ones are productive. The author elaborates based on the original text: if the conditional is implicative (mutasila luzumiyya), two possibilities yield a valid conclusion: affirming the antecedent (to get the consequent) and denying the consequent (to get the antecedent). The other two (affirming consequent, denying antecedent) are invalid because the relation is not biconditional—the consequent may be wider in scope. He then turns to disjunctive conditionals (munfasila), specifying that here they mean exclusive disjunction (al-'anadiyya). He explains three types: man'at al-jam' (mutually exclusive but not exhaustive) yields conclusion only from affirming one part to the denial of the other; man'at al-khuluw (exhaustive but not mutually exclusive) does the opposite; and haqiqiyya (both exclusive and exhaustive) yields valid conclusions in all four forms. Finally, he gives examples: for implicative conditional, 'If it is human, it is animal'—affirming the antecedent or denying the consequent; for the true disjunctive, 'Either this number is even or odd' with affirmation of one part, but the example cuts off. The passage is careful to distinguish the types of conditionals and their corresponding valid inference patterns.
