The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Definition and Structure of Conditional Syllogism
Author defines conditional syllogism and lists the four conceivable possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a conditional syllogism and distinguish it from a categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
The conditional syllogism embeds its conclusion in its premises; extracting it requires correct exclusion.

The conditional syllogism (al-qiyās al-istithnā'ī) is a cornerstone of Islamic logic, used extensively in kalam and usul al-fiqh. This lesson unpacks its definition and structure, showing how a conditional premise and a categorical premise combine to produce a conclusion that is already implicit in the conditional.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
A conditional syllogism always has a conditional premise (sharṭiyya) and a categorical premise (ḥamliyya). The categorical premise excludes one part of the conditional or its contradictory.
The categorical premise is called the istithnā' (exception).
2
Locate the exception in the categorical premise
The categorical premise either affirms or denies one of the two parts of the conditional. This is the 'exclusion' that drives the inference.
3
Determine the conclusion
The conclusion follows as the other part or its contradictory, depending on the type of conditional and the exception used.
The four possibilities are: affirm antecedent, deny antecedent, affirm consequent, deny consequent.
4
Recognize that not all combinations are productive
Depending on the type of conditional (conjunctive or disjunctive), only specific combinations yield valid conclusions. The author details these in the following sections.
This lesson focuses on the general framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: الاستثنائى ينتج مع المتصلة وضع المقدم ورفع التالي ومع الحقيقية. قوله «الاستثنائى» اى القياس الاستثنائى و هو الذى تكون النتيجة مذكورة فيه بمادته و هيئته ابداً يتركب من مقدمة شرطية و مقدمة حملية يستثنى فيها عين احد جزئى الشرطية او نقيضه لينتج عين الآخر او نقيضه فالاحتمالات المتصورة فى انتاج كل استثنائى اربعة : وضع كل و رفع كل لكن المنتج فى كل قسم شيء. وتفصيله ما افاده المصنف: من ان الشرطية ان كانت متصلة ينتج منها احتمالان لان وضع المقدم ينتج وضع التالى لاستلزام تحقق الملزوم تحقق اللازم ورفع التالى ينتج رفع المقدم لاستلزام انتفاء اللازم انتفاء الملزوم واما وضع التالى فلا ينتج وضع المقدم ولا رفع المقدم ينتج رفع التالي الجوازان يكون اللازم اعم فلا يلزم من تحققه تحقق الملزوم ولامن انتفاء الملزوم انتفائه . وقد عرفت من .. :هذا ان المراد بالمتصلة فى هذا الباب اللزومية واعلم ايضاً: ان المراد بالمنفصلة هيهنا العنادية و ان كانت الشرطية منفصلة. فمانعة الجمع تنتج من وضع كل جزء رفع الاخر لامتناع اجتماعهما ولا تنتج من رفع كل جزء وضع الآخر لعدم امتناع الخلو بينها ومانعة الخلو بالعكس و اما الحقيقية فلما اشتملت على منع الجمع و الخلومعاً تنتج فى الصور الاربع النتائج الاربع. قوله «وضع المقدم و رفع التالى»: نحو: ان كان هذا انساناً كان حيواناً لكنه انسان فهو حيوان لكنه ليس بحيوان فهو ليس بانسان قوله « والحقيقية»: كقولنا : اما ان يكون هذا العدد زوجاً او فرداً لكنه زوج فليس بفرد لكنه فرد فليس بزوج لكنه ليس بفرد فهو زوج لكنه ليس بزوج فهو فرد. قوله « كمانعة الجمع»: نحو: اما ان يكون هذا شجراً او حجراً لكنه شجر فليس بحجر لكنه حجر فليس بشجر. قوله « كمانعة الخلو»: نحو هذا اما لاحجر او لا شجر لكنه ليس بلا شجر فهو لاحجر لكنه ليس بلا حجر فهولا شجر
Section: The conditional [syllogism] produces, with the conjunctive [conditional], the affirmation of the antecedent and the denial of the consequent, and with the real [disjunctive, all four combinations]. His statement: 'al-istithnā'ī' meaning the conditional syllogism, which is that in which the conclusion is mentioned in its matter and form always, composed of a conditional premise and a categorical premise in which is excluded exactly one of the two parts of the conditional or its contradictory, so as to produce exactly the other or its contradictory. The conceivable possibilities in the production of every conditional syllogism are four: the affirmation of each and the denial of each, but the productive in each division is something. Its detail is what the author expressed: that if the conditional is conjunctive, two possibilities produce from it, because affirming the antecedent produces affirming the consequent due to the implication that realization of the antecedent necessitates realization of the consequent, and denying the consequent produces denying the antecedent due to the implication that the non-existence of the consequent necessitates the non-existence of the antecedent. As for affirming the consequent, it does not produce affirming the antecedent, nor does denying the antecedent produce denying the consequent, because the consequent may be more general, so its realization does not necessitate the realization of the antecedent, nor does the denial of the antecedent necessitate its denial. And you have known from this that what is meant by 'conjunctive' in this chapter is the implicative. And know also that what is meant by 'disjunctive' here is the exclusive. And if the conditional is disjunctive, then the 'preventive of conjunction' produces from affirming each part the denial of the other, due to the impossibility of their conjunction, and does not produce from denying each part the affirmation of the other, due to the possibility of the void between them. And the 'preventive of void' is the opposite. As for the 'real', since it includes the prevention of both conjunction and void together, it produces in the four forms the four conclusions. His statement: 'the affirmation of the antecedent and the denial of the consequent': like: if this is a human, it is an animal; but it is a human, therefore it is an animal; but it is not an animal, therefore it is not a human. His statement: 'and the real': like our saying: either this number is even or odd; but it is even, therefore it is not odd; but it is odd, therefore it is not even; but it is not odd, therefore it is even; but it is not even, therefore it is odd. His statement: 'like the preventive of conjunction': like: either this is a tree or a stone; but it is a tree, therefore it is not a stone; but it is a stone, therefore it is not a tree. His statement: 'like the preventive of void': like: this is either not a stone or not a tree; but it is not 'not a tree', therefore it is 'not a stone'; but it is not 'not a stone', therefore it is 'not a tree'.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage defines the conditional syllogism (al-qiyās al-istithnā'ī) and explains its productive moods. The author clarifies that a conditional syllogism consists of a conditional premise (sharṭiyya) and a categorical premise (ḥamliyya) in which either one part of the conditional or its contradictory is excluded, yielding the other part or its contradictory. There are four possible combinations: affirming the antecedent, affirming the consequent, denying the antecedent, denying the consequent. For a conjunctive conditional (muttasila), only two are productive: affirming the antecedent yields the consequent, and denying the consequent yields the denial of the antecedent. The other two are not productive because the consequent may be more general than the antecedent, as in 'if it is a human, it is an animal' — affirming 'it is an animal' does not imply 'it is a human', and denying 'it is a human' does not imply 'it is not an animal'. The author then notes that the conjunctive in this chapter is the implicative (luzūmiyya), not merely accidental. For disjunctive conditionals (munfaṣila), the productive moods depend on the type: 'preventive of conjunction' (māni'at al-jam') yields only from affirming one part the denial of the other; 'preventive of void' (māni'at al-khuluw) yields only from denying one part the affirmation of the other; the 'real' (ḥaqīqiyya), which prevents both, yields all four combinations. Examples are given for each: for conjunctive, a standard implication from 'human' to 'animal'; for real disjunctive, the even-odd example showing all four possibilities; for preventive of conjunction, 'tree or stone' where affirming one gives denial of the other; for preventive of void, a double negation example. This passage is a detailed commentary on the matn, explaining the conditions under which each type of conditional syllogism produces a valid conclusion.

Productive Moods in Conjunctive Conditionals
Author explains the two productive moods for conjunctive conditionals and why the others are invalid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which two of the four possible inference patterns produce valid conclusions in an implicative conjunctive conditional, and explain why the other two fail.

Core Idea
In a conjunctive conditional, only affirming the antecedent and denying the consequent are valid; the other two are fallacies.

The conjunctive conditional (al-muttaṣila al-luzūmiyya) expresses a necessary implication. The author explains that from the four logical possibilities, only two produce valid conclusions: affirming the antecedent gives the consequent, and denying the consequent gives the denial of the antecedent. The other two are invalid because the consequent may be more general.

Guided Path
1
Write the conditional form
Start with 'If A then B' where A implies B necessarily. This is an implicative conjunctive (al-muttaṣila al-luzūmiyya).
The author specifies this chapter deals only with implicative conditionals, not accidental conjunctions.
2
List the four possible operations
The four operations are: affirm A, deny A, affirm B, deny B. Each corresponds to a categorical premise.
3
Test each operation for validity
Check if the conclusion follows necessarily. Affirming A yields B (modus ponens). Denying B yields not A (modus tollens). Affirming B does not yield A (affirming the consequent fallacy). Denying A does not yield not B (denying the antecedent fallacy).
The author explains the reason: the consequent may be more general, so its realization does not necessitate the antecedent, nor does the denial of the antecedent necessitate the denial of the consequent.
4
Identify the two valid moods
Only two moods are productive: affirming the antecedent (modus ponens) and denying the consequent (modus tollens).
These correspond to the phrase 'وضع المقدم ورفع التالي' in the source.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Given the Islamic ruling: 'If a liquid is khamr (intoxicating), then it is impure (najis).' Write one valid inference using modus ponens and one using modus tollens, each with an appropriate categorical premise.
?
Explain why the argument 'It is impure, therefore it is khamr' is invalid, referencing the author's explanation about the generality of the consequent.
